Rod Inspection & Maintenance

Inspect Every Trip:

  • Thread condition (clean, no damage)
  • Rod straightness (look down string)
  • Tube condition (no cracks, dents)
  • Pin and box threads (no cross-threading)
  • Elevator marks (excessive = retire rod)

Thread Maintenance:

  1. Clean threads thoroughly every trip
  2. Inspect for damage/wear
  3. Apply proper thread compound
  4. Torque to specification
  5. Never use damaged threads

When to Retire a Rod:

  • Bent beyond acceptable tolerance
  • Cracks anywhere on tube
  • Thread damage (stripped, cross-threaded)
  • Excessive elevator marks (>50% circumference)
  • Severe corrosion or erosion
  • Any doubt about safety = retire it

‚úÇÔ∏è Cutting Rods in the Hole

üî™ Wire-Line Knife Operation Guide

When rods are stuck or broken in the hole, a wire-line knife tool is used to cut the inner tube assembly, allowing you to retrieve everything above the knife and abandon what's below.

‚ö†Ô∏è CRITICAL SAFETY: Cutting rods is a last resort operation after all fishing attempts have failed. This permanently abandons equipment downhole and may end the hole.

When to Use a Knife:

  • Stuck inner tube - Cannot retrieve after multiple fishing attempts
  • Broken wireline - Line parted and inner tube is stuck downhole
  • Collapsed hole - Formation closed around inner tube, cannot pull free
  • Core barrel jam - Core blockage cannot be cleared, tube won't budge
  • Economic decision - Cost of continued fishing exceeds value of equipment/hole

üìè Knife Tool Specifications by Rod Size

Rod Size Rod OD Inner Tube OD Knife Length Weight (approx)
BQ 60mm (2.36") ~36.5mm 600-900mm 8-12 kg
NQ 76mm (3.0") ~47.6mm 900-1200mm 12-18 kg
HQ 96mm (3.78") ~63.5mm 1200-1500mm 18-25 kg
PQ 123mm (4.85") ~85mm 1500-1800mm 25-35 kg

Important: Always verify knife size matches your rod size. Using wrong size knife will not cut properly and may get stuck itself.

üíß Water Volume Requirements

Proper water circulation is critical for knife cutting operations - keeps cuttings clear and lubricates the cutting blades.

Recommended Flow Rates:

Rod Size Minimum Flow Rate Optimal Flow Rate Pressure Range
BQ 15 LPM (4 GPM) 20-25 LPM (5-7 GPM) 200-400 PSI
NQ 20 LPM (5 GPM) 30-40 LPM (8-10 GPM) 250-500 PSI
HQ 30 LPM (8 GPM) 40-50 LPM (10-13 GPM) 300-600 PSI
PQ 40 LPM (10 GPM) 50-60 LPM (13-16 GPM) 350-700 PSI

Water Quality:

  • Clean water preferred - Avoid mud or additives that could jam knife mechanism
  • Temperature: Use ambient temperature water, avoid extremely cold water
  • Monitor returns: Watch for metal filings coming back - indicates knife is cutting

üìê Calculating Proper Depth for Knife

Critical calculation - cut too high and you waste good rods, cut too low and knife might not reach or could get stuck.

Step-by-Step Depth Calculation:

  1. Determine stuck point depth
    • Note the wireline depth when inner tube got stuck
    • Example: Stuck at 450 meters wireline depth
  2. Account for wireline stretch
    • Add 1-2% for wireline stretch under tension
    • Example: 450m √ó 1.5% = 6.75m, so actual depth ‚âà 456-457m
  3. Add clearance above stuck point
    • Cut 3-5 meters ABOVE stuck point to ensure clean cut
    • Example: 457m - 4m clearance = 453m target depth
  4. Count rods to target depth
    • Standard rod length = 3 meters (10 feet)
    • Example: 453m √∑ 3m = 151 rods
    • Mark the 151st rod from bottom - this is your cut point

‚ö†Ô∏è Double Check: Have two people independently count the rods. Mark the target rod clearly with paint or tape. Cutting at wrong depth wastes time and equipment.

Rod Counting Best Practices:

  • Lay rods out in groups of 10 for easy counting
  • Use tally counter or mark every 10th rod
  • Account for non-standard length rods (some may be 1.5m or 10ft)
  • Write count on rod with chalk/paint marker
  • Verify count before running in hole
  • Keep running tally as you lower string

‚öôÔ∏è Rotation & Torque Settings

Initial Lowering Phase:

Parameter Setting Why
Rotation 60-80 RPM Moderate speed helps knife follow existing path without binding
Pulldown Light (200-500 lbs) Just enough to keep knife descending, not forcing
Water Flow Start at minimum, increase gradually Avoid shocking stuck tube with sudden pressure

Cutting Phase Settings:

Rod Size RPM Range Cutting Pressure Typical Cut Time
BQ 80-120 RPM 500-800 lbs 15-30 minutes
NQ 60-100 RPM 800-1200 lbs 20-40 minutes
HQ 50-80 RPM 1200-1800 lbs 30-60 minutes
PQ 40-70 RPM 1800-2500 lbs 45-90 minutes

What to Watch For During Cutting:

  • Metal filings in returns - Good sign, knife is cutting
  • Torque increase - Normal as knife engages tube
  • Gradual weight loss - Rod weight decreases as cut progresses
  • Sudden free fall - Cut complete, tube separated
  • Temperature rise - Keep water flowing to cool blades

‚ö†Ô∏è STOP IMMEDIATELY IF:

  • Torque spikes suddenly (knife jamming)
  • Weight increases (knife getting stuck)
  • No metal returns after 15 minutes (knife not cutting)
  • Unusual vibration or noise

üîß Bench Testing the Knife Before Use

MANDATORY: Always bench test knife on surface before lowering it downhole. This could save your entire operation.

Pre-Trip Bench Test Procedure:

  1. Visual Inspection
    • Check all knife blades for damage, cracks, or dull edges
    • Inspect body for cracks or deformation
    • Verify size stamped on knife matches your rod size
    • Check thread condition (clean, no damage)
    • Inspect activation mechanism (collet, cam, or hydraulic)
  2. Mechanical Function Test
    • Mount knife on test stand or in drill head
    • Attach to old/scrap inner tube of correct size
    • Apply rotation at expected RPM (60-100 RPM)
    • Gradually apply downward pressure
    • Verify blades expand and engage tube
    • Confirm knife cuts cleanly through test tube
  3. Blade Engagement Check
    • Measure blade protrusion (should be even all around)
    • Check blade spring tension (equal on all blades)
    • Verify activation depth/trigger mechanism works
    • Test retraction - blades should fully retract when pulled up
  4. Water Flow Test
    • Connect to water swivel
    • Run water through knife at operating pressure
    • Check for leaks around seals
    • Verify waterways are clear (watch for blockages)
    • Confirm pressure rating matches your system

Test Cut Result: Knife should produce clean, complete cut through test tube in 5-10 minutes. If it takes longer or cut is incomplete, knife needs service or replacement.

Failed Bench Test - Do Not Use If:

Problem Risk Action Required
Blades damaged/dull Won't cut, will get stuck Replace blades or entire knife
Uneven blade protrusion Incomplete cut, knife jam Service knife, adjust blade springs
Activation mechanism stiff May not engage properly downhole Lubricate, repair, or replace
Water leaks Loss of circulation, poor cutting Replace seals, test again
Body cracks/damage Catastrophic failure downhole Do not use - replace knife

‚ö†Ô∏è Safety Procedures

Before Lowering Knife:

  • Safety meeting - Brief entire crew on knife operation plan
  • Clear work area - Keep non-essential personnel away from rig floor
  • Check hoisting equipment - Verify winch, cable, and connections
  • Prepare retrieval tools - Have backup fishing tools ready
  • Document everything - Record depths, rod counts, times
  • Communication plan - Establish clear signals between driller and helpers

During Operation:

  • One person directs - Driller calls all shots, crew follows orders
  • Hands clear - Keep hands away from rotating rods
  • Eye protection - Wear safety glasses (metal filings in returns)
  • Monitor continuously - Watch weight, torque, returns, RPM
  • Slow and steady - Don't rush the cut, patience is critical
  • No distractions - Full attention on operation until complete

After Cutting:

  • Stop rotation gradually - Don't jam brakes suddenly
  • Pull knife slowly - Watch for obstructions coming up
  • Inspect knife - Check blade condition when back on surface
  • Retrieve wireline - May still have inner tube attached above cut
  • Document results - Record what was cut, what was lost, lessons learned

üö® EMERGENCY STOP CONDITIONS:

  • Knife gets stuck and won't move up or down
  • Excessive vibration or unusual noises
  • Rods start jumping or chattering
  • Water pressure spikes suddenly
  • Anyone in danger on rig floor

If knife gets stuck: DO NOT FORCE IT. You now have two stuck tools. Stop, assess, call for experienced help.

üí° Pro Tips from Experienced Drillers

Before You Cut:

  • Try one more time - Make one final fishing attempt before cutting. Once you cut, there's no going back.
  • Check rod connections - Verify all rods above cut point are tight. Loose connection during pull = disaster.
  • Photograph setup - Take photos of knife, test cut, depth marks for documentation.
  • Have Plan B ready - If knife fails, what's next? Another knife? Cement and abandon?
  • Consult experienced driller - If it's your first knife job, get someone experienced on site.

During Cutting:

  • Feel the drill - Experienced drillers can feel when knife engages and when cut completes through drill feedback.
  • Keep water clean - If returns get muddy, reduce flow slightly - don't wash cuttings back into knife.
  • Mark 30-minute intervals - If no progress after 45-60 minutes, something's wrong.
  • Don't over-torque - Let the blades do the work. Excessive force dulls blades and jams knife.
  • Watch weight indicator closely - The weight drop when cut completes can be subtle - don't miss it.

Lessons from Failed Knife Jobs:

What Went Wrong Why It Happened How to Avoid
Knife got stuck below target Cut too deep, knife entered bad ground Add 3-5m clearance above stuck point
Cut incomplete, tube still attached Dull blades or wrong knife size Always bench test, verify size match
Knife wouldn't engage Activation mechanism failed Test activation multiple times on surface
Rods above cut point came unscrewed Loose connections during pull Check ALL connections before cutting
Lost knife downhole Knife body failed under stress Inspect for cracks, don't use damaged knife

After Successful Cut:

  • Inspect retrieved sections - Look at cut surface quality, tells you how well knife worked.
  • Clean and service knife immediately - Don't store dirty knife, it may be needed again soon.
  • Replace worn blades - If blades show significant wear, replace before next use.
  • Document in log - Record: depth, rod count, time to cut, settings used, results.
  • Debrief with crew - What went well? What would you do differently? Share knowledge.

‚úì Success Indicator: You know you've done a good knife job when: (1) Cut completed in expected time, (2) Clean separation confirmed, (3) Knife retrieved in good condition, (4) No damage to remaining rod string. If all four check out, job well done.

üìã Quick Reference Checklist

‚òê Task Details
‚òê Calculate depth Stuck point + stretch - clearance = cut depth
‚òê Count rods Two people count independently, mark target rod
‚òê Select correct knife Match rod size: BQ/NQ/HQ/PQ
‚òê Bench test knife Cut test tube, verify blades, check water flow
‚òê Safety briefing Entire crew understands plan and risks
‚òê Check all rod connections Tight threads above and below cut point
‚òê Lower knife to depth 60-80 RPM, light weight, moderate water
‚òê Begin cutting Increase RPM and pressure per table above
‚òê Monitor progress Metal filings, weight loss, torque, time
‚òê Confirm cut complete Sudden weight drop, free rotation
‚òê Retrieve knife Slow pull, watch for hangs
‚òê Inspect & document Check knife condition, log results

‚öñÔ∏è Rod Weight & Water Management

Why Rod Weight Matters

Knowing your rod weight is critical for:

  • Calculating hydraulic pull force requirements
  • Preventing stuck pipe situations
  • Planning trips and equipment capacity
  • Calculating water volume needed for circulation
  • Understanding weight on bit (WOB)

Rod Weight Reference Chart

Weight per 3-meter (10-foot) rod section, including threads and couplings:

Rod Series Weight per 3m Rod (kg) Weight per 10ft Rod (lbs) Weight per 100m (kg) Weight per 1000ft (lbs)
BQ 18 kg 39.7 lbs 600 kg 3,970 lbs
NQ 24 kg 52.9 lbs 800 kg 5,290 lbs
HQ 35 kg 77.2 lbs 1,167 kg 7,720 lbs
PQ 45 kg 99.2 lbs 1,500 kg 9,920 lbs

Note: Weights include standard drill rod with threads. Add weight of drill bit, core barrel assembly, and any stabilizers/BHA components to total.

How to Calculate Total Rod Weight in Hole

Step-by-Step Calculation:

Step 1: Determine Your Rod Count

Count the number of rods you've added to the hole.

Example: 50 rods at 3 meters each = 150 meters depth

Step 2: Calculate Rod Weight

Formula:

Total Rod Weight = Number of Rods √ó Weight per Rod

Example (NQ):

  • 50 rods √ó 24 kg/rod = 1,200 kg
  • Or: 50 rods √ó 52.9 lbs/rod = 2,645 lbs

Step 3: Add BHA Components

Add weight of:

  • Drill bit: ~5-15 kg (10-30 lbs) depending on size
  • Core barrel: ~20-50 kg (45-110 lbs)
  • Stabilizers: ~10-25 kg each (20-55 lbs)
  • Any other downhole tools

Example Total: 1,800 kg (rods) + 30 kg (bit + barrel) = 1,830 kg

Step 4: Consider Water Buoyancy

Rods submerged in drilling fluid are lighter due to buoyancy.

Buoyancy Factor: ~0.85 (rods weigh 85% in water)

Effective Weight = Dry Weight √ó 0.85

Example: 1,830 kg √ó 0.85 = 1,555 kg effective weight

Using Hydraulic Gauge to Measure Rod Weight

Why This Matters

Your hydraulic pressure gauge can tell you the ACTUAL weight of your rod string, accounting for buoyancy, hole conditions, and drag.

Procedure:

  1. Establish Zero Reference
    • With rods off bottom and hanging free
    • Note the hydraulic pressure reading
    • This is your baseline "hanging weight"
  2. Read Pressure in PSI or BAR
    • Most rigs show hydraulic pressure
    • Check your machine's conversion factor
    • Typical: 1000 PSI = ~450 kg pull force
  3. Calculate Actual Weight
    • Use machine's specific conversion chart
    • Usually in operator's manual
    • Example: 2000 PSI = 900 kg string weight
  4. Compare to Calculated Weight
    • If hydraulic reading HIGHER than calculated ‚Üí Drag or sticking
    • If LOWER than calculated ‚Üí Check for issues
    • Should be within 10-15% normally

Common Hydraulic Conversions (Approximate):

  • 500 PSI ‚âà 225 kg (500 lbs)
  • 1000 PSI ‚âà 450 kg (1,000 lbs)
  • 2000 PSI ‚âà 900 kg (2,000 lbs)
  • 3000 PSI ‚âà 1,350 kg (3,000 lbs)

‚ö†Ô∏è Check your machine's manual for exact conversion!

Water Volume Requirements

Why Water Volume Matters:

  • Adequate flow removes cuttings efficiently
  • Insufficient water = bit loading, slow penetration
  • Too much pressure = lost circulation

Water Requirements by Rod Size:

Rod Size Minimum Flow Rate Typical Flow Rate Optimal Flow Rate
AQ 40 L/min (10 gpm) 60 L/min (15 gpm) 80 L/min (20 gpm)
BQ 60 L/min (15 gpm) 80 L/min (20 gpm) 100 L/min (25 gpm)
NQ 80 L/min (20 gpm) 110 L/min (30 gpm) 150 L/min (40 gpm)
HQ 110 L/min (30 gpm) 150 L/min (40 gpm) 190 L/min (50 gpm)
PQ 150 L/min (40 gpm) 190 L/min (50 gpm) 230 L/min (60 gpm)

Water Requirements by Formation Type:

Formation Type Adjustment Factor Why
Soft sedimentary +20% flow More cuttings volume to remove
Medium metamorphic Standard flow Normal cuttings production
Hard igneous -10% flow OK Less cuttings, but need cooling
Fractured/broken +30% flow High cuttings, potential loss zones
Clay-rich +25% flow Sticky cuttings need flushing

How to Calculate Water Needs for Your Hole

Method 1: Calculate Annular Volume

Formula (Metric):

Volume (Liters) = œÄ √ó (Hole Diameter¬≤ - Rod OD¬≤) √∑ 4 √ó Depth √ó 1000

Example (NQ, 100m depth):

  • Hole diameter: 76mm (0.076m)
  • Rod OD: 60mm (0.060m)
  • Volume = 3.14 √ó (0.076¬≤ - 0.060¬≤) √∑ 4 √ó 100 √ó 1000
  • Volume = 3.14 √ó (0.00578 - 0.0036) √∑ 4 √ó 100 √ó 1000
  • Volume = 171 Liters

This is the volume to FILL the annulus once.

Method 2: Simple Reference Table

Annular volume per 10 meters (33 feet) of depth:

Rod Size Liters per 10m Gallons per 100ft
AQ 11 L 7 gal
BQ 14 L 9 gal
NQ 17 L 11 gal
HQ 22 L 14 gal
PQ 28 L 18 gal

Quick Calculation:

Multiply depth (in 10m increments) by table value

Example: NQ at 150m = 15 √ó 17L = 255 Liters total annular volume

Calculate Water Supply Needed

For continuous drilling, you need:

  1. Active circulation volume: 2√ó annular volume minimum
    • Allows for settling pit/return system
    • Example: 255L √ó 2 = 510L active
  2. Reserve volume: 1√ó annular volume
    • For lost circulation events
    • For cleaning hole
    • Example: 255L reserve
  3. Total water needed: 3√ó annular volume
    • Example: 255L √ó 3 = 765 Liters total

Quick Rule of Thumb:

Have 3 times your annular volume available at all times.

This covers normal operation, cleaning, and emergencies.

Water Supply Planning by Depth

Recommended Water Supply (NQ Standard):

Hole Depth Annular Volume Recommended Supply Minimum Supply
50m (165 ft) 85 L (22 gal) 250 L (65 gal) 170 L (45 gal)
100m (330 ft) 170 L (45 gal) 500 L (130 gal) 340 L (90 gal)
200m (660 ft) 340 L (90 gal) 1,000 L (260 gal) 680 L (180 gal)
300m (985 ft) 510 L (135 gal) 1,500 L (400 gal) 1,020 L (270 gal)
500m (1640 ft) 850 L (225 gal) 2,500 L (660 gal) 1,700 L (450 gal)

Water Logistics Considerations:

  • Remote locations: Plan for 4-5√ó annular volume (no easy resupply)
  • Fractured rock: Add 50-100% more for potential loss zones
  • Dry/desert areas: Critical - plan carefully, have backup sources
  • Winter conditions: Prevent freezing - heated storage or antifreeze

Monitoring Rod Weight During Operations

What to Watch:

  • Record hydraulic pressure every 10 meters (30 feet)
  • Compare to calculated weight - should match within 15%
  • Sudden increase = drag, sticking, or blockage
  • Gradual increase = normal (more rods added)
  • Decrease = potential problem (broken rod, lost bit)

Troubleshooting Weight Issues:

Symptom Possible Cause Action
Weight 30%+ higher than calculated Hole drag, sticking Work string, improve fluid
Weight suddenly doubles Severe sticking STOP - see Stuck Pipe section
Weight gradually increasing Poor hole cleaning Increase flow, circulate clean
Weight suddenly drops Rod or bit failure Pull string, inspect
Weight fluctuates wildly Hydraulic system issue Check hydraulics, gauges

Rod Weight Quick Reference Cards

NQ Standard (Most Common)

Per Rod: 36 kg (79 lbs)

Per 100m: 1,200 kg (2,640 lbs)

Water per 100m: 170 L (45 gal) annular volume

Recommended supply: 500 L (130 gal) for 100m hole

HQ (Larger Diameter)

Per Rod: 56 kg (123 lbs)

Per 100m: 1,867 kg (4,110 lbs)

Water per 100m: 220 L (58 gal) annular volume

Recommended supply: 660 L (175 gal) for 100m hole

üï≥ Casing & Collaring Procedures

Why Proper Collaring Matters

The first 3-10 meters are the most üï≥critical part of your hole.

  • Poor collar = crooked hole
  • Unstable collar = hole collapse and stuck pipe
  • No casing in weak ground = constant problems
  • Proper setup = smooth drilling for entire hole

Taking time to collar properly saves hours of problems later.

When to Use Casing

ALWAYS Use Casing When:

  • Overburden present (soil, sand, gravel, clay)
  • Unconsolidated material in first 3+ meters
  • Fractured or broken rock near surface
  • Water-bearing zones near surface
  • Collapsing or caving ground
  • When drilling through ice (see Ice Drilling section)
  • Any time hole stability is questionable

May Not Need Casing When:

  • Solid competent bedrock at surface
  • No overburden (rare)
  • Very short holes in stable ground
  • Previous hole showed excellent stability

When in doubt, USE CASING. It's easier to install casing than to fix a collapsed hole.

üîß What Are Casing Shoes & Rod Shoes?

Casing shoes are threaded to the bottom of casing tubes to advance them through overburden (loose soil, clay, gravel, boulders) into bedrock. They protect the casing and create a tight seal for drilling fluid return.

Key Differences:

Tool Inside Diameter Purpose Core Barrel Passage
Casing Shoe Flush with casing ID Advance casing through overburden ‚úì Yes - allows free passage
Casing Bit Smaller than casing ID Deep/difficult overburden or reaming over stuck rods ‚úó No - ID too small
Rod Shoe Flush with rod ID Advance drill rods (same concept as casing shoe) ‚úì Yes - for rod applications
Crown Shoe Flush with casing ID High-performance version with taller crown ‚úì Yes - premium performance

Main Functions:

  • Protect casing ends from distortion
  • Help casing pass through overburden to bedrock
  • Carry drilling fluids and cuttings to surface
  • Assist casing advancement when drill rod is in hole

üíé Types of Casing Shoe Cutting Media

Type Best For Advantages Cost
Tungsten Carbide (TC) Soil, clay, soft materials Most economical, durable in soft ground $
Impregnated Diamond Medium to hard overburden Widest range of applications, most popular $$
Surface-Set Diamond Soft to medium formations Aggressive cutting, faster penetration $$
Electroplated Diamond Specialized applications Single layer of diamonds, specific uses $$$

Quick Selection Guide: Use Tungsten Carbide for soft overburden (cheapest option). Use Impregnated Diamond for everything else - it's the industry standard with the widest application range.

ü¶é Fordia Gator Casing Shoes

Gator line offers excellent quality/price ratio with four performance levels for any overburden condition.

Performance Comparison

Model Diamond Impreg. Height Waterways Best For When to Use
Gator Standard 5mm 6 Easy overburden (sand, gravel) Bedrock not far from surface
Gator Super 6mm 8 Moderate overburden (gravel, clay, rocks) Deeper bedrock, better cutting needed
Gator High-Performance 5mm or 6mm Varies Very difficult overburden or deep bedrock Choice of matrices for hard ground
Gator Crown Shoe 8mm Similar to core bit Extremely difficult conditions, large boulders Highest performance, deeper depths

Crown Profile Options:

  • Flat Profile: Standard design for most applications
  • V-Ring Profile: Better for broken or fractured formations
  • Crown Shape (Crown Shoe): Similar to core bit crown - optimal cutting properties

Matrix Selection (High-Performance & Crown Shoes):

Matrix Hardness Ground Conditions Application
Hard Matrix Changing formations with high & low thrust Durable for variable conditions
Medium-Hard Matrix Moderately abrasive & fractured formations Balance of life and penetration
Soft Matrix Very hard, compact overburden Faster diamond exposure for cutting

Fordia Advantage: Gator Crown Shoes use the same matrix options as core bits, giving you premium performance in extremely difficult overburden with large boulders. The 8mm diamond impregnation is 60% taller than standard shoes.

üíé Boart Longyear CSUMX‚Ñ¢ Casing Shoes

Revolutionary patented technology delivers superior performance in all overburden conditions.

Patented Technologies:

Feature Technology Benefit
UMX‚Ñ¢ Crown Patented crown design Penetrates through changing ground conditions
Razorcut‚Ñ¢ Face Raised diamond protrusions Cuts soft ground right "out of the box" - no break-in needed
Twin-Taper‚Ñ¢ Waterways Dual-angle flushing channels Dramatically improves flushing, clears bit face, reinforces ID

Performance Types:

Type Crown Height Best For
Standard Casing Shoe Standard height Average overburden conditions
Heavy Duty Casing Shoe 6mm crown height Deep or difficult overburden, oversize OD available

Key Innovation: Unlike traditional V-groove designs that need exposed diamonds to cut, Razorcut‚Ñ¢ raised protrusions cut immediately - even in the softest ground. This keeps the shoe tracking straight and exposing diamonds naturally.

Razorcut‚Ñ¢ vs Traditional V-Groove:

  • Traditional V-Groove: Needs diamonds to be exposed before cutting begins - slow start
  • Razorcut‚Ñ¢: Raised profile cuts immediately, helps expose diamonds, tracks straight

üìè Standard Casing Shoe Sizes

Available in all DCDMA standard sizes.

Size Outside Diameter Inside Diameter Common Use
AW / BW 1.89" / 48mm Varies by series Small diameter applications

Note: Casing shoes are also available for Q-series wireline systems (NQ, HQ, PQ). For Q-series, standard casing shoes are used with drill rods since it's a wireline system.

üéØ Casing Shoe Selection Guide

Selection Decision Tree:

Ground Condition Depth to Bedrock Recommended Shoe Cutting Media
Soil, clay, soft sand Shallow Standard - Tungsten Carbide TC (most economical)
Sand, gravel, easy overburden Shallow to medium Gator Standard / BL Standard 5mm impregnated diamond
Gravel, clay, some rocks Medium to deep Gator Super / BL Heavy Duty 6mm impregnated diamond
Very difficult, rocky overburden Deep Gator High-Performance 5-6mm impreg, hard matrix
Extremely difficult, large boulders Very deep Gator Crown Shoe 8mm impreg, core bit matrix
Broken, abrasive, fractured Any depth High-Performance with hard matrix Impregnated diamond
Loose conglomerates Variable Medium-hard matrix shoe Impregnated diamond

Cost vs Performance Considerations:

  • Disposable Approach: Sometimes casing is left in hole when bedrock is reached - choose economical Standard or Super shoes
  • Performance Approach: In really tough overburden, choose High-Performance or Crown shoes to maintain good penetration rate and avoid premature wear
  • Depth Factor: Shallow holes = Standard shoes work fine (easy to replace if needed). Deep holes = invest in better shoes (rod trips are expensive)

üîç Casing Shoe Maintenance & Inspection

Before Each Run:

  • Clean threads thoroughly - no debris or old compound
  • Inspect waterways - clear and unobstructed
  • Check gauge diameter - not worn undersize
  • Examine diamond crown - even wear pattern
  • Verify thread condition - no damage or cross-threading
  • Apply proper thread compound

Crown Wear Patterns:

Wear Pattern Cause Action
Even diamond exposure across face Normal wear ‚úì Continue use until matrix worn down
Heavy center wear Excessive weight on shoe Reduce WOB (Weight On Bit)
Gauge worn undersize Abrasive formation Replace shoe, consider harder matrix
Waterways eroded/enlarged Excessive pump pressure Replace shoe, reduce pressure
Matrix loaded with clay/mud Poor flushing Clean shoe, improve fluid properties
Polished diamonds (not cutting) Insufficient WOB or wrong matrix Increase parameters or change matrix

When to Replace:

  • Matrix worn down to 2mm or less remaining
  • Gauge diameter worn undersize (won't maintain hole size)
  • Waterways eroded or damaged
  • Thread damage prevents proper make-up
  • Penetration rate drops significantly despite good drilling practices

Pro Tip: In changing formations with alternating soft and hard zones, a harder matrix will last longer but may drill slower in soft zones. Balance shoe life against penetration rate based on your specific conditions.

‚öôÔ∏è Special Applications

Casing Advancer Shoes:

Oversized OD casing shoes designed to allow roller cone bits to pass through the ID while providing clearance for extra cuttings. Used in specific overburden drilling with DTH (Down-The-Hole) hammers.

Reaming Casing Downward:

Casing shoes can be used to ream the casing downward when advancing a casing string in an existing hole with the rod string still in place.

Custom Applications:

  • Special diameters available (HQ to PQ equivalents)
  • Custom thread types for non-standard casing
  • Gauge protection with TSP diamond elements and TC pads
  • Multi-waterway designs (4, 6, 8+ waterways)
  • Special crown profiles for unique conditions

Types of Casing

Casing Type Material Best For Advantages Disadvantages
Steel Casing Steel pipe Permanent installations, hard drilling Very strong, reusable, durable Heavy, expensive, can rust
PVC Casing Schedule 40/80 PVC Soft formations, temporary holes Lightweight, cheap, won't rust Can crack, not for hard drilling
HWT Casing Heavy wall tube Through overburden to bedrock Purpose-built, strong, removable Expensive, special tools needed
Split Tube Split PVC or steel Easy removal, temporary support Can be removed easily Not as strong, limited depth

Casing Size Selection:

Rod Size Hole Diameter Casing ID Needed Common Casing Size
AQ 48mm (1.9") 50mm+ (2"+) 2" PVC or 2.5" steel
BQ 60mm (2.4") 65mm+ (2.5"+) 3" PVC or 3" steel
NQ 76mm (3") 80mm+ (3.2"+) 4" PVC or 4" steel
HQ 96mm (3.8") 100mm+ (4"+) 5" PVC or 5" steel
PQ 123mm (4.8") 127mm+ (5"+) 6" PVC or 6" steel

Rule of Thumb: Casing ID should be at least 1 size larger than rod OD to allow for misalignment and easy passage.

Collaring Methods

Method 1: Drive Casing (Softer Ground)

Best for: Clay, sand, soft soil, loose gravel

Procedure:

  1. Prepare casing:
    • Cut to length needed (typically 3-6 meters)
    • For steel: weld drive shoe (cutting edge) on bottom
    • For PVC: attach drive point or shoe
    • Install casing hanger/clamp at top
  2. Position casing:
    • Set exactly where hole should be
    • Use level to ensure perfectly vertical
    • Brace with wood or stakes if needed
  3. Drive casing down:
    • Method A: Rotary drive with drill rig
    • Method B: Manual driving with sledge hammer and drive cap
    • Method C: Use casing hammer attachment
    • Check vertical alignment frequently
    • Drive until refusal or desired depth
  4. Clean out inside:
    • Use bailer, auger, or drill to remove material inside
    • May need to drive more as material is removed
    • Continue until casing seated in firm material

Method 2: Drill and Set Casing (Most Common)

Best for: Mixed ground, overburden over bedrock, most conditions

Procedure:

  1. Drill pilot hole:
    • Use bit slightly SMALLER than casing ID
    • For NQ: Use HQ bit (96mm) to set 4" (100mm) casing
    • Drill to depth needed for casing
    • Keep hole as straight as possible
    • Typical depth: To bedrock + 0.5m into rock
  2. Clean hole thoroughly:
    • Pull bit, circulate clean
    • Remove all loose material
    • Check hole depth
  3. Lower casing into hole:
    • Guide carefully to stay centered
    • May need to rotate while lowering
    • Watch for hang-ups or binding
    • Seat firmly at bottom
  4. Secure casing:
    • Cement or grout around outside (recommended)
    • Or pack with bentonite chips/pellets
    • Fill annular space completely
    • Allow to set (cement: 24 hours minimum)
  5. Trim and finish:
    • Cut casing to proper height (15-30cm above ground)
    • Install casing cap or protector
    • Mark with high-visibility paint or flag

Method 3: ODEX/Eccentric (Hard Ground)

Best for: Cobbles, boulders, cemented gravel, hard overburden

Requires: Special ODEX bit and casing system (specialist equipment)

How it works:

  • Eccentric reaming bit cuts oversize hole
  • Casing follows directly behind bit
  • Can drill and case simultaneously
  • Effective in very difficult ground
  • Consult specialist or manufacturer for procedures

Drilling the Collar - Step by Step

Goal: Start the Hole Straight and True

First 3 meters determine the rest of the hole. A crooked start = crooked hole.

Pre-Collar Setup:

Step 1: Position Drill Rig

  • Level drill as much as possible
  • Use hydraulic jacks or blocking to level
  • Check with spirit level in both directions
  • Stable footing - won't shift during drilling
  • Clear area of obstacles
  • Have water supply connected and ready

Step 2: Set Up Alignment

  • Use plumb bob or laser to mark exact hole location
  • Set angle indicator if drilling at angle
  • Check mast vertical alignment
  • Have helper sight down mast for straightness
  • Make final adjustments before starting

Step 3: Prepare First Rod

  • Use SHORTEST rod available for collaring (1.5m ideal)
  • Inspect bit - must be sharp and true
  • Apply thread compound
  • Torque to specification
  • Double-check bit is tight

Why short rod? Easier to control alignment, less wobble, better visibility

Starting the Collar:

Step 4: Initial Penetration (Critical First 30cm)

Settings for Collaring (NQ Example):

Parameter Setting Why
RPM 400-600 RPM Slow = More control, less wandering
WOB Light (30-40%) Let bit find its path, don't force
Water Flow Medium (70-80%) Cool bit, clear cuttings
Feed Rate SLOW Controlled start

Procedure:

  1. Position bit exactly on mark
  2. Start rotation at LOW speed (400 RPM)
  3. Start water flow
  4. Apply light pressure - let bit cut naturally
  5. Watch alignment constantly
  6. First 30cm is CRITICAL - go slow
  7. Have helper check alignment from two directions
  8. If starting to go crooked - STOP, evaluate, may need to start over

Step 5: Collar Depth (30cm to 3 meters)

Once bit is established (after 30cm):

  • Can gradually increase RPM to 600-800
  • Can increase WOB slightly (50-60%)
  • Still watch alignment closely
  • Check every 0.5 meter

Through Overburden:

  • May encounter soft/hard/soft layers
  • Adjust parameters for each layer
  • In soft: reduce WOB, maintain RPM
  • In hard: increase WOB, may reduce RPM
  • Keep hole straight - don't let bit wander

Through Casing:

  • Reduce RPM when entering casing (400-500)
  • Light WOB - don't damage casing
  • Keep centered - avoid hitting casing walls
  • Once through casing, back to normal parameters

Step 6: Into Bedrock (Critical Transition)

When you hit bedrock:

  • You'll feel it - sudden change in drilling feel
  • Penetration rate drops significantly
  • May hear change in sound
  • Core sample will show rock

Adjust parameters for rock:

  • Increase RPM to rock speed (800-1200)
  • Adjust WOB for formation hardness
  • May need more water pressure
  • Drill 0.5-1.0m into solid rock

Why drill into rock?

  • Confirms you're in solid competent bedrock
  • Provides stable foundation for rest of hole
  • Casing should be set into rock minimum 0.5m

RPM Guide for Different Materials

Material RPM Range (NQ) WOB Notes
Starting Collar 400-600 Light Control and alignment critical
Soil/Clay 300-500 Light Don't overload, can ball up
Sand/Gravel 400-600 Light-Medium May collapse, use casing
Soft Rock 600-800 Medium Standard drilling
Medium Rock 800-1000 Medium-High Most common bedrock
Hard Rock 1000-1200 High Maximum speed for hardness
Very Hard/Abrasive 1200-1400 Medium-High High speed, but watch bit wear
Through Casing 400-500 Light Protect casing from damage
Broken/Fractured 600-800 Light-Medium Can grab and torque

General RPM Principles:

  • Softer material = SLOWER RPM (prevents balling, overloading)
  • Harder material = HIGHER RPM (diamond bits need speed to cut hard rock)
  • Starting/collaring = SLOWER RPM (control and alignment)
  • Smaller bits = HIGHER RPM (AQ can run faster than PQ)
  • Worn bits = HIGHER RPM (compensate for dull diamonds)

Drilling Fluids for Collaring

Through Overburden:

For Soil/Clay:

  • Clear water usually adequate
  • Add dispersant if clay is sticky
  • May need polymer to prevent balling
  • Low viscosity for easy cuttings removal

For Sand/Gravel:

  • Clear water with polymer
  • Helps stabilize loose material
  • Prevents hole collapse
  • Use casing as soon as possible

For Mixed Overburden:

  • Light bentonite mud (2-4%)
  • Provides wall support
  • Suspends cuttings
  • Typical mix: 2-4 kg bentonite per 100L water
  • Don't make too thick - hard to remove cuttings

Into Bedrock:

Standard Rock Drilling:

  • Clear water is standard
  • May add small amount bentonite (1-2%) if unstable zones
  • Foam additives for very broken rock
  • Keep simple unless problems arise

Fluid Additives for Collaring:

Additive Purpose When to Use Typical Amount
Bentonite Hole stabilization, viscosity Loose overburden, fractured rock 2-5 kg per 100L
Polymer Hole stability, clay inhibition Swelling clays, sand/gravel 0.2-0.5 kg per 100L
Dispersant Reduce viscosity, disperse clays Sticky clay formations 0.5-1 kg per 100L
Surfactant Reduce bit balling Sticky formations 0.1-0.3% solution
Foam Improve cutting removal Very broken/fractured rock Per manufacturer

Grouting/Cementing Casing

Why Grout Casing?

  • Seals annular space
  • Prevents surface water contamination downhole
  • Stabilizes casing
  • Required by regulation in many jurisdictions
  • Professional appearance

Grouting Procedure:

Step 1: Prepare Grout Mix

Cement Grout (Most Common):

  • Portland cement + water
  • Mix ratio: 5-6 L water per 25 kg bag cement
  • Consistency: Like thick milkshake
  • Can add 5-10% bentonite for better flow

Bentonite Seal (Alternative):

  • Bentonite chips or pellets
  • Hydrate when wet
  • Pour dry around casing
  • Add water to hydrate
  • Cheaper but less permanent than cement

Step 2: Place Grout

Method A: Tremie Pipe (Best):

  1. Lower small pipe to bottom of annular space
  2. Pour grout through pipe
  3. Raise pipe as space fills
  4. Ensures grout goes to bottom, no voids
  5. Keep pipe submerged in grout

Method B: Direct Pour (Acceptable):

  1. Pour grout directly into annular space
  2. Work from bottom up if possible
  3. May need to add in stages
  4. Tap casing to settle grout and release air
  5. Top up as grout settles

Step 3: Cure Time

  • Minimum 24 hours before drilling through casing
  • 48-72 hours for full strength
  • Keep moist during curing (cover with wet burlap)
  • Don't disturb while curing
  • Cold weather: May need 3-5 days

How Much Grout Needed:

Calculate Annular Volume:

Volume (L) = œÄ √ó (Hole Dia¬≤ - Casing OD¬≤) √∑ 4 √ó Depth √ó 1000

Quick Reference (per meter of depth):

Casing Size Hole Size Liters per Meter 25kg Bags per 3m
2" PVC 76mm (NQ) 3.5 L/m ~1 bag
4" Steel 96mm (HQ) 2.0 L/m ~0.5 bag
4" PVC 96mm (HQ) 3.0 L/m ~0.75 bag
6" Steel 123mm (PQ) 2.5 L/m ~0.6 bag

Add 20% extra to account for irregular hole, losses, and waste

Common Collaring Problems & Solutions

Problem Cause Prevention Fix
Hole starts crooked Poor alignment, uneven ground, bad bit Level rig, check alignment, use sharp bit Start over if >5¬∞ off in first meter
Casing won't go down Hole too small, misalignment, ledge Drill oversize, keep straight, clean hole Ream hole larger, check for obstructions
Hole collapses during casing Unstable ground, water inflow Use drilling fluid, work quickly Use drive casing or ODEX method
Can't drill through casing Casing offset, too small, damaged Use correct size, align properly Use smaller bit, ream casing if needed
Bit wanders in overburden Soft/hard layers, loose material Use casing, proper WOB/RPM Reduce parameters, use stabilizer
Grout won't set Too much water, contamination, cold Proper mix ratio, clean materials Wait longer, may need to re-do

Collaring Checklist

Before Starting:

  • Drill rig leveled and stable
  • Exact hole location marked
  • Alignment checked and verified
  • Sharp bit installed and torqued
  • Water supply connected and tested
  • Drilling fluid mixed if needed
  • Casing on site and inspected
  • Grout materials ready if needed
  • Safety equipment in place
  • Crew briefed on procedure

During Collaring:

  • Monitor alignment constantly
  • Adjust parameters for formation
  • Watch for signs of trouble
  • Keep good notes on what works
  • Don't rush - take time to do it right

After Collaring:

  • Verify hole is straight
  • Casing properly installed and grouted
  • Allow proper cure time before continuing
  • Document collar depth and conditions
  • Mark collar location clearly

ü™ù Backend Assembly & Core Barrel

Why Backend Assembly Matters

The backend (core barrel assembly) is the heart of your core recovery system.

  • Properly assembled backend = Good core recovery
  • Wrong reaming shell length = No core or stuck backend
  • Bad bearings = Lost cores and damaged equipment
  • Worn rubbers = Poor stabilization and vibration
  • Understanding your backend saves time, money, and frustration

Reaming Shell Length Chart

‚ö†Ô∏è CRITICAL: Wrong shell length = No core or stuck backend!

The reaming shell MUST match your tube extension length.

Too short = Backend won't latch, water flow problems | Too long = Backend stuck in hole

Standard Reaming Shell Lengths

Shell NameActual LengthUse WithNQ/BQ Size
6" Shell6" (152mm)Single tube, no extension6" (152mm)
10" Shell10" (254mm)1 x 4" extension10" (254mm)
18" Shell18" (457mm)2 x 4" extensions18" (457mm)
36" Shell36" (914mm)Multiple extensions36" (914mm)

Formula: Shell Length = Base (6") + (Extensions √ó 4")

Always round UP to next available shell size if between sizes

Industry Rules of Thumb - Critical Checks

‚úÖ "The Drop Test"

Drop backend 6" onto bench. Listen for rattles:

  • No rattle: ‚úÖ Good - everything tight
  • Slight rattle: ‚ö†Ô∏è Check bearings/rubbers
  • Loud rattle: ‚ùå Bad - worn bearings or loose parts

‚úÖ "The Spin Test"

Hold inner tube and spin reaming shell:

  • Smooth spin: ‚úÖ Good bearings
  • Rough/gritty: ‚ö†Ô∏è Bearings contaminated
  • Won't spin: ‚ùå Replace bearings immediately

‚úÖ "The Wobble Test"

Support one end, wiggle the other:

  • Minimal movement: ‚úÖ Good stabilizers
  • Significant wobble: ‚ö†Ô∏è Worn rubbers
  • Extreme play: ‚ùå Replace rubbers and bearings

Pre-Trip Checklist (2 Minutes)

  • Correct shell length for tube config
  • Shell extends 1/8"-1/4" beyond bit
  • Spins freely without binding
  • No rattles when dropped
  • Minimal wobble
  • Water flows freely
  • Latch engages and holds
  • Core lifter moves freely
  • All threads clean and torqued
  • Bit sharp and secure

Identifying Bad Components

üîß Bad Bearings

SymptomCauseAction
Rough rotationContaminated bearingsClean or replace
Binding/catchingDamaged bearing raceReplace immediately
Grinding noiseFailed bearingReplace immediately
Heat generationLack of lubricationReplace and lubricate
Excessive playWorn bearingReplace immediately

When in doubt, throw it out! A bearing costs $20-50. Lost core time costs hundreds.

üî¥ Worn Rubbers/Seals

Rubber TypeLocationReplace When
Top StabilizerTop of inner tubeEvery 200-300m
Bottom StabilizerAbove core lifter caseEvery 150-200m
O-ringsVarious sealsAny signs of wear

Signs Rubbers Need Replacement:

  • Cracking or splitting visible
  • Hardening (not flexible)
  • Flattened or compressed permanently
  • Chunks missing
  • Excessive wobble in wobble test
  • Vibration during drilling

Core Lifter Types & Replacement

Types of Core Lifters:

TypeBest ForAdvantagesDisadvantages
Split Ring Hard competent rock Reliable, simple Can damage soft core
Basket Broken/fractured rock Catches fragments Can jam
Spring Soft to medium rock Gentle on core Less positive grip
Collet Very soft formations Minimal damage May slip

How to Replace Core Lifter:

  1. Unscrew core lifter case from inner tube
  2. Remove old spring and lifter
  3. Inspect case threads and interior
  4. Insert new spring (proper orientation)
  5. Install new core lifter (check engagement)
  6. Test: Should move in easily, grip when pulled
  7. Thread case back onto tube (proper torque)

Common Problems:

  • Core dropping: Lifter worn, wrong type, or spring weak
  • Core jamming: Lifter engaging too early, wrong size
  • Won't release: Lifter damaged or wrong type for formation

Backend Troubleshooting Chart

ProblemPossible CauseSolution
Won't latch in rods Backend too long; Weak latch spring; Dirty latch Check measurements; Replace spring; Clean mechanism
Core won't pump up No water flow; Blocked waterways; Wrong pump pressure Check clearances; Clean bit/case; Increase pressure
No core recovery Core lifter bad; Inner tube rotating; Core washing away Replace lifter; Check bearings; Reduce pump pressure
Core jamming in tube Core lifter engaging early; Tube too small; Swelling formation Check lifter type; Verify size; Change drilling fluid
Water flow problems Blocked annulus; Wrong shell length; Clearances too tight Check clearances; Verify shell; Clean/ream if needed
Backend stuck downhole Shell too long; Collapsed hole; Latch won't release Work gently; May need fishing tools; Check shell length
Overshot won't engage Wrong overshot; Head damaged; Dirt/debris Verify compatibility; Inspect head; Clean thoroughly
Excessive vibration Bad bearings; Worn rubbers; Unbalanced assembly Replace bearings; Replace rubbers; Check alignment
Core twisted/ground up Inner tube rotating; Bad bearings; Wrong parameters Replace bearings; Check for binding; Reduce RPM/WOB
Backend rattles/loose Worn bearings; Loose components; Damaged threads Inspect all parts; Tighten properly; Replace damaged

Backend Maintenance Schedule

After Every Trip:

  • Clean entire assembly thoroughly
  • Inspect rubbers for wear
  • Check bearings (spin test)
  • Inspect threads
  • Check core lifter condition
  • Verify no cracks or damage

Every 50 Meters:

  • Deep clean all components
  • Re-lubricate bearings
  • Check all measurements
  • Replace worn rubbers
  • Inspect for hairline cracks

Every 200 Meters:

  • Replace all rubbers
  • Inspect bearings closely
  • Check reaming shell threads
  • Replace core lifter if worn
  • Complete measurement check

Signs Backend Needs Complete Overhaul:

  • Frequent core loss
  • Consistent vibration problems
  • Multiple worn components
  • Poor core quality
  • Difficulty latching
  • Water flow issues

Remember: Prevention is Cheaper Than Repair

  • Inspect backend before EVERY trip
  • Replace worn components proactively
  • Keep spares on site
  • Document what works
  • A $5 rubber can save hours of lost time

üö® Emergency Procedures

üî¥ CRITICAL Blowout / Kick

‚ö†Ô∏è EXTREMELY DANGEROUS

Formation fluids entering borehole uncontrolled

Symptoms:

  • Sudden increase in return flow
  • Pit level rising
  • Gas bubbles in returns
  • Flow continues when pump off
  • Drilling fluid being pushed out

IMMEDIATE ACTIONS:

  1. Alert Everyone (10 seconds)
    • Sound alarm
    • Notify all personnel
    • Clear non-essential people
  2. Stop Drilling (30 seconds)
    • Raise bit off bottom
    • Keep rotating slowly
    • KEEP PUMP ON
  3. Increase Mud Weight
    • Add weighting agent quickly
    • Circulate heavy mud
    • Build hydrostatic pressure
  4. Monitor Continuously
    • Watch pit levels
    • Monitor return flow
    • Check for gas
  5. If Cannot Control: EVACUATE
    • Move upwind minimum 300m
    • Call emergency services
    • Do not return until safe

üî¥ CRITICAL Fire / Explosion

Priority: LIFE SAFETY FIRST

Equipment is replaceable. People are not.

Immediate Actions:

  1. Alert (5 seconds)
    • Shout "FIRE!"
    • Sound alarm
    • Call emergency services
  2. Evacuate (30 seconds)
    • Get all personnel out
    • Move upwind 100m minimum
    • Account for everyone
  3. Assess (1 minute)
    • Small fire you can fight?
    • Fire extinguisher available?
    • Safe to approach?
  4. Fight or Flee
    • Small fire: Use extinguisher (PASS method)
    • Large fire: LEAVE - wait for fire department
    • Never risk life for equipment

Fire Extinguisher PASS Method:

  • Pull the pin
  • Aim at base of fire
  • Squeeze the handle
  • Sweep side to side

If Clothing Catches Fire:

STOP - DROP - ROLL

  • Do NOT run (makes it worse)
  • Drop to ground
  • Cover face with hands
  • Roll to smother flames

üî¥ CRITICAL Severe Injury

Medical Emergency Response:

  1. Assess Scene (5 seconds)
    • Is scene safe?
    • What happened?
    • How many injured?
  2. Call for Help (30 seconds)
    • Radio/phone emergency services
    • Give exact location
    • Describe injuries
    • Request helicopter if needed
  3. Check ABCs
    • Airway - Is it clear?
    • Breathing - Are they breathing?
    • Circulation - Is there pulse? Severe bleeding?
  4. Provide First Aid
    • Control severe bleeding
    • Don't move if spine injury suspected
    • Keep comfortable and calm
    • Monitor vital signs

Severe Bleeding Control:

  1. Apply direct pressure with clean cloth
  2. Maintain pressure for 10+ minutes
  3. Elevate if possible (not if fracture)
  4. Apply pressure to artery if necessary
  5. Tourniquet as LAST RESORT only

When to Use Tourniquet:

  • Life-threatening bleeding that won't stop
  • Multiple casualties (triage situation)
  • Partial or complete amputation

Apply 2-3 inches above wound, never on a joint. Note time applied.

Equipment Failure Emergency

Catastrophic Equipment Failure:

Examples: Engine explosion, hydraulic line burst, structural collapse

  1. Shut down all power immediately
  2. Evacuate danger zone
  3. Check for injuries
  4. Secure area (prevent others from entering)
  5. Notify supervisor
  6. Document what happened
  7. Do NOT restart until inspected

Emergency Contact Information

Keep This Information Posted:

  • Emergency services number (911 or local)
  • Company emergency contact
  • Nearest hospital location and phone
  • Poison control center
  • Site GPS coordinates
  • Helicopter landing zone coordinates
  • Nearest road access

Emergency Kit Contents:

  • First aid kit (comprehensive)
  • Fire extinguishers (multiple types)
  • Emergency radio/satellite phone
  • Emergency blankets
  • Flashlights and batteries
  • Emergency water and food
  • Signal flares
  • Emergency shelter

üöÅ Helicopter Operations & Safety

‚ö†Ô∏è HELICOPTER HAZARDS ARE DEADLY ‚ö†Ô∏è

NEVER approach from rear - tail rotor is invisible and INSTANTLY FATAL!

Ground Safety Rules

APPROACH RULES:

  • ‚úÖ ALWAYS from FRONT where pilot sees you
  • ‚úÖ Stay CROUCHED/LOW (rotors can dip)
  • ‚úÖ Wait for pilot signal before approaching
  • ‚úÖ Eye contact with pilot
  • ‚ùå NEVER from rear (TAIL ROTOR KILLS)
  • ‚ùå NEVER carry anything above head
  • ‚ùå NEVER on downhill side (rotor lowest point)
  • ‚ùå NEVER run near helicopter

Tail Rotor Safety

The tail rotor spins at 2000+ RPM and is nearly INVISIBLE

  • Stay 50+ feet away from tail
  • Never walk behind helicopter
  • Wind can push you into tail rotor
  • Most helicopter fatalities are tail rotor strikes

Loading/Unloading:

  1. Wait for pilot's thumbs up
  2. Approach from front, crouched low
  3. Secure all loose items
  4. No loose clothing or hats
  5. Load/unload from pilot's side when possible
  6. Exit same way - low and to front

Sling Load Operations

NEVER stand under hovering helicopter or suspended load!

Sling Load Procedure:

  1. Prepare Load
    • Secure all items properly
    • Balance load correctly
    • Remove loose items
    • Attach tag lines (12-15ft minimum)
  2. During Pickup
    • NEVER touch load until it touches ground
    • Wait 2 seconds for static discharge
    • Use tag lines to control load
    • Never stand under helicopter
  3. Attaching Hook
    • Let hook touch ground first
    • Use tag line to bring hook to load
    • Attach quickly but carefully
    • Signal pilot clearly when ready
    • Clear area immediately

Hand Signals:

  • Move Up: Arms extended, thumbs up
  • Move Down: Arms extended, thumbs down
  • Hold: Arms horizontal, fists clenched
  • Release Load: Arms crossed above head
  • Emergency Stop: Arms waving rapidly

Landing Zone (LZ) Setup

LZ Requirements:

  • 100ft x 100ft minimum clear area
  • Flat ground (slope <10¬∞)
  • No loose debris (tarps, tools, dust)
  • Clear of 50ft obstacles in approach path
  • No overhead wires or cables
  • Surface firm (not mud or deep snow)
  • Wind indicator visible to pilot

LZ Marking:

  • Mark center with weighted object (tire, bucket)
  • Use bright colored markers if available
  • Remove all loose items from area
  • Wet down dusty areas if possible
  • Have fire extinguisher ready

Communication with Pilot:

  • Provide GPS coordinates in advance
  • Describe landmarks
  • Report wind speed and direction
  • Note any hazards or obstacles
  • Give weather conditions
  • Establish radio contact before arrival

üöÅ Helicopter Hand Signals - Critical Safety Reference

Universal hand signals for communicating with helicopter pilots during landing, loading, and takeoff operations. These signals are critical for safety in remote drill sites.

‚ö†Ô∏è SAFETY FIRST:

  • Always maintain eye contact with pilot
  • Stand where pilot can clearly see you
  • Use exaggerated, deliberate movements
  • Never approach helicopter without pilot acknowledgment
  • Always approach from front where pilot can see you
  • Stay clear of tail rotor at all times

‚úã Basic Control Signals

Signal Description When to Use
START ENGINE Point to pilot, then make circular motion above head with index finger When pilot needs to start rotors
MOVE UP / TAKEOFF Arms extended, palms up, repeatedly move hands upward Signal pilot to increase altitude or take off
MOVE DOWN / LAND Arms extended, palms down, repeatedly move hands downward Direct pilot to decrease altitude or land
HOVER Arms extended horizontally to sides, palms down, hold steady Signal pilot to hold current position
MOVE FORWARD Arms extended forward, palms facing each other, move hands forward Direct helicopter to move toward you
MOVE BACK Arms extended forward, palms out, push hands away from body Direct helicopter to move away from you
MOVE LEFT (Pilot's Left) Extend right arm horizontally, make sweeping motion to your left Direct pilot to move left from their perspective
MOVE RIGHT (Pilot's Right) Extend left arm horizontally, make sweeping motion to your right Direct pilot to move right from their perspective

Remember: Left and right are always from the pilot's perspective, not yours. Stand facing the helicopter and signal to move them in the direction YOU want them to go.

‚ö†Ô∏è Critical Safety Signals

Signal Description When to Use
üõë EMERGENCY STOP Arms crossed above head forming an "X", hold position IMMEDIATE DANGER - Stop all operations now
‚ö†Ô∏è HOLD POSITION One or both arms extended with clenched fists held steady Hold current position, wait for next signal
üö´ WAVE OFF / ABORT Wave both arms vigorously side to side above head Abort landing, unsafe conditions, go around
CUT ENGINE Move hand across throat in slashing motion Shut down engines (use only in emergency)
ALL CLEAR / OK Thumbs up with arm extended, or make "OK" sign with fingers Operation complete, all clear, acknowledged
PROBLEM / NOT OK Thumbs down with arm extended Problem exists, do not proceed

EMERGENCY STOP (X above head) overrides all other signals. Use immediately if you see danger the pilot cannot see - person in rotor zone, equipment hazard, wildlife, anything life-threatening.

üì¶ Load Handling Signals

Signal Description When to Use
HOOK LOAD Pat top of head with one hand Ready to attach load to longline hook
UNHOOK LOAD Touch top of head then point away Ready to release load from hook
RELEASE LOAD Make throwing motion away from body Tell pilot to release external load
MOVE UP SLOWLY Arms extended up, palms up, slow gentle upward motion Lift load slowly - delicate or unstable cargo
MOVE DOWN SLOWLY Arms extended down, palms down, slow gentle downward motion Lower load slowly - setting down delicate items
ADJUST LOAD Point at load, then signal direction needed Load is swinging or needs repositioning

Sling Load Safety Checklist:

  • Ensure load is properly secured and balanced
  • Check sling rating exceeds load weight
  • Remove all loose items that could fly off
  • Stand clear before signaling "hook load"
  • Never stand under suspended load
  • Let suspended load touch ground first (static discharge)
  • Always use hook-up gloves (never bare hands)

üéØ Precision Positioning Signals

Signal Description When to Use
MOVE FORWARD SLOWLY Arms bent at elbows, hands chest level, make small forward pushing motions Final positioning, approach slowly
MOVE BACK SLOWLY Arms bent at elbows, hands chest level, make small backward pulling motions Back away from position slowly
SLIGHT UP One hand raised, palm up, small upward movements Minor altitude adjustment needed
SLIGHT DOWN One hand extended, palm down, small downward movements Minor altitude adjustment needed
HOLD ALTITUDE One arm extended horizontally, palm down, hold steady Perfect height, maintain current altitude
INCHES UP/DOWN Hold thumb and index finger close together (showing small gap), move up or down Very small adjustments - final positioning

Precision Tip: Use exaggerated movements for large adjustments, smaller controlled movements for fine positioning. Speed of your signal indicates urgency - faster movements = faster response needed.

üö∂ Approach & Departure Zones

Safe Approach Zones:

Zone Safety Level Instructions
‚úÖ FRONT (10-2 o'clock) SAFE Always approach from front where pilot can see you. Stay low, walk toward nose in pilot's view
‚ö†Ô∏è SIDES (3-4, 8-9 o'clock) CAUTION Avoid if possible. Pilot has limited visibility. Main rotor tips lower on sides
üö´ REAR (5-7 o'clock) DEADLY - NEVER NEVER approach from rear. Tail rotor is invisible when spinning and LETHAL

Critical Approach Rules:

  • Stay LOW: Crouch when approaching - main rotor dips on downhill side
  • Wait for signal: Never approach until pilot signals you forward
  • Secure loose items: Hard hats, tools, tarps can be sucked into rotors
  • Watch your step: Uneven ground + rotor wash = trip hazard
  • No long objects: Drill rods, pipes, lumber carried vertically can hit rotor
  • One person signals: Only one designated person gives hand signals

üö´ TAIL ROTOR DANGER ZONE: The rear 180¬∞ of any helicopter is a NO-GO zone. Tail rotors spin at 2000+ RPM and are nearly invisible. They have killed experienced workers who momentarily forgot this rule. NEVER APPROACH FROM BEHIND.

üìã Landing Zone (LZ) Preparation

Minimum LZ Requirements:

Requirement Specification Why It Matters
Size 100 feet (30m) diameter minimum 2x rotor diameter clearance needed
Surface Level, firm, free of loose debris Prevents rotor wash from blowing objects
Slope Less than 10¬∞ grade Steep slope = rotor strike risk
Obstacles Clear of trees, poles, wires Approach/departure path must be clear
Wind Indicator Windsock or smoke visible to pilot Pilot needs wind direction for landing

LZ Preparation Checklist:

  • Clear all loose items (tarps, empty drums, plastic, cardboard)
  • Mark LZ center with weighted marker (H-pattern if possible)
  • Remove or secure drilling supplies, core boxes
  • Tie down equipment, flags, signs within 100ft
  • Brief all personnel on helicopter safety
  • Designate one person for hand signals
  • Establish communication with pilot (radio if available)
  • Have fire extinguisher nearby
  • Know location of nearest medical facility

Pro Tip: Place a bright colored tarp weighted at corners in LZ center with a large "H" marked on it. This helps pilot identify the exact landing spot from distance.

üå¨Ô∏è Weather & Environmental Signals

Signal Description When to Use
WIND DIRECTION Stand with back to wind, arms extended to sides Show pilot which way wind is blowing
DUST/DEBRIS WARNING Point at ground, make swirling motion Alert pilot to loose material on ground
VISIBILITY PROBLEM Hold hands near eyes, shake head Dust-out, whiteout, can't see helicopter
GO AROUND / WAVE OFF Wave both arms frantically above head Conditions unsafe, abort landing

When to Wave Off Landing:

  • Animal or person entered LZ
  • Equipment shifted/fell into LZ
  • Sudden wind gust or wind change
  • Visibility suddenly reduced (dust, fog)
  • You see hazard pilot cannot see
  • You're not ready for any reason

Better Safe Than Sorry: If you're uncertain about ANY aspect of safety, wave off the landing. Pilots would rather go around than risk an accident. Your judgment on ground conditions is critical.

üéì Training & Communication

Before Any Helicopter Operations:

  1. Pre-flight briefing: Discuss signals with pilot and ground crew
  2. Designate signal person: Only ONE person gives signals to avoid confusion
  3. Practice signals: Do a quick practice session if crew is inexperienced
  4. Establish radio contact: If available, use as backup to hand signals
  5. Review emergency procedures: What to do if something goes wrong

Common Mistakes to Avoid:

Mistake Risk Correct Action
Multiple people signaling Pilot confusion, conflicting directions Only designated person signals
Unclear, hesitant signals Pilot can't understand intent Use exaggerated, confident movements
Breaking eye contact with pilot Pilot loses signaler, unsafe Always maintain eye contact
Standing in poor visibility position Pilot can't see signals Position yourself in pilot's clear view
Approaching without acknowledgment Pilot not ready, rotor strike risk Wait for pilot's "come ahead" signal

Remember: Helicopter operations are high-risk activities. Take them seriously, stay focused, and never become complacent. One moment of distraction can be fatal.

üì± Radio Communication Backup

If radio communication is available, use it as backup to hand signals. Never rely on radio alone - pilot may not hear in noisy conditions.

Standard Radio Calls:

  • "Helicopter [callsign], LZ is clear for landing"
  • "Hold position, checking LZ"
  • "Wave off, wave off - animal in LZ"
  • "Load secure, ready for pickup"
  • "Load released, clear to depart"
  • "Emergency, emergency - shut down engines"

Best Practice: Use radio for information ("LZ is rocky on east side") and hand signals for directions. This combination provides clear communication while maintaining visual contact.

Emergency Procedures

If Helicopter Crashes:

  1. Stay Clear Initially
    • Wait for rotors to stop completely
    • Watch for fire
    • Approach only if occupants in immediate danger
  2. If Safe to Approach
    • Shut off fuel (red cutoff switch on left side)
    • Turn off battery master switch
    • Help occupants exit away from aircraft
    • Move upwind 300+ feet minimum
  3. Call Emergency Services
    • Report location precisely
    • Number of occupants and injuries
    • Fire or fuel leak?
    • Request EMS and fire response

DO NOT risk your life attempting rescue

Aircraft are replaceable. You are not. Only approach if:

  • Rotors have stopped completely
  • No fire or imminent fire risk
  • You can safely reach occupants
  • Immediate danger to occupant life

‚õèÔ∏è Underground Diamond Drilling Operations

üö® UNDERGROUND HAZARD WARNING

Underground drilling is HIGH RISK. Multiple hazards can be IMMEDIATELY FATAL.

  • Gas exposure - CH‚ÇÑ, CO, H‚ÇÇS, CO‚ÇÇ can kill in minutes
  • Ground falls - Rock falls, cave-ins, rib failures
  • Confined spaces - Oxygen deficiency, entrapment
  • Fire underground - Smoke inhalation, limited escape
  • Water inrush - Drowning risk in flooded areas
  • Equipment hazards - Moving machinery in tight spaces

NEVER work underground without proper training, equipment, and supervision!

üìã Underground Drilling Overview

Underground diamond drilling is performed from within mines, tunnels, and underground excavations. It requires specialized procedures beyond surface drilling due to confined spaces, ground conditions, ventilation requirements, and mine-specific regulations.

This section covers: Up-holes, down-holes, fan drilling, back reaming, underground equipment operation, refuge stations, fire survival, rig positioning, James rods, overshot procedures, and underground-specific troubleshooting.

üìú Underground Regulations & Mine Procedures

‚ö†Ô∏è CRITICAL: Follow ALL Mine-Specific Rules

Every mine has unique regulations based on:

  • Geology and ground conditions
  • Ventilation system design
  • Mining method (cut and fill, long-hole, shrinkage, etc.)
  • Hazard assessments (gas, water, seismic)
  • Jurisdictional requirements (provincial/state, federal)

Mine rules ALWAYS override general procedures. When in doubt, ask mine supervision.

Standard Underground Requirements:

Requirement Description Why It Matters
Mine Orientation Mandatory safety training before entering mine Learn emergency exits, refuge locations, hazards, procedures
Lamp & Self-Rescuer Cap lamp + self-contained self-rescuer (SCSR) Essential for visibility and emergency escape from gas/fire
Tag Board System Check in/out when entering/leaving mine Critical for accounting for all personnel in emergency
Communication Two-way radio on mine frequency Report to surface, call for help, receive emergency alerts
Work Permit Hot work, confined space, electrical, etc. Ensures hazards assessed and controls in place
Ground Inspection Examine back and ribs before starting work Identify loose rock, scaling required, support needs
Gas Testing Multi-gas detector before and during work Detect CH‚ÇÑ, CO, H‚ÇÇS, O‚ÇÇ deficiency before exposure
Barricade & Signage Block access, post warning signs Prevent unauthorized entry to drill area
Ventilation Check Verify airflow direction and volume Ensure adequate fresh air, remove drill fumes/dust
Buddy System Never work alone underground Immediate help available if injured or in trouble

ü¶∫ Underground Personal Protective Equipment (PPE)

Mandatory Underground PPE:

  • Hard hat with chin strap - Impact protection from ground falls, low backs
  • Cap lamp (headlamp) - Hands-free lighting, battery charged before shift
  • Self-contained self-rescuer (SCSR) - Emergency breathing apparatus for fire/gas escape
    • Worn on belt at all times underground
    • Provides 30-60 minutes breathable air (varies by model)
    • Training required to use properly
    • Check expiry date before each shift
  • Safety glasses with side shields - Flying rock chips, splashing fluids
  • Hearing protection - Drills, equipment, blasting noise exposure
  • High-visibility vest or jacket - Visibility to mobile equipment operators
  • Steel-toed boots - CSA approved, ankle support for rough ground
  • Work gloves - Cut-resistant for handling rods, chain, sharp core
  • Two-way radio - On mine frequency, fully charged, spare battery
  • Multi-gas detector - Personal or crew unit, calibrated and bump-tested

Additional PPE for Specific Tasks:

Task Additional PPE Required
Drilling (wet) Rubber boots, rain suit or apron, face shield for splashing
Handling chemicals Chemical-resistant gloves, goggles, apron per SDS
Cutting/grinding Full face shield, leather gloves, welding jacket if hot work
Confined space entry Full-body harness, lifeline, additional SCBA if required
Scaling loose rock Leather gloves, long-sleeved shirt, possibly face shield
Working at heights Full-body harness, double lanyard, shock absorber, anchor point

SCSR (Self-Contained Self-Rescuer) - Life-Saving Device:

Know how to use it BEFORE you need it!

  • Attend training session - practice donning the unit
  • Inspect before each shift - check seal, expiry date
  • Wear on belt - accessible in seconds
  • In emergency: 1) Activate, 2) Don over head, 3) Pull tight, 4) Breathe through mouth, 5) Escape immediately
  • Limited duration - 30-60 min - get out fast!

üö™ Working in Confined Spaces Underground

‚ö†Ô∏è CONFINED SPACE = HIGH RISK OF DEATH

Confined spaces underground kill multiple workers every year.

A confined space is any area that:

  • Is not designed for continuous human occupancy
  • Has limited or restricted means of entry/exit
  • Can trap hazardous atmospheres (low O‚ÇÇ, high gas)

Examples underground: Sumps, ore passes, raise climbs, old workings, voids

Confined Space Entry Procedure:

STEP 1: OBTAIN CONFINED SPACE ENTRY PERMIT ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Document from site supervision ‚Ä¢ Identifies hazards specific to space ‚Ä¢ Lists required controls (ventilation, PPE, testing, rescue) ‚Üì STEP 2: ATMOSPHERIC TESTING ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Test BEFORE entry with calibrated gas detector ‚Ä¢ Oxygen: 19.5% - 23% acceptable ‚Ä¢ LEL (Lower Explosive Limit): < 10% acceptable ‚Ä¢ Toxic gases (CO, H‚ÇÇS): Below exposure limits ‚Ä¢ If atmosphere unsafe ‚Üí ventilate and retest ‚Üì STEP 3: ESTABLISH VENTILATION ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Set up forced-air ventilation if required ‚Ä¢ Flexible duct to bring fresh air into space ‚Ä¢ Blow air in (preferred) or exhaust air out ‚Ä¢ Maintain continuous ventilation during work ‚Üì STEP 4: SETUP RESCUE EQUIPMENT ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Tripod and winch if vertical entry ‚Ä¢ Full-body harness on entrant ‚Ä¢ Lifeline attached to harness ‚Ä¢ Rescue personnel standing by topside ‚Ä¢ Additional SCBA available if needed ‚Üì STEP 5: ASSIGN HOLE WATCH (ATTENDANT) ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Trained person stationed at entry ‚Ä¢ Maintains visual/voice contact with entrant ‚Ä¢ Monitors gas detector readings ‚Ä¢ Never leaves post unless relieved ‚Ä¢ Authority to order immediate evacuation ‚Üì STEP 6: CONTINUOUS GAS MONITORING ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Entrant carries gas detector ‚Ä¢ Hole watch monitors readings ‚Ä¢ If alarm activates ‚Üí evacuate immediately ‚Üì STEP 7: EMERGENCY RESCUE READY ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Rescue team identified and briefed ‚Ä¢ Rescue equipment tested and ready ‚Ä¢ If entrant in trouble ‚Üí DO NOT RUSH IN ‚Ä¢ Use retrieval system to extract entrant ‚Ä¢ Call for emergency help immediately

‚ö†Ô∏è NEVER Enter Confined Space Without:

  • ‚úì Valid entry permit
  • ‚úì Atmospheric testing complete and acceptable
  • ‚úì Continuous ventilation established
  • ‚úì Hole watch (attendant) stationed at entry
  • ‚úì Rescue equipment ready and tested
  • ‚úì Communication established (voice, radio, signals)
  • ‚úì Emergency response team alerted and standing by

More people die trying to rescue someone than from the original incident. DO NOT become a secondary victim!

‚¨ÜÔ∏è Up-Hole Drilling (Drilling Vertically Upward)

Up-holes are drilled from lower levels to upper levels for ventilation, escape routes, ore passes, exploration, or hydrological testing. They present unique challenges compared to surface or down-hole drilling.

Up-Hole Drilling Challenges:

Challenge Why It's a Problem Solution
Water return Water runs back down hole onto rig and crew Rain gear, tarps, pump water away, use water rings/packers
Core blockage Core wants to fall out of barrel with gravity Use core lifters, reduce WOB, pull runs shorter (1-1.5m)
Cuttings accumulation Cuttings settle in annulus, pack off hole High fluid flow, viscosity, frequent circulation
Rod weight Full weight of string on bit - excessive WOB Use pullback pressure to reduce WOB to desired level
Collar positioning Difficult to position rig at ceiling/back Build stable platform, anchor rig securely, use jacks
Rock falls Loose rock falls from back onto crew/rig Scale back thoroughly, install ground support, use catch nets

Up-Hole Drilling Procedure:

  1. Setup and Positioning:
    • Build level, stable platform under collar location (see staging section below)
    • Position rig vertically - use plumb bob to verify
    • Anchor rig securely - bolts to floor/platform, chains/cables to walls if needed
    • Install catch tarp or canopy over rig to deflect water
    • Set up sump and pump to remove water from area
  2. Collar the Hole:
    • Use short collar tube (casing) set with resin or mechanical anchor
    • Collar must be secure - full weight of string pulls on it
    • Start with low RPM and WOB - rock often fractured near opening
    • First meter critical - keep hole straight
  3. Drilling Parameters:
    • WOB: Use pullback to reduce weight - aim for 30-50% of normal
    • RPM: Normal to slightly reduced - avoid vibration
    • Fluid Flow: Maximum pump capacity - need high velocity to lift cuttings
    • Fluid Viscosity: Increase with bentonite/polymers - helps suspend cuttings
  4. Core Runs:
    • Pull runs shorter than normal - 1.0 to 1.5m typical
    • Use core lifter case (inner tube lifter spring)
    • Don't push last bit - core can jam and fall out
    • Pull barrel slowly - sudden jerks can lose core
  5. Water Management:
    • Expect water to run back down continuously
    • Keep work area pumped out - slipping hazard
    • Wear rain gear - you will get soaked
    • Consider water rings or packers if severe

‚ö†Ô∏è Up-Hole Safety Critical Points:

  • Platform stability: Rig + string can weigh 2-3+ tonnes - must support safely
  • Overhead hazards: Scale back thoroughly, install support, watch for loose
  • Water hazards: Slippery floors, electrical shock risk if equipment wet
  • String hanging weight: Secure rig - if collar fails, string falls violently
  • Breakthrough: When break through to upper level, risk of water inrush or bad air

üîÑ Back Reaming (Reaming Up-Hole or Enlarging Hole)

Back reaming is the process of enlarging a drilled hole by pulling a larger-diameter reaming shell or bit back through the hole. Common for creating ventilation raises, ore passes, or utility holes.

Back Reaming Methods:

Method Description Applications
Conventional Reaming Use reaming shell pulled behind drill string Enlarge pilot hole 1-2 sizes (e.g., NQ to HQ to PQ)
Raise Boring Specialized raise boring machine, large diameter Production raises, ventilation shafts (0.5m to 3m+ diameter)
Slot Drilling Drill multiple parallel holes, blast between Create larger opening when mechanical reaming impractical

Conventional Back Reaming Procedure:

  1. Drill Pilot Hole:
    • Drill NQ (or BQ) pilot hole full depth
    • Ensure hole is straight and true
    • Break through to upper level carefully
    • Leave rods in hole if reaming immediately
  2. Attach Reaming Shell:
    • At top of hole (upper level), attach reaming shell to rod string
    • Reaming shell is larger diameter (e.g., HQ reaming shell on NQ rods)
    • Secure connection - full torque, safety wire if required
  3. Pull Reaming Shell Down:
    • From lower level (drilling level), slowly pull string down
    • Reaming shell grinds hole larger as it's pulled through
    • Rotate slowly - 30-50 RPM typical
    • Use feed pressure (pullback) to control reaming rate
    • Maximum fluid flow - must remove cuttings efficiently
  4. Repeat if Needed:
    • Can ream in stages: NQ ‚Üí HQ ‚Üí PQ ‚Üí larger
    • Each pass enlarges hole one size
    • Cuttings volume increases significantly with each pass

üö® REAMING HAZARDS:

  • Rod string tension: Pulling heavy reaming shell creates high tension - rod or connection failure risk
  • Reaming shell stuck: Can jam in hole if cuttings accumulate or hole collapses
  • Water inrush: Enlarging hole can encounter water zones - sudden flooding risk
  • Ground instability: Reaming removes rock support - ground can fall into hole
  • Cuttings overload: Huge volume of cuttings to remove - can overwhelm sump/pump

üöú Mine Cat (Tractor) Operations - Drill Rig Transport

Mine Cats (also called underground tractors, loaders, or LHDs - Load-Haul-Dump machines) are used to transport drill rigs and equipment within the mine. Safe operation is critical in confined underground spaces.

Common Underground Tractor Types:

  • Small Diesel LHD: 1-2 cubic yard bucket, articulated steering
  • Medium Diesel LHD: 3-4 cubic yard bucket, used for rig transport
  • Electric Underground Tractor: Battery or trolley powered, zero emissions
  • Utility Tractor: Flatbed or trailer attachment for equipment hauling

Safe Tractor Operation Underground:

Safety Practice Why It Matters
Training & Authorization Only trained, authorized personnel operate equipment - prevent accidents
Pre-Operational Inspection Check brakes, steering, lights, horn, ROPS/FOPS, fire extinguisher - catch defects before failure
Seat Belt Always Critical if ROPS-equipped - keeps operator in protective zone during rollover
Travel Speed Limits Mine-specific limits (often 15-25 km/h) - stopping distance, visibility limited
Use Lights & Horn Alert pedestrians, low visibility in drifts and crosscuts
Load Securely Rig or equipment must be chained/strapped - cannot shift during transport
Low Center of Gravity Keep bucket/load low when traveling - prevents tipover
Watch for Pedestrians Limited visibility, blind spots - pedestrians have right of way
Avoid Soft Ground Muskeg, wet fill, unmaintained drifts - can sink or tip
Clearance Awareness Know equipment height - watch for low backs, cables, pipes, roof bolts
Grade Awareness Descending ramps with load - gravity accelerates machine, brake fade risk
Communication Radio traffic control for main haulage ways - prevent head-on collisions

Transporting Drill Rig with Tractor:

  1. Preparation:
    • Secure all loose items on rig (tools, sample bags, fire extinguisher)
    • Lower mast to travel position if applicable
    • Drain fuel and oil if required by mine rules
    • Ensure rig balanced on transport attachment
  2. Loading:
    • Load rig onto flatbed, trailer, or forks carefully
    • Center load - equal weight distribution
    • Chain or strap rig in minimum 4 points
    • Use load binders - must be tight (no slack)
    • Test load security - shake rig, no movement allowed
  3. Transport:
    • Travel slowly - 10-15 km/h max with heavy/tall load
    • Watch clearances - back height, wall proximity
    • Stop completely before turning - articulated machines can tip
    • Descend ramps in low gear - use engine braking
    • If load shifts - STOP IMMEDIATELY, re-secure before continuing
  4. Unloading:
    • Level ground required for stable setup
    • Remove chains/straps carefully - rig under tension
    • Use spotter for backing up/positioning
    • Lower rig gently - damage to rig or floor if dropped

‚ö†Ô∏è Underground Tractor Accident Prevention:

  • Never operate alone - spotter helps with blind spots, assists if stuck
  • NEVER ride on outside of machine - extremely dangerous, often fatal
  • NEVER allow passengers unless designated seat - no riding on forks, bucket, or hood
  • If visibility poor (dust, smoke) - STOP and wait for air to clear
  • If machine tips or pins you - STAY IN CAB if ROPS equipped, call for help

Mine Cat Troubleshooting:

Problem Possible Cause Solution
Brakes weak/fading Overheating, low fluid, worn pads, air in lines Stop and cool brakes, check fluid level, bleed brakes, service
Steering sluggish Low hydraulic fluid, worn steering cylinder, linkage binding Check fluid, inspect cylinders, lubricate steering linkage
Engine overheating Coolant low, radiator blocked, fan belt broken, thermostat stuck Stop engine, check coolant, clean radiator, replace belt, test thermostat
Hydraulics slow Low fluid, pump worn, filter clogged, cold oil Check fluid level, warm up system, replace filter, test pump
Won't start Dead battery, fuel empty, safety interlock, starter failure Charge battery, refuel, check seat switch/brake pedal, test starter

üöß Barricades & Refuge Stations

Barricades (Blocking Drill Area Access):

Purpose: Prevent unauthorized personnel from entering drill area where hazards exist.

Barricade Type When Used Requirements
Safety Fence/Rope Standard drill setup Hi-vis rope/chain across drift entrance with warning signs
Hard Barricade High risk work (blasting, raise drilling) Physical barrier (timber, steel panels) + lock + signage
Lockout/Tagout Equipment maintenance, electrical work Energy isolation + personal lock + tag + verification

Barricade Setup Procedure:

  1. Position barricade at all access points to drill area (cross-cuts, ramps, drifts)
  2. Install warning signs: "DANGER - DRILLING IN PROGRESS - AUTHORIZED PERSONNEL ONLY"
  3. Additional signs as needed: "HEARING PROTECTION REQUIRED", "OVERHEAD WORK", "CONFINED SPACE"
  4. Log barricade in mine logbook - location, date/time, who installed, reason
  5. Communicate to mine control and supervision - everyone informed work area restricted
  6. Remove barricade only when work complete and area safe for general access

Refuge Stations (Emergency Shelter):

Purpose: Provide safe haven for miners if unable to escape mine during emergency (fire, gas, ground fall, etc.)

Refuge Station Features:

  • Sealed chamber - Airtight door, positive pressure inside
  • Breathable air supply - Compressed air cylinders or oxygen generation system (48-96 hour minimum)
  • Communication - Hardwired phone to surface, backup radio
  • Emergency supplies:
    • Water (4 liters per person per day)
    • Food (high energy, long shelf life)
    • First aid kit
    • Sanitation (toilet bags, hygiene supplies)
    • Lighting (battery powered, long duration)
    • Self-rescuers (extra SCSRs stored)
