  • Environmental controls - CO and CO‚ÇÇ scrubbers, temperature monitoring
  • Signage - Reflective "REFUGE STATION" signs on access routes

Refuge Station Protocols:

EMERGENCY OCCURS (fire, gas, collapse) ‚Üì Can you escape to surface safely? ‚Üì NO ‚Üì PROCEED TO NEAREST REFUGE STATION ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Take self-rescuer and cap lamp ‚Ä¢ Stay calm - don't run, conserve air ‚Ä¢ Help injured/slower colleagues ‚Ä¢ Close all doors behind you (slow fire/smoke) ‚Üì ENTER REFUGE STATION ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Close and seal door ‚Ä¢ Count personnel - report via phone/radio ‚Ä¢ Activate air supply ‚Ä¢ Put on SCSR if air quality poor ‚Ä¢ Monitor CO/CO‚ÇÇ levels ‚Üì WAIT FOR RESCUE ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Conserve energy - sit, stay calm ‚Ä¢ Ration food and water ‚Ä¢ Maintain communication with surface ‚Ä¢ Follow instructions from mine rescue team ‚Ä¢ DO NOT leave unless directed by rescue personnel

üö® CRITICAL: Know Your Refuge Stations!

  • During mine orientation, learn location of ALL refuge stations in your work area
  • Know multiple escape routes - primary and alternate
  • Participate in emergency drills - practice makes muscle memory
  • If working in new area, locate nearest refuge station FIRST
  • Report any refuge station deficiencies immediately

üî• Surviving Underground Fire

üö® UNDERGROUND FIRE = EXTREME DANGER

Smoke and toxic gases kill faster than flames underground.

  • Carbon monoxide (CO) from combustion - colorless, odorless, DEADLY
  • Smoke reduces visibility to zero - can't find way out
  • Heat and flame consume oxygen - suffocation risk
  • Panic and confusion - leads to wrong decisions

Your survival depends on: Speed of reaction, use of SCSR, staying calm, knowing escape routes

Fire Survival Priorities (in order):

  1. ALERT OTHERS - Yell "FIRE!", sound alarm, radio mine control immediately
  2. DON YOUR SCSR - Put on self-rescuer BEFORE air becomes unbreathable
  3. ESCAPE UPWIND - Move against air current (fresh air direction) if possible
  4. STAY LOW - Smoke rises, cleaner air at floor level
  5. MOVE QUICKLY BUT SAFELY - Don't run blindly, feel your way if needed
  6. IF CAN'T ESCAPE ‚Üí GO TO REFUGE STATION

How to Use SCSR in Fire Emergency:

FIRE DETECTED / SMOKE SMELLED / ALARM SOUNDS ‚Üì IMMEDIATELY ACTIVATE YOUR SCSR ‚Üì Step 1: Pull SCSR from belt pouch ‚Üì Step 2: Tear open package (pull red tab) ‚Üì Step 3: Remove nose clip and start retainer ‚Üì Step 4: Place mouthpiece in mouth, bite on tabs ‚Üì Step 5: Put nose clip on nose (MUST breathe through mouth only) ‚Üì Step 6: Pull hood over head, tighten neck strap ‚Üì Step 7: Start breathing - SLOWLY, through mouth ‚Üì Step 8: ESCAPE IMMEDIATELY ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Don't panic - SCSR provides 30-60 min breathable air ‚Ä¢ Air may feel hot - this is normal, KEEP BREATHING ‚Ä¢ Do NOT remove SCSR until confirmed safe by rescue personnel ‚Ä¢ If breathing becomes difficult, CHECK NOSE CLIP and MOUTHPIECE SEAL ‚Ä¢ Move toward fresh air or refuge station

Fire Survival Tips:

Survival Tip Why It Works
Don SCSR immediately Toxic gases can render you unconscious in 1-2 breaths - don't wait until you smell smoke
Know ventilation direction Escape against airflow (upwind) to reach fresh air - downwind leads into smoke
Feel the walls In zero visibility, running hand along wall guides you out - stay oriented
Close doors behind you Slows fire and smoke spread - gives others more time to escape
Stay with your crew Group survival better than solo - help each other, share information
Communicate your position Radio mine control with your location and direction - rescue knows where to find you
If trapped, go to refuge Sealed refuge station buys time (48-96 hours) for rescue to reach you
Don't investigate the fire Not your job - GET OUT, let mine rescue handle it

‚ö†Ô∏è What NOT to Do in Underground Fire:

  • ‚ùå DON'T remove SCSR to "check air quality" - one breath can kill
  • ‚ùå DON'T travel toward smoke - always escape upwind if possible
  • ‚ùå DON'T panic and run blindly - causes injuries, gets you lost
  • ‚ùå DON'T hide in dead-end drifts or old workings - rescue won't find you
  • ‚ùå DON'T attempt to fight fire unless trained and equipped
  • ‚ùå DON'T assume drill water supply can extinguish fire - it can't

üìê Building Level Floors & Staging for Rig Setup

Proper rig setup requires a level, stable platform. Underground floors are often uneven rock, muck piles, or wet ground. Building a stable base is critical for safe drilling and hole accuracy.

Requirements for Drill Platform:

  • Level: Within 1¬∞ in all directions - verified with level or digital inclinometer
  • Stable: No shifting, settling, or compacting under rig weight (2-5+ tonnes)
  • Solid base: Competent rock, compacted fill, or engineered timber platform
  • Load-bearing capacity: Must support rig + full rod string (up to 10+ tonnes for deep holes)
  • Drainage: Water runoff away from platform - prevents undermining and slip hazards
  • Access: Space for rod handling, core boxes, crew movement

Platform Construction Methods:

METHOD 1: Timber Mat Platform (Most Common)

Best for: Uneven rock floors, moderate spans, temporary setups

  1. Materials needed:
    • Hardwood timbers (6"x6" or 8"x8") - length as required (typically 2-4 meters)
    • Plywood sheets (3/4" thick minimum) for deck
    • Wood wedges and shims for leveling
    • Nails or screws to secure plywood
  2. Construction:
    • Lay timbers parallel, 2-3 feet apart (closer for heavy rigs)
    • Level each timber using rock wedges or shims underneath
    • Check level across all timbers with long straightedge + level
    • Secure plywood deck to timbers - no gaps, no overhangs
    • Re-check level after rig positioned - adjust wedges if needed
  3. Advantages: Fast to build, adjustable, reusable
  4. Limitations: Can settle over time (re-level periodically), flammable (keep fuel away)
METHOD 2: Leveled Rock Surface

Best for: Solid competent rock, when timber not available

  1. Tools needed: Rock hammer, crowbar, cold chisel, level, grinder (if available)
  2. Procedure:
    • Select area of solid competent bedrock
    • Remove loose rock, blast muck, debris
    • Identify high spots - mark with chalk/paint
    • Grind or chisel high spots down (slow work!)
    • Fill low spots with dry-pack concrete or grout (if available)
    • Check level frequently - aim for within 1¬∞
  3. Advantages: Permanent, very stable, no timber required
  4. Limitations: Time-consuming, requires solid bedrock, may not be achievable in soft rock
METHOD 3: Controlled Fill Platform

Best for: Large areas, long-term setups, soft ground

  1. Materials: Crushed rock, gravel, or well-graded fill (NO muck or organic material)
  2. Construction:
    • Remove soft ground, muck, or unstable material down to competent base
    • Place fill in 6-8 inch lifts (layers)
    • Compact each lift thoroughly with plate compactor or roller
    • Check level after each lift - adjust grade as needed
    • Final surface: well-compacted, level, no soft spots
  3. Advantages: Large areas, very stable if properly compacted
  4. Limitations: Time-consuming, requires materials and compaction equipment

Leveling Tips and Best Practices:

  • Use long level: 4-6 foot level more accurate than short one
  • Check multiple directions: Level must be true in all orientations
  • Don't rush: Properly leveled platform saves time later (straight holes, no rig vibration)
  • Secure platform: Anchor timbers or bolt rig down if required
  • Mark finished grade: Paint line on wall to verify platform hasn't settled
  • Monitor during drilling: Re-check level periodically, especially first few days

‚ö†Ô∏è Platform Safety:

  • Load limit: Know maximum load - rig + string + water can exceed 10 tonnes
  • Span limit: Timbers have maximum safe span - too long = break risk
  • No cantilevering: Platform must be supported full width - no overhangs
  • Edge protection: If platform elevated > 6 feet, guardrails required
  • Fall hazard: Gap between platform and wall can trap or drop people/tools

üí£ Safe Distance from Bootlegs, Cuts, and Blast Areas

üö® BOOTLEG = EXTREME DANGER

A "bootleg" is unexploded or partially exploded explosive charge left in a blast hole. Drilling into a bootleg can detonate it, killing the driller instantly.

NEVER assume a blast face is safe without proper inspection and clearance.

Definitions:

Term Definition
Bootleg Unexploded explosive charge remaining in blast hole after firing
Cut Initial blast holes (usually center of face) designed to create void for remaining holes to break into
Blast Face / Heading Freshly blasted rock face where excavation/drilling will occur
Misfired Hole Blast hole that failed to detonate as intended

Safe Approach to Blast Areas:

  1. WAIT for Blaster Clearance:
    • Only enter blast area after certified blaster inspects and declares safe
    • Blaster must account for all charges - none missing or misfired
    • Minimum waiting period: usually 15-30 minutes after blast (for fume clearing)
  2. Ventilation Check:
    • Blasting fumes (NOx gases) are toxic - must clear before entry
    • Use gas detector - CO and NO‚ÇÇ must be below safe limits
    • Adequate airflow established before workers enter
  3. Ground Inspection:
    • Certified ground control person inspects back and ribs
    • Scaling performed to remove loose rock
    • Support installed if required (rockbolts, mesh, shotcrete)
  4. Bootleg Inspection:
    • Blaster examines muck pile and face for unexploded charges
    • Look for: detonator wire, explosive sticks, suspicious holes
    • If bootleg suspected - EVACUATE AREA, call blaster immediately

Safe Drilling Distance from Blast Holes:

Minimum safe distances (check mine-specific rules - may be more conservative):

Situation Minimum Distance Reasoning
From known blast hole 3 meters (10 feet) Avoid drilling into hole or encountering stemming/charge
From blast face edge 2 meters (6 feet) Blast holes often angled - can intersect drill hole
After reported misfire 5+ meters (15+ feet) OR avoid area entirely Misfire location may be uncertain - extreme caution
In old workings NEVER drill without blaster clearance Historical bootlegs possible, undocumented charges

Procedure if Bootleg Encountered While Drilling:

DRILLING - HIT UNUSUAL RESISTANCE OR SEE EXPLOSIVE MATERIAL ‚Üì STOP DRILLING IMMEDIATELY ‚Üì DO NOT ATTEMPT TO REMOVE OR INVESTIGATE ‚Üì MARK LOCATION CLEARLY (paint, flagging tape) ‚Üì EVACUATE AREA - Minimum 100 meters (300 feet) ‚Üì NOTIFY MINE SUPERVISION & CERTIFIED BLASTER ‚Üì BARRICADE AREA - No entry until cleared by blaster ‚Üì BLASTER RESPONDS ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Identifies explosive type ‚Ä¢ Determines safest removal method ‚Ä¢ Removes or neutralizes bootleg ‚Ä¢ Declares area safe or remains restricted ‚Üì ONLY RETURN TO AREA AFTER BLASTER CLEARANCE

üö® BOOTLEG SAFETY - NEVER:

  • ‚ùå Continue drilling if you suspect bootleg
  • ‚ùå Attempt to remove explosive material yourself
  • ‚ùå Pull rods back forcefully - can detonate charge
  • ‚ùå Use water jet to clear hole - can sensitize explosive
  • ‚ùå Smoke, weld, or create spark near suspected bootleg
  • ‚ùå Assume old bootleg is "safe" because it's been there a long time

Explosives are ALWAYS DANGEROUS. Respect them or they will kill you.

üîß Victaulic Clamps, Header Assembly & Water Systems

Victaulic grooved-coupling systems are standard underground for water supply due to quick assembly/disassembly.

Victaulic Assembly (5 Steps):

  1. Inspect pipe grooves and gasket for damage
  2. Slide gasket over pipe end, center between grooves
  3. Position coupling halves over gasket
  4. Insert and hand-tighten bolts
  5. Torque bolts alternately (40-50 ft-lbs typical), pressure test

Header Assembly for Multiple Rigs:

  • Main supply: 3-6" from mine water system
  • Tees with valves: At each rig location
  • Individual hoses: 1-2" flexible to each rig
  • Label valves: Quick shutoff in emergency
  • Pressure gauge: Monitor system health

Troubleshooting:

ProblemSolution
Low pressure at rigCheck for leaks, verify valves open, check line size
Coupling leakingReplace gasket, re-torque bolts, check groove condition
Water hammerClose valves slowly, install air release valve

üî© James Rods (Casing Advancement System)

James Rods allow casing to advance simultaneously with drilling - critical for unconsolidated or fractured ground where holes collapse.

System Components:

  • Outer casing tube: Large diameter (HWT), prevents collapse
  • James rod: Threaded rod inside casing, transmits rotation
  • Casing shoe: Hardened cutting edge on casing bottom
  • Inner drill string: Standard rods (NQ, HQ) inside casing
  • Casing adapter: Connects to rig spindle

When to Use:

  • Unconsolidated ground (sand, gravel, broken rock)
  • Heavily fractured rock zones
  • Water-bearing zones (casing seals inflow)
  • Through mine workings (voids, old stopes)
  • Surface overburden before competent rock

Drilling Procedure:

  1. Install casing with shoe, insert inner drill string (bit extends 10-15 cm beyond shoe)
  2. Drill with inner string while advancing casing by rotation + downward force
  3. Pull inner string, add casing section, tighten joint with casing wrench
  4. Re-insert inner string and continue
  5. When through bad ground, continue with conventional drilling

Key Parameters:

  • Bit lead: 10-15 cm ahead of casing shoe
  • Fluid flow: Maximum - cuttings must exit between string and casing
  • Lubrication: Grease casing threads for easier extraction

Troubleshooting:

ProblemSolution
Casing won't advanceReplace dull shoe, drill ahead further, increase downforce
Inner string jammingIncrease fluid flow, pull back and circulate, reduce drilling rate
Can't pull casing outMay be stuck permanently - cut off at top if must abandon

ü™ù Overshots - Recovery Operations

Overshot is a fishing tool to recover stuck or broken drill string. Critical underground skill.

Overshot Types:

TypeBest For
Internal GrappleParted rod with open top, inside grip
External GrappleTwisted-off rod, collapsed top, outside grip
Taper TapLeft-hand thread taps into stuck rod
SpearExpandable wedge for hollow rod/casing
Screw-In TapFish has good accessible threads

Fishing Procedure:

  1. Assess: Determine fish depth, cause of sticking, if moveable
  2. Select overshot: Match to rod size and condition
  3. Run overshot: Lower to fish, circulate, engage (rotate/push to seat)
  4. Attempt recovery: Pull straight up, gradual force
  5. If won't pull: Work fish (push/pull/rotate), circulate, apply jar

Jarring (Impact Tool):

  • Up-Jar: Compress string, release for upward impact (for pack-off, keyseat)
  • Down-Jar: Stretch string, release for downward impact (for collapse)

Safety Warning:

  • Don't exceed rig pullback capacity - can damage rig
  • If fish frees suddenly, string recoils violently - stay clear
  • Know when to quit - abandon and bypass if hopelessly stuck

üíß Water Pumping & Management Underground

Water Sources:

  • Drill return water (cuttings-laden)
  • Groundwater inflow (fractures, aquifers)
  • Up-hole return (large volume)
  • Mine water and equipment runoff

Water Management System:

  1. Sump: Collect water at low point (1-2 m¬≥ minimum)
  2. Settling: Allow cuttings to settle, screen over pump intake
  3. Pump: Submersible (continuous), diaphragm (heavy sediment), or centrifugal (clean water)
  4. Discharge: Layflat hose to mine sump, route safely along walls

Pump Selection:

Pump TypeBest ForLimitation
SubmersibleContinuous duty, handles sedimentCan clog if heavy sediment
DiaphragmHeavy sediment, slurriesLower flow, needs air
CentrifugalHigh flow, clean waterClogs easily

Key Practices:

  • Monitor sump level - adjust pump rate as needed
  • Keep floor dry (slip hazard!)
  • Clean pump screen daily
  • Have backup pump available
  • If high inflow (10-100+ LPM), may need multiple pumps or grouting

Safety:

  • Wet floors = #1 injury cause - keep pumped
  • Water + electricity = danger - use GFI protection
  • Sudden inrush from unknown void can flood area

‚öì Anchoring Drill Rigs Underground

Anchoring secures rig against thrust forces, vibration, and movement during drilling.

Why Required:

  • Thrust force (rig pushes on floor when drilling down)
  • Pullback force (can tip rig when pulling rods)
  • Rotation torque (twisting force on rig)
  • Vibration ("walks" rig across floor)
  • Angled holes (directional thrust)

Anchoring Methods:

1. Bolt-Down (Best - Permanent)
  • Drill 1" holes 8-12" deep in rock floor
  • Install expansion anchors or resin bolts
  • Bolt rig base with washers and nuts
  • Very secure, won't move
2. Chain-Down (Common - Moderate)
  • Set rock bolts in back/walls (min 2, preferably 4)
  • Attach 3/8"-1/2" chains to rig anchor points
  • Tighten with load binders
  • Quick setup, adjustable, less secure than bolts
3. Deadweight (Quick - Low Security)
  • Place heavy objects (concrete, steel, rock) on rig base
  • Need 2-3x rig weight to resist thrust
  • Fast, no drilling, least secure
4. Timber Wedging (Emergency - Temporary)
  • Drive hardwood wedges between rig and rock
  • Very temporary, not secure

Anchor by Hole Angle:

AnglePrimary ForceStrategy
DownDownward thrustFloor anchors or chains to back
Up-holeUpward thrustFloor bolts essential, heavy chains
AngledCombined thrust + lateralMulti-directional - floor AND wall/back

‚ö†Ô∏è Inadequate Anchoring = DANGER!

  • Rig can tip over - crush operator
  • Rig can slide - collide with personnel/walls
  • Hole angle changes - drifts off target
  • Rod breakage - movement creates stress

If rig not securely anchored - DO NOT START DRILLING!

üí° Underground Drilling Tips & Best Practices

General Underground Wisdom:

  • Communication is critical: Radio surface regularly - they know you're okay
  • Never rush: Underground mistakes costlier and more dangerous - think ahead
  • Keep area clean: Good housekeeping prevents injuries and equipment damage
  • Buddy system always: Two-person crew minimum - immediate help if injured
  • Know escape routes: Multiple ways out - practice mentally
  • Respect the ground: Ground falls kill - scale ribs/back, don't work under unsupported rock
  • Inspect your rig: Catch problems early - small issue becomes big problem fast
  • Manage fatigue: Long shifts exhausting - rest breaks are safety breaks
  • Document everything: Depths, parameters, problems, ground conditions

Efficiency Tips:

  • Stage supplies: Pre-position rods, core boxes, tools - reduces travel time
  • Batch trips: Plan surface trips efficiently - consolidate errands
  • Organize workspace: Tools in consistent locations - find in dark
  • Pre-thread connections: Have next rod ready before pulling barrel
  • Label core boxes clearly: Depth, date, shift - prevents mix-ups

Underground-Specific Considerations:

TopicUnderground Reality
Hole AnglesUp-holes, down-holes, fan patterns common - rig must handle all
Setup TimeLonger than surface - platform building, rig transport, anchoring
WaterAlways an issue - sump and pump mandatory
DustConfined space amplifies - ventilation critical
LightingTotal darkness without power - backup lights essential
NoiseEchoes, amplifies - hearing protection always
SuppliesEvery item must be transported underground - plan ahead
EmergenciesLimited escape routes - know them cold

End of Shift Procedure:

  1. Complete current run, pull barrel, store core safely
  2. Clean work area - remove trip hazards, coil hoses
  3. Shut down rig properly - don't just kill power
  4. Check sump pump running if water inflow continues
  5. Secure tools and loose equipment
  6. Log shift: depth, conditions, problems, next shift plan
  7. Report to surface before leaving mine
  8. Check out on tag board - account for all personnel

üéØ The Underground Driller's Mindset:

Underground drilling requires constant situational awareness. You're working in a hazardous environment with limited escape routes, confined spaces, and multiple simultaneous hazards. Success comes from:

  • Planning ahead: Think three steps forward
  • Staying calm: Panic kills underground
  • Asking questions: If unsure - ASK
  • Respecting hazards: They're real and they're serious
  • Teamwork: Your crew is your lifeline

Every shift you go underground and come back safely is a success. Never take that for granted.

üßä Ice Drilling Safety - Frozen Lake Operations

‚ö†Ô∏è EXTREME HAZARD WARNING

Breaking through ice = HIGH RISK OF DEATH

Cold water shock can incapacitate you in seconds. You have minutes before hypothermia sets in.

  • NEVER work alone on ice
  • ALWAYS wear a floatation suit or PFD
  • ALWAYS have ice rescue equipment ready
  • Know ice rescue procedures before going out
  • When in doubt - DON'T GO OUT

Ice Thickness Safety Standards

CRITICAL: These are MINIMUM thicknesses for CLEAR, SOLID BLUE ICE

White ice, snow ice, or slush ice is ONLY 50% as strong!

Double the thickness requirements for white/snow ice.

Safe Load Capacity - Clear Blue Ice:

Ice Thickness Maximum Safe Load What It Can Support
10 cm (4") 90 kg (200 lbs) 1 person on foot - WALKING ONLY
15 cm (6") 450 kg (1,000 lbs) Group of people, snowmobile
20 cm (8") 900 kg (2,000 lbs) Small car, light equipment
30 cm (12") 2,700 kg (6,000 lbs) Light truck, small drill rig
38 cm (15") 4,500 kg (10,000 lbs) Medium truck, standard drill rig
45 cm (18") 6,800 kg (15,000 lbs) Heavy truck, large drill rig
60 cm (24") 11,300 kg (25,000 lbs) Heavy equipment, loaded trucks
75 cm (30") 18,000 kg (40,000 lbs) Very heavy equipment

üî¥ SAFETY FACTORS - ALWAYS APPLY

  • Moving loads: Reduce capacity by 50% (vehicles, equipment in motion)
  • Stationary loads: Use table values above
  • Congregated loads: Multiple items close together = Add weights and reduce by 30%
  • White/snow ice: DOUBLE the required thickness
  • River or flowing water: TRIPLE the required thickness (unsafe in most cases)

How to Calculate Your Drill Rig Weight

Step 1: Base Drill Rig Weight

Find in operator's manual or weigh on scale

Typical weights:

  • Portable handheld rig: 100-200 kg (220-440 lbs)
  • Small skid-mounted: 500-1,000 kg (1,100-2,200 lbs)
  • Medium truck-mounted: 2,000-4,000 kg (4,400-8,800 lbs)
  • Large track-mounted: 5,000-10,000 kg (11,000-22,000 lbs)

Step 2: Add Fuel, Fluids, Tools

  • Full fuel tank: 50-200 kg (110-440 lbs) depending on size
  • Hydraulic oil: 50-150 kg (110-330 lbs)
  • Water for drilling: Calculate based on operation (see Rod Weight section)
  • Drill rods on rig: Use rod weight chart (see Rods section)
  • Tools and supplies: 50-200 kg (110-440 lbs)

Step 3: Add Support Vehicles/Equipment

  • Support truck: 2,000-4,000 kg (4,400-8,800 lbs)
  • Water tank trailer: 500 kg empty + water weight
  • Core boxes and samples: 50-500 kg (110-1,100 lbs)
  • Generator: 100-300 kg (220-660 lbs)
  • Heated shelter/shack: 200-500 kg (440-1,100 lbs)

Step 4: Calculate Total and Check Ice

Total Weight = Rig + Fluids + Rods + Support Equipment

Example Calculation:

  • Medium drill rig: 3,000 kg
  • Fuel and fluids: 150 kg
  • Drill rods (100m NQ): 1,200 kg
  • Water supply: 500 kg
  • Tools and misc: 150 kg
  • Total: 5,000 kg (11,000 lbs)

Required ice thickness: 40-45 cm (16-18") minimum for stationary drilling

For moving on ice: 60-75 cm (24-30") minimum

Don't Forget to Account For:

  • Personnel weight (80-100 kg / 175-220 lbs per person)
  • Snow accumulation on ice (reduces effective thickness)
  • Water on top of ice (slush = dangerous)
  • Temperature (warmer = weaker ice)
  • Time of season (spring ice = unpredictable)

Ice Assessment Procedure

Test Ice BEFORE Every Operation

Ice conditions change daily. What was safe yesterday may not be safe today.

Pre-Operation Ice Survey:

Step 1: Visual Inspection (From Shore)

  • Look for cracks, pressure ridges, or dark spots
  • Check for open water or slush areas
  • Look for signs of previous break-throughs
  • Check for snow loading (heavy snow = weaker ice)
  • Identify inlet/outlet areas (weakest ice)
  • Note wind direction (affects ice integrity)

Step 2: Initial Penetration Test (First Person Only)

  1. First person wears:
    • Floatation suit or PFD (MANDATORY)
    • Ice picks around neck
    • Rope tied to shore or second person
    • Whistle or air horn
  2. Drill test holes every 10-15 meters:
    • Use hand auger or ice chisel
    • Measure ice thickness accurately
    • Record measurements on map
    • Mark safe vs. unsafe areas
  3. What to look for:
    • Clear blue ice = GOOD (strongest)
    • White bubbly ice = WEAK (50% strength)
    • Gray ice = DANGER (indicates water presence)
    • Layered ice = CHECK TOTAL THICKNESS

Step 3: Establish Safe Route

  • Mark safe route with flags or stakes every 5-10m
  • Avoid areas near shore (weakest)
  • Avoid inlets, outlets, and moving water
  • Stay away from pressure ridges and cracks
  • Test ice thickness along entire route
  • Create alternative escape routes

Step 4: Progressive Load Testing

  1. Send lightest vehicle first with NO passengers
  2. Driver wears floatation suit, door open, window down
  3. Move slowly (8-15 km/h / 5-10 mph maximum)
  4. Listen for cracking sounds
  5. If ice cracks - STOP, back up slowly, find new route
  6. Once light vehicle passes safely, progressively add heavier loads

Drilling Setup on Ice

Site Selection:

  • Choose thickest ice area available
  • Minimum 50 meters (165 ft) from shore
  • Avoid areas with visible cracks or pressure ridges
  • Away from inlets, outlets, springs, or current
  • Level surface (minimal slope)
  • Consider wind exposure and snowdrift patterns

Load Distribution:

Spread the Weight

NEVER concentrate heavy loads in small area

  • Use mats or planks: Distribute rig weight over larger area
  • Spacing: Keep support vehicles 10+ meters apart
  • No clustering: Don't park multiple vehicles together
  • Core storage: Move away from rig regularly
  • Fuel/water storage: 20+ meters from drilling area

Load Distribution Formula:

Pressure (kg/m¬≤) = Total Weight √∑ Contact Area

Example:

  • 5,000 kg rig on 4 small pads (0.5m¬≤ total) = 10,000 kg/m¬≤
  • Same rig on large mat (4m¬≤) = 1,250 kg/m¬≤ (MUCH SAFER)

Drilling Through Ice Layer:

  1. First, drill through ice to water/ground:
    • Ice is easy to drill - use standard bit
    • Typical ice thickness: 30-120 cm
    • Have plastic or metal casing ready
  2. Install surface casing immediately:
    • Prevents hole collapse
    • Keeps drilling fluid contained
    • Usually PVC or steel pipe
    • Extends above ice surface 30+ cm
  3. Continue drilling bedrock below:
    • Normal drilling procedures apply
    • May have water/sediment layer before bedrock
    • Keep fluid returns clear of ice surface

Ice Drilling Safety Equipment (MANDATORY)

Personal Safety Gear (EVERY person on ice):

  • Floatation suit or PFD - Type approved for ice rescue
  • Ice picks/awls - Worn around neck on cord
  • Whistle or air horn - For signaling emergency
  • Rope - 15m+ length, tied to shore point or vehicle
  • Thermal protection - Layers, waterproof outer
  • Headlamp/flashlight - For low-light conditions
  • Cell phone or radio - In waterproof case

Site Safety Equipment:

  • Ice rescue throw rope - 25m+ with floating bag
  • Rescue ladder or planks - To distribute weight during rescue
  • Ice thickness gauge - Manual auger or measuring device
  • Heated shelter - For warming if someone gets wet
  • Spare dry clothes - Full set including boots
  • Emergency blankets - Space blankets for hypothermia
  • First aid kit - With hypothermia treatment supplies
  • Emergency communication - Satellite phone if remote

Vehicle Equipment:

  • Tow straps and chains - Heavy-duty recovery gear
  • Winch - For vehicle recovery (if equipped)
  • Axe or ice saw - For emergency ice cutting
  • Shovel - Clear snow, dig out if stuck
  • Sand or traction aids - For getting unstuck

Ice Break-Through Emergency Response

If Vehicle or Equipment Breaks Through:

IMMEDIATE ACTIONS (First 30 seconds):

  1. GET OUT IMMEDIATELY - Don't try to save equipment
  2. Exit away from the hole
  3. Crawl or roll - distribute your weight
  4. Head toward solid ice or shore
  5. Call for help

DO NOT:

  • ‚ùå Try to drive out - makes it worse
  • ‚ùå Stay in vehicle hoping it will hold
  • ‚ùå Run toward the hole to save equipment
  • ‚ùå Attempt recovery until area is secured

If Person Falls Through Ice:

FOR THE VICTIM:

  1. Don't panic - Control breathing (cold shock reflex)
  2. Turn toward direction you came from - Ice was strong there
  3. Reach forward - Get arms onto solid ice
  4. Kick hard - Get horizontal in water
  5. Pull with ice picks - Dig into ice and pull yourself out
  6. Roll away from hole - Don't stand up immediately
  7. Crawl to safety - Distribute weight

FOR RESCUERS:

  1. Call for emergency help FIRST - 911 or emergency services
  2. DO NOT rush onto ice - You will break through too
  3. Throw rope or push rescue device - Reach, throw, row, GO (in that order)
  4. Extend ladder or plank - If close enough to reach
  5. Form human chain - If multiple rescuers, lie flat and link together
  6. Pull victim out horizontally - Keep them flat
  7. Get to warm shelter immediately - Hypothermia treatment critical

Post-Rescue Hypothermia Treatment:

Hypothermia can kill even AFTER rescue!

Immediate Actions:

  1. Move to warm, dry shelter
  2. Remove ALL wet clothing
  3. Wrap in dry blankets or sleeping bag
  4. Give warm (NOT hot) sweet drinks if conscious
  5. Warm core first (chest, neck, head, groin)
  6. Call for medical help
  7. Monitor breathing and consciousness
  8. DO NOT rub extremities or give alcohol

Ice Conditions by Season

Time Period Ice Conditions Safety Level
Early Winter (First Ice) Thin, unpredictable, uneven thickness üî¥ DANGEROUS - Wait for thicker ice
Mid-Winter (Peak) Thick, stable, strongest of season üü¢ SAFEST - Best time for operations
Late Winter Still thick but starting to weaken üü° CAUTION - Monitor closely
Spring (Breakup) Honeycomb structure, very weak üî¥ EXTREMELY DANGEROUS - DO NOT USE

Temperature Effects:

  • Below -10¬∞C (14¬∞F): Ice strengthens, very safe if thick enough
  • -10¬∞C to 0¬∞C (14-32¬∞F): Ice stable but monitor for changes
  • Above 0¬∞C (32¬∞F) daytime: Ice weakening - be very careful
  • Rain or warm spell: EVACUATE - Ice strength drops rapidly
  • Freeze-thaw cycles: Create weak spots - test frequently

Daily Ice Monitoring Checklist

Every Morning Before Starting Work:

  • Check weather forecast - Temperature? Precipitation?
  • Re-measure ice thickness at drill site
  • Visual inspection for new cracks or changes
  • Test ice with spud bar while walking to site
  • Check equipment weight hasn't increased (added supplies)
  • Verify all safety equipment is present and functional
  • Brief crew on ice conditions and escape routes
  • Ensure everyone wearing proper safety gear
  • Test communication devices
  • Confirm rescue equipment accessible

During Operations:

  • Listen for cracking sounds or ice movement
  • Watch for water seeping onto ice surface
  • Monitor ice around drill site for settling
  • Check vehicle/equipment hasn't sunk or tilted
  • Keep safe distance between personnel and equipment
  • Limit number of people in drilling area
  • Regularly clear snow from ice (see through to check condition)

Signs to STOP Operations Immediately:

  • üî¥ Cracking sounds increasing
  • üî¥ Water appearing on ice surface
  • üî¥ Equipment settling or tilting
  • üî¥ New cracks forming
  • üî¥ Ice thickness below safe minimum
  • üî¥ Temperature above freezing
  • üî¥ Rain forecast
  • üî¥ Any crew member feels unsafe

The Golden Rules of Ice Drilling

  1. When in doubt, DON'T GO OUT
  2. No job is worth your life
  3. Always work with a partner - NEVER alone
  4. Wear floatation protection AT ALL TIMES
  5. Have rescue equipment ready BEFORE going on ice
  6. Know how to rescue yourself and others
  7. Monitor conditions constantly - ice changes quickly
  8. Evacuate immediately if conditions worsen
  9. Equipment is replaceable - YOU ARE NOT

üèïÔ∏è Wilderness Survival & Bear Safety

üêª BEAR AWARENESS & SAFETY

Bears run 35mph, climb trees faster than you, swim, and are incredibly powerful.

PREVENTION is your best defense!

Identifying Bears:

Feature Black Bear Grizzly Bear
Size 200-600 lbs 400-1500 lbs
Shoulder Hump No hump Large hump
Face Profile Straight Dish-shaped
Claws 1.5" curved 2-4" long
Ears Taller, pointed Short, rounded
Color Usually black (can be brown) Brown/blonde (varies)

Bear Prevention

Make Your Presence Known:

  • Talk loudly, sing, clap hands
  • Use bear bells (drill noise helps!)
  • Travel in groups when possible
  • Make extra noise near streams, thick brush
  • Never surprise a bear

Food Storage:

  • Use bear-proof containers
  • Hang food 10ft high, 4ft from tree trunk
  • Store 100+ yards from camp
  • Never keep food in tent
  • Cook away from sleeping area
  • Dispose of garbage properly

Camp Location:

  • Away from trails and game paths
  • Not near berry patches
  • Away from water sources
  • Check for bear sign (tracks, scat, claw marks)
  • Have escape route planned

Bear Spray:

  • Carry on your person (not in pack!)
  • Know how to use before you need it
  • Effective range: 20-30 feet
  • Check expiration date
  • Practice removing safety
  • Spray downwind only

If You Encounter a Bear

GENERAL RULES - DO NOT:

  • ‚ùå RUN (triggers chase instinct)
  • ‚ùå CLIMB TREE (both species climb)
  • ‚ùå Scream or make sudden movements
  • ‚ùå Turn your back
  • ‚ùå Make direct eye contact

Distant Bear (>100 yards):

  • Stay calm
  • Speak in calm, firm voice
  • Back away slowly
  • Make yourself large (raise arms)
  • Keep bear in sight
  • Give bear escape route

Close Bear (<100 yards):

  1. Stop moving
  2. Talk in calm, monotone voice
  3. Get bear spray ready
  4. Stand your ground
  5. Make yourself large
  6. Back away slowly if bear allows

Bear Charges (Often Bluff):

  • Stand your ground
  • Prepare bear spray
  • Spray when bear is 20-30ft away
  • Aim for face and nose
  • Most charges are bluffs - bear will veer off

If Bear Attacks

Response Depends on Bear Type and Situation

BLACK BEAR Attack:

FIGHT BACK AGGRESSIVELY

  • Hit nose, eyes with anything available
  • Use rocks, sticks, tools
  • Yell and be aggressive
  • Kick and punch
  • DO NOT play dead with black bears

GRIZZLY Bear - Defensive Attack (with cubs):

PLAY DEAD

  • Lie flat on stomach
  • Hands clasped behind neck
  • Spread legs to prevent rolling
  • Stay still even if attacked
  • Wait for bear to leave area
  • Stay down for 20+ minutes

GRIZZLY Bear - Predatory Attack (stalking/hunting you):

FIGHT FOR YOUR LIFE

  • This bear wants to eat you
  • Hit face, eyes, nose
  • Use any weapon available
  • Be as aggressive as possible
  • Do not give up

How to Tell Defensive vs. Predatory:

  • Defensive: You surprised bear, cubs nearby, bear is agitated/nervous
  • Predatory: Bear is calm, focused on you, stalking behavior, no cubs visible

Wilderness Survival Priorities

Rule of 3's:

  • 3 minutes without air
  • 3 hours without shelter (in harsh conditions)
  • 3 days without water
  • 3 weeks without food

S.T.O.P. Method (If Lost):

  1. Stop - Don't panic, don't wander
  2. Think - Assess your situation calmly
  3. Observe - Survey your surroundings
  4. Plan - Develop a reasonable action plan

Survival Priority Order:

  1. First Aid - Treat injuries first
  2. Shelter - Protection from elements
  3. Fire - Warmth, signaling, morale
  4. Signaling - 3 of anything = distress
  5. Water - Find safe water source
  6. Food - Lowest priority (can wait weeks)

Signaling for Rescue

Universal Distress Signal:

Three of anything = SOS

  • 3 whistle blasts
  • 3 gunshots
  • 3 fires in triangle
  • 3 mirror flashes
  • 3 rock piles

Ground-to-Air Signals:

Make large symbols (3m+ tall) visible from air:

  • X = Need medical assistance
  • V = Need assistance
  • ‚Üí = Proceeding in this direction
  • LL = All is well

Making Signal Fire:

  • Choose open area visible from air
  • Have materials ready to add quickly
  • Green branches create smoke (daytime)
  • Dry material creates flame (nighttime)
  • 3 fires in triangle is best

üî• STARTING A FIRE - ESSENTIAL SURVIVAL SKILL

Why Fire is Critical:

  • Warmth (prevents hypothermia)
  • Signaling for rescue
  • Purifies water through boiling
  • Cooks food
  • Psychological comfort (morale)
  • Keeps predators away
  • Dries wet clothing

Fire Triangle - All Three Required:

  • Fuel: Combustible material (wood, grass, etc.)
  • Oxygen: Air flow
  • Heat: Ignition source

Gather Materials BEFORE Attempting Fire:

1. Tinder (catches spark/flame easily):

  • Dry grass, leaves, pine needles
  • Birch bark (paper-like, even when wet)
  • Cedar bark (shredded/frayed)
  • Cattail fluff, milkweed down
  • Lint from pockets
  • Fine wood shavings, wood dust
  • Dead pine twigs (very small, < pencil thickness)
  • Gather 2-3 handfuls

2. Kindling (small sticks that catch from tinder):

  • Dead, dry twigs (pencil thickness)
  • Small branches (finger thickness)
  • Bark strips
  • Wood shavings/feather sticks
  • Gather 2-3 armfuls
  • Must be completely dry!

3. Fuel Wood (sustains the fire):

  • Thumb-thick branches
  • Wrist-thick branches
  • Arm-thick logs
  • Progressively larger pieces
  • Gather 5-10 armfuls
  • Dead standing wood is driest (not ground wood)

‚ö†Ô∏è Golden Rule: Gather 3x MORE wood than you think you need!

You don't want to leave a struggling fire to search for wood in the dark.

Fire Location Selection:

  • Clear area 10 feet diameter down to mineral soil
  • Away from overhanging branches
  • Protected from wind (use natural windbreak)
  • Near water source if possible
  • Near your shelter (but not too close - 6-10 feet)
  • Rock ring or fire pit to contain fire
  • Away from dry grass or combustibles

Fire Lay Structures (Choose One):

Structure Best For How To Build
Teepee Quick ignition, boiling water Cone of kindling around tinder bundle, leave opening for air
Log Cabin Long-lasting warmth, cooking Square layers of logs, alternating direction, tinder in center
Lean-To Windy conditions Large log as backwall, kindling leaned against it at 45¬∞
Platform/Upside-Down Wet ground, long burn Large logs on bottom, progressively smaller on top, tinder on top

Ignition Methods:

Method 1: Lighter/Matches (Best if available)

  1. Always carry waterproof matches or lighter in survival kit
  2. Light tinder bundle from below/underneath
  3. Shield from wind with your body
  4. Once tinder is burning, gently add kindling
  5. Don't smother with too much wood too fast
  6. Add progressively larger pieces as fire grows

Tip: Dip matches in candle wax at home for waterproofing

Method 2: Ferrocerium Rod (Ferro Rod)

  1. Best emergency fire starter (works wet, lasts thousands of strikes)
  2. Hold rod close to tinder bundle (1-2 inches)
  3. Strike scraper AWAY from you at 45¬∞ angle
  4. Aim shower of sparks into tinder
  5. Use fast, firm strokes
  6. May take 3-10 strikes to ignite tinder

Tip: Practice at home! This skill requires technique.

Method 3: Flint & Steel

  1. Traditional method (works if wet)
  2. Hold char cloth or tinder fungus under flint
  3. Strike steel against flint edge at 45¬∞ angle
  4. Catch spark on char cloth
  5. Transfer glowing ember to tinder bundle
  6. Blow gently to create flame

Method 4: Magnifying Glass/Lens (Sunny days only)

  1. Use eyeglasses, camera lens, water in clear bag/bottle
  2. Focus smallest, brightest point of sunlight on tinder
  3. Hold steady until tinder begins to smoke
  4. Blow gently to create ember, then flame
  5. This can take 30-60 seconds of patience

Method 5: Battery & Steel Wool (Emergency)

  1. Use 9V battery or AA/AAA batteries
  2. Touch steel wool to both battery terminals simultaneously
  3. Steel wool will spark and glow
  4. Immediately place in tinder bundle
  5. Blow gently to create flame

Caution: Steel wool burns fast - have tinder ready!

Primitive Methods (Last Resort - Require Skill):

Bow Drill / Hand Drill: Friction-based, very difficult without practice

  • Requires specific wood types
  • Requires perfect technique
  • Very physically demanding
  • Low success rate without training
  • Don't rely on this unless you've practiced extensively

Fire Building Step-by-Step:

  1. Prepare fire site (clear area, rock ring)
  2. Gather all materials (tinder, kindling, fuel - MORE than you think)
  3. Build fire lay (teepee, log cabin, etc.)
  4. Place tinder bundle at center/base
  5. Ignite tinder (lighter, ferro rod, etc.)
  6. Shield from wind with your body
  7. Blow gently if needed (low, steady breaths)
  8. Add kindling gradually as tinder catches
  9. Add larger pieces as fire grows
  10. Maintain fire with consistent fuel additions

Fire Maintenance:

  • Keep plenty of wood nearby (within arm's reach)
  • Add wood before fire gets too small
  • Don't smother fire with too much wood at once
  • Create bed of coals for sustained heat
  • Bank fire at night (cover with ash for slow burn)
  • Never leave fire unattended
  • Keep water or dirt nearby to extinguish if needed

Wet Weather Fire Starting:

  • Look for dry wood under dense evergreens
  • Standing dead wood is drier than ground wood
  • Split wood exposes dry interior
  • Birch bark burns even when wet
  • Look for resin-rich "fatwood" (pine with heavy resin)
  • Feather sticks (shave wood into thin curls while attached)
  • Build fire under shelter (tarp, rock overhang)
  • Use more tinder and kindling than usual

üíß FINDING & PURIFYING WATER

Water is Life - You Have ~3 Days Without It

Dehydration symptoms: Thirst, dark urine, headache, dizziness, fatigue, confusion

You need: Minimum 2 liters/day (more in hot weather or physical exertion)

Finding Water Sources:

Best Water Sources (Priority Order):

  1. Running streams/rivers (better than stagnant)
  2. Springs (often cleaner)
  3. Lakes (use water away from shore)
  4. Rain collection (safest if fresh)
  5. Snow/ice (melt first, never eat frozen)
  6. Dew collection (tie cloth around ankles, walk through grass)
  7. Plant transpiration (bag over leafy branch)

Signs of Water Nearby:

  • Converging animal tracks (animals visit water daily)
  • Birds flying in straight lines at dawn/dusk
  • Swarms of insects (especially bees, flies)
  • Green vegetation in otherwise dry area
  • Sound of running water
  • Follow valleys/low ground downhill
  • Rock crevices may collect rainwater

‚ö†Ô∏è Water Sources to AVOID:

  • Stagnant pools (disease risk)
  • Water with bad smell or color
  • Water downstream of dead animals
  • Water near agricultural areas (pesticides)
  • Salt water (dehydrates you further)

Water Purification Methods:

Method 1: BOILING (Best - Kills All Pathogens)

  • Rolling boil for 1 minute (3 minutes above 6,500 ft elevation)
  • Kills bacteria, viruses, parasites, giardia
  • Let cool before drinking
  • Doesn't remove chemicals or sediment
  • Most reliable method in wilderness

Container options: Metal cup, can, birch bark container (won't burn if filled with water)

Method 2: Water Purification Tablets

  • Iodine or chlorine dioxide tablets
  • Follow package directions (usually 30-60 min wait)
  • Effective against most bacteria and viruses
  • Less effective against Cryptosporidium
  • Water may taste chemical (add vitamin C after wait time)
  • Always carry in survival kit

Method 3: Portable Water Filter

  • Sawyer, LifeStraw, Katadyn, etc.
  • Removes bacteria, protozoa, sediment
  • Does NOT remove viruses (less common in North American wilderness)
  • Follow manufacturer instructions
  • Keep filter from freezing (destroys it)

Method 4: Improvised Filter + Boiling (Emergency)

Step 1: Pre-filter to remove sediment

  • Use cloth, bandana, or coffee filter
  • Or build improvised filter: bottle with layers of grass, sand, charcoal, gravel
  • This removes particles but NOT pathogens

Step 2: MUST still boil after filtering!

  • Filtering alone does NOT purify water
  • Boil for 1 minute minimum

Solar Disinfection (SODIS) - Emergency Only

Clear plastic bottle + 6+ hours direct sunlight

  • Fill clear bottle, shake to oxygenate
  • Place in direct sun for 6 hours (48 hours if cloudy)
  • UV rays kill most pathogens
  • Only use if no other method available
  • Not 100% reliable

Water Collection Techniques:

Rain Collection:

  • Tarp, plastic sheet, or poncho funnel into container
  • Rainwater is generally safe to drink without purification
  • Let first rain rinse collection surface (removes debris)

Dew Collection:

  • Tie cloth around legs, walk through wet grass at dawn
  • Wring water into container
  • Can collect 1 liter+ in good conditions
  • Should still purify (may have contamination)

Transpiration Bag:

  • Clear plastic bag over leafy branch (still alive)
  • Seal around branch
  • Water condenses inside bag
  • Can collect 50-100ml per bag per day
  • Use non-poisonous plants only!

Snow/Ice:

  • NEVER eat snow/ice directly (causes hypothermia)
  • Melt first using body heat, fire, or sun
  • Takes a lot of snow to make little water (~10:1 ratio)
  • Add small amount of water first to prevent scorching container

üî¥ When to Risk Drinking Unpurified Water

Last Resort Only: If severely dehydrated and no purification possible

  • Choose clearest, fastest-moving water available
  • Prefer higher elevation sources (less contamination)
  • Better to risk giardia than die of dehydration
  • Giardia symptoms take 7-14 days - rescue may come first
  • But always purify if possible!

üè† BUILDING EMERGENCY SHELTER

Shelter is Your #2 Priority (After First Aid)

Hypothermia can kill in 3 hours without shelter

Core body temperature drop of just 2¬∞C (3.6¬∞F) causes serious problems

Shelter Location Selection - CRITICAL:

  • DRY ground (test by sitting - if you feel dampness, move)
  • Protected from wind (behind rocks, dense trees)
  • NOT in valley bottom (cold air sinks, fog collects)
  • Away from dead trees (widow makers can fall)
  • Not under large dead branches
  • Near water BUT not in flood zone
  • Visible to rescuers if possible
  • Check for animal dens/signs (bears, cougars)
  • Flat spot (sleeping on angle is miserable)
  • Drainage (rain won't pool where you sleep)

Shelter Types (By Difficulty & Resources):

Level 1: Immediate Protection (First Hour)

Use What You Have:

  • Get out of wind immediately
  • Use emergency blanket/space blanket from survival kit
  • Put on all available clothing layers
  • Find natural shelter: rock overhang, fallen tree, dense evergreen
  • Pile leaves/pine boughs for insulation
  • Don't wait to start building - use what's available NOW

Level 2: Tarp/Emergency Blanket Shelter (1-2 Hours)

A-Frame Tarp Shelter:

  1. Tie rope/paracord between two trees (7-8 feet high)
  2. Drape tarp over rope (ridgeline)
  3. Stake or weight down corners at 45¬∞ angle
  4. Close one end with gear/debris for wind protection
  5. Layer ground with pine boughs, leaves, grass for insulation

Lean-To Tarp Shelter:

  1. Tie ridgeline between trees (one end higher than other)
  2. Tarp slopes from high to low
  3. Stake out lower edge and sides
  4. Open side faces AWAY from wind
  5. Build fire 6-8 feet from opening (heat reflects in)

Tip: Always carry 8x10 tarp or emergency blanket in survival kit!

Level 3: Debris Hut (3-4 Hours, No Tarp)

Best Natural Shelter - Surprisingly Warm:

  1. Frame: Long ridgepole from tree to ground (45¬∞ angle)
  2. Ribs: Lean branches along both sides of ridgepole
  3. Lattice: Weave smaller sticks through ribs
  4. Debris: Pile leaves, pine boughs, bark 2-3 feet thick
  5. Interior: Stuff with dry leaves for insulation/bedding
  6. Door: Pile debris/boughs to close entrance

Key Principles:

  • Make it SMALL (just big enough to lay in) - easier to heat
  • Thick debris layer insulates (24+ inches minimum)
  • Opening should be small (reduce heat loss)
  • Fill interior with leaves to nest in
  • Expect to spend 3-4 hours building properly

Debris hut can keep you alive in freezing temperatures!

Your body heat is trapped by the insulating debris layer. Many survivors have made it through -20¬∞C nights in debris huts.

Level 4: Snow Cave/Quinzee (Winter Only, 2-4 Hours)

Snow Cave (If deep snow drift available):

  1. Find deep drift (8+ feet) against rocks/trees
  2. Dig entrance tunnel upward into drift
  3. Carve sleeping platform higher than entrance (cold air sinks)
  4. Dome ceiling (prevents dripping)
  5. Poke ventilation hole through roof (prevents CO2 buildup)
  6. Block entrance with snow blocks/pack

Quinzee (Built snow shelter):

  1. Pile snow into large mound (8+ feet high)
  2. Let settle for 1-2 hours (snow bonds)
  3. Insert 12-inch sticks all over mound as depth guides
  4. Dig out interior until you hit all sticks
  5. Create sleeping platform, dome ceiling
  6. Ventilation hole in roof

Inside temperature: Snow shelter can be 0¬∞C even when outside is -40¬∞C!

Level 5: Natural Shelters (Immediate Use)

  • Rock Overhang: Check for stability, watch for water dripping
  • Fallen Tree: Use root ball as windbreak, lean branches against trunk
  • Dense Evergreen: Branches create natural roof, clear snow from under
  • Cave: Check for animals first! Bears use caves.

Always improve natural shelter: Add debris, block wind, insulate ground

Ground Insulation - CRITICAL:

üî¥ Ground Steals Your Heat Faster Than Air

You can freeze to death on cold ground even in a warm sleeping bag!

Insulation Methods (Use Multiple Layers):

  • Pine boughs: 6-8 inches thick, overlapped like shingles
  • Dry leaves: 12+ inches compacted (they compress)
  • Dry grass: Thick layer, replace if gets damp
  • Bark slabs: Create platform off ground
  • Sleeping pad: Even 1/4" foam makes huge difference
  • Extra clothing: Lay under you (not just over)
  • Emergency blanket: Under you reflects heat back up

Test: Sit on your insulation. If you feel cold in 1 minute, add more layers.

Heat Retention Tips:

  • Make shelter SMALL (traps body heat better)
  • Block entrance (reduce drafts)
  • Layer clothing (air pockets insulate)
  • Remove WET clothing (cotton kills - "cotton kills when wet")
  • Keep head covered (lose 40% of heat through head)
  • Eat before sleeping (digestion generates heat)
  • Do jumping jacks before bed (warm up first)
  • Sleep with water bottle (prevents freezing, provides warmth)
  • Spoon position if with others (share warmth)

Shelter Building Common Mistakes:

  • ‚ùå Building too large (can't heat with body warmth)
  • ‚ùå Building in valley bottom (coldest spot)
  • ‚ùå No ground insulation (freeze from below)
  • ‚ùå Shelter faces wind (defeats purpose)
  • ‚ùå Under dead trees/branches (widow makers)
  • ‚ùå In animal trail (surprise encounters)
  • ‚ùå Too far from water (dehydration)
  • ‚ùå Waiting too long to build (dark/cold/exhausted)

Shelter Priority Decision Tree:

START: Do you have tarp/blanket?
  ‚îú‚îÄ YES ‚Üí Build tarp shelter (1-2 hours)
  ‚îî‚îÄ NO ‚Üí Continue
  
Is there good natural shelter nearby?
  ‚îú‚îÄ YES ‚Üí Use and improve it (add insulation)
  ‚îî‚îÄ NO ‚Üí Continue
  
Is it winter with deep snow?
  ‚îú‚îÄ YES ‚Üí Build snow cave/quinzee (3-4 hours)
  ‚îî‚îÄ NO ‚Üí Build debris hut (3-4 hours)

ALWAYS: Insulate ground heavily!
ALWAYS: Build before dark if possible!

‚úÖ Quick Shelter Checklist:

  • Location: dry, protected from wind, not in valley
  • Size: small enough to heat with body
  • Ground: heavily insulated (6-12 inches debris)
  • Roof: waterproof or thick debris (24+ inches)
  • Wind: entrance faces away from prevailing wind
  • Heat: fire 6-8 feet from opening if possible
  • Signal: visible to searchers if safe
  • Safety: no dead trees, not in flood zone

üÜò IF LOST OR TRAPPED - ACTION PLAN

STOP - Don't Panic and Wander

Most lost people die from hypothermia, not lack of rescue.

Staying put with shelter often saves more lives than trying to hike out.

S.T.O.P. Protocol - First 30 Minutes:

  1. S - STOP Moving:
    • Sit down immediately
    • Don't wander trying to "find the trail"
    • You'll only get more lost
    • Breathe deeply, calm yourself
  2. T - THINK About Your Situation:
    • When did you last know your location?
    • How long have you been lost?
    • What time is it? How many hours until dark?
    • What gear do you have?
    • Are you injured?
    • Does anyone know where you are?
  3. O - OBSERVE Your Surroundings:
    • Scan 360¬∞ around you
    • Do you recognize anything?
    • Can you hear vehicles, water, aircraft?
    • What's the weather? Getting worse?
    • Are you near water, shelter, firewood?
  4. P - PLAN Your Next Steps:
    • Should you stay put or try to navigate out?
    • What are your immediate needs? (First aid? Shelter?)
    • Create priority list based on conditions

Stay Put vs. Hike Out Decision:

STAY PUT if:

  • Someone knows your planned route/return time
  • You're injured or exhausted
  • Weather is bad or getting worse
  • It's getting dark (< 2 hours of light)
  • You have no navigation tools
  • Terrain is dangerous
  • You have good shelter location where you are

HIKE OUT ONLY if:

  • Nobody knows you're missing (won't be searched)
  • You're certain of direction to safety (road, town)
  • It's early day (4+ hours of light)
  • You're physically able (no injuries)
  • Weather is good
  • You can follow a linear feature (river, ridge, power line)

When in doubt, STAY PUT!

Statistically, people who stay in one place are found faster and alive more often than those who wander.

Survival Priority Checklist:

HOUR 1: Immediate Actions
‚îú‚îÄ Apply first aid to any injuries
‚îú‚îÄ Get out of immediate danger (cliff edge, avalanche zone)
‚îú‚îÄ Put on extra clothing (before you get cold)
‚îú‚îÄ Find/improve basic shelter from wind
‚îî‚îÄ Inventory what you have

HOUR 2-4: Establish Camp
‚îú‚îÄ Select proper shelter location
‚îú‚îÄ Build shelter OR improve natural shelter
‚îú‚îÄ Gather firewood (LOTS of it)
‚îú‚îÄ Start fire (warmth, signal, morale)
‚îú‚îÄ Locate water source
‚îî‚îÄ Set up signaling (mirror, whistle, bright clothing visible)

FIRST NIGHT:
‚îú‚îÄ Maintain fire through night
‚îú‚îÄ Stay dry and warm
‚îú‚îÄ Rest and conserve energy
‚îî‚îÄ Signal if you hear aircraft

DAY 2 ONWARD:
‚îú‚îÄ Improve shelter
‚îú‚îÄ Purify and drink water regularly
‚îú‚îÄ Maintain signal fires (3 = distress)
‚îú‚îÄ Conserve energy - don't overexert
‚îú‚îÄ Stay positive - most people are rescued within 72 hours
‚îî‚îÄ Ration food if you have any (but water is priority)

Survival Psychology - Stay Mentally Strong:

  • Will to Live: Strongest survival factor
  • Stay Busy: Tasks keep mind focused (gather wood, improve shelter)
  • Routine: Create daily schedule, gives sense of control
  • Positive Thoughts: Think about loved ones, reasons to survive
  • Small Victories: Celebrate each success (fire started, water found)
  • Don't Give Up: Rescue often comes when you least expect it

Remember:

Survival is 90% Mental, 10% Skills

Stay calm, work the priorities, conserve energy, and wait for rescue. Most lost people are found within 1-3 days.

üîå Hydraulic Hose & Fitting Reference

Hose Size to Wrench Size Guide

Hose Dash # ID (inches) Thread Size Wrench Size
-4 1/4" 7/16"-20 9/16"
-6 3/8" 9/16"-18 11/16"
-8 1/2" 3/4"-16 7/8"
-10 5/8" 7/8"-14 1-1/16"
-12 3/4" 1-1/16"-12 1-1/4"
-16 1" 1-5/16"-12 1-5/8"
-20 1-1/4" 1-5/8"-12 1-7/8"
-24 1-1/2" 1-7/8"-12 2-1/4"

How to Identify Hose Size

Method 1: Calculate from ID

Formula: Inside Diameter (inches) √ó 16 = Dash Size

Example: 1/2" ID √ó 16 = #8 hose (-8)

Method 2: Check Hose Markings

  • Look for printed markings on hose
  • Will show dash size (-4, -8, -12, etc.)
  • May also show pressure rating
  • Manufacturer and date code

Method 3: Measure Thread with Caliper

  • Measure across threads of male fitting
  • Compare to chart above
  • Thread pitch also helps identify

Quick Reference:

  • Quarter-sized = -4 or -6
  • Half-dollar sized = -8 or -10
  • Silver-dollar sized = -12 or -16

Common Drill Rig Applications

System Typical Hose Size Wrench Size
Pilot/Control lines -4 (1/4") 9/16"
Auxiliary functions -6 (3/8") 11/16"
Main hydraulics -8 to -10 7/8" to 1-1/16"
Rotation motor -10 to -12 1-1/16" to 1-1/4"
Pulldown/Hoist -12 to -16 1-1/4" to 1-5/8"
Pump suction -16 to -20 1-5/8" to 1-7/8"
Tank return -20 to -24 1-7/8" to 2-1/4"

Hose Pressure Ratings

SAE 100R Series:

Type Construction Pressure (PSI)
100R1 1-wire braid 1,500-3,000
100R2 2-wire braid 2,250-5,800
100R12 4-spiral wire 4,000-5,000
100R13 4-spiral wire 5,000-6,000

Always match or exceed original pressure rating!

Using lower-rated hose can result in catastrophic failure and injury.

When to Replace Hydraulic Hose

Replace Immediately If:

  • Cover damage exposes reinforcement
  • Wire braid visible through cover
  • Bulging or soft spots (weakened)
  • Hardening or cracking of cover
  • Kinks or permanent bends
  • Fitting leaks that won't stop
  • Loose or separated fittings
  • Any seepage or weeping
  • Hose age >10 years (regardless of condition)
  • Excessive abrasion or wear

Hose Failure Modes:

Symptom Cause Prevention
Burst near fitting Improper assembly Professional crimping
Cover cracking Age, UV, ozone Replace by date
Bulging Pressure spike, damage Use correct rating
Abrasion wear Rubbing on surfaces Proper routing, guards
Kinking Bend radius too tight Follow min. radius

Hose Installation Best Practices

Routing Guidelines:

  • Avoid sharp bends (follow min. bend radius)
  • Keep away from hot surfaces
  • Avoid areas of wear/abrasion
  • Allow for movement/flexing
  • Don't twist hose (align stripe)
  • Use protective sleeves where needed
  • Secure with proper clamps (not too tight)

Minimum Bend Radius:

Hose Size Min. Bend Radius
-4 2.5"
-6 3.5"
-8 4.5"
-10 5.5"
-12 6.5"
-16 8.0"

Connection Tips:

  1. Clean threads before assembly
  2. Use proper thread sealant (not Teflon tape on hydraulics!)
  3. Torque to specification
  4. Don't over-tighten (causes damage)
  5. Check for leaks after installation
  6. Re-check after first hour of operation

Emergency Field Repairs

Field repairs are TEMPORARY only!

Replace with proper hose assembly as soon as possible.

Temporary Options:

  • Minor leak: Hydraulic hose tape (temporary)
  • Cut/damaged section: Splice with proper fittings if available
  • Blown hose: Cap both ends and re-route if possible
  • Loose fitting: Tighten properly (don't over-torque)

What NOT to Do:

  • ‚ùå Use duct tape or wire (will fail)
  • ‚ùå Use lower-pressure hose
  • ‚ùå Use garden hose or air hose
  • ‚ùå Leave leaking hose in service
  • ‚ùå Over-torque fittings to stop leak

1. Marcote 2500 Series

AVD Series - Common Maintenance Schedule:

Daily Checks (All Models):

  • Engine oil level and condition
  • Hydraulic oil level and leaks
  • Coolant level
  • Grease all fittings (see lubrication chart)
  • Check hose connections for leaks
  • Inspect chuck and swivel condition
  • Check safety systems operation
  • Clean screens and filters

Weekly Maintenance:

  • Change hydraulic return filter (if dirty)
  • Inspect drill head bearings
  • Check wireline cable condition
  • Torque check all critical bolts
  • Inspect feed cylinder condition
  • Test emergency shutdown systems

250 Hour Service:

  • Change engine oil and filter
  • Change hydraulic oil filter
  • Grease all bearings thoroughly
  • Inspect and adjust chains/belts
  • Check hydraulic cylinder seals
  • Calibrate instrumentation

1000 Hour Service:

  • Change hydraulic oil (or lab test)
  • Rebuild hydraulic cylinders if needed
  • Replace worn bearings
  • Overhaul chuck assembly
  • Complete electrical system inspection
  • Professional technician recommended

AVD Series - Operating Tips:

Maximize Rig Performance:

  • Warm-up procedure: Run at idle for 5-10 minutes before drilling, especially in cold weather
  • Hydraulic temperature: Maintain 40-60¬∞C (104-140¬∞F) for optimal performance
  • WOB application: Use gradual increases - these rigs have sensitive controls
  • RPM selection: Start low (~300 RPM) and increase based on formation
  • Monitor instrumentation: AVD rigs have gauges - use them! Watch pressure, temperature, RPM
  • Rod handling: Let automated systems do their job - don't force manual override
  • Pump pressure: Typical range 500-1,500 PSI depending on depth and core size
  • Clean operations: AVD rigs are precision equipment - keep work area clean

üî¥ AVD Series - Common Problems & Solutions:

Problem: Loss of hydraulic power

  • Check: Hydraulic oil level (most common)
  • Check: Hydraulic filter clogged
  • Check: Pump pressure relief setting
  • Check: Oil temperature (too hot reduces pressure)

Problem: Erratic rotation speed

  • Check: Control valve linkage adjustment
  • Check: Rotation motor relief valve
  • Check: Electronic control module (if equipped)
  • Check: Flow divider condition

Problem: Chuck won't grip properly

  • Check: Chuck jaws for wear (replace if needed)
  • Check: Chuck cylinder pressure
  • Check: Rod thread condition (damaged threads slip)
  • Clean: Chuck interior (mud buildup prevents grip)

Problem: Feed cylinder sticking or jerking

  • Check: Feed chains for proper tension
  • Check: Cylinder seals for wear
  • Check: Mast alignment and straightness
  • Lubricate: Feed rails and slides

Manufacturer Support:

  • Parts: Epiroc (formerly Atlas Copco) global parts network
  • Service: Factory-trained technicians available
  • Manuals: Detailed service manuals available from Epiroc
  • Training: Operator and maintenance courses offered
  • 24/7 Support: Technical hotline for critical issues

7. DrillCo Series (Various Models)

Overview: DrillCo manufactures a range of surface and underground core drilling equipment. Known for rugged, simple designs suitable for harsh conditions.

Common DrillCo Models:

  • DrillCo 1000: Light exploration rig, 300-400m capacity
  • DrillCo 2000: Mid-range surface rig, 600-800m capacity
  • DrillCo 3000: Heavy surface rig, 1,000m+ capacity
  • DrillCo U-Series: Underground-specific models
General DrillCo Characteristics:
  • Design Philosophy: Simple, robust, field-repairable
  • Power: Typically diesel hydraulic, some electric models
  • Core Sizes: Most handle AQ through HQ, some PQ capable
  • Control: Manual hydraulic controls (fewer electronics to fail)
  • Build Quality: Heavy steel construction, proven in remote areas
  • Maintenance: Designed for in-field servicing with basic tools

DrillCo Advantages:

  • ‚úÖ Extremely durable and reliable
  • ‚úÖ Easy to maintain with minimal specialized tools
  • ‚úÖ Good parts availability
  • ‚úÖ Lower cost than premium brands
  • ‚úÖ Proven performance in harsh environments
  • ‚úÖ Simple hydraulic systems are easy to troubleshoot

Common Applications:

  • Remote exploration drilling
  • Underground mine exploration
  • Third-world and developing region projects
  • Budget-conscious drilling programs
  • Areas with limited technical support available

Operating Notes:

  • Manual controls require experienced operators
  • Less automation than modern rigs (more operator skill needed)
  • Regular lubrication is critical (simple but requires discipline)
  • Hydraulic system is straightforward - easy to bleed and maintain
  • Parts are interchangeable across many DrillCo models

üí° DrillCo vs Premium Brands:

DrillCo rigs trade automation and refinement for reliability and repairability. They're the "workhorse" rigs - not fancy, but they get the job done in places where a premium rig might leave you stranded waiting for a specialized technician.

üîß Rig Setup Procedures

Pre-Setup Site Assessment:

  • Survey drill site for level ground
  • Identify overhead hazards (power lines, trees)
  • Mark underground utilities (call before you dig)
  • Assess access routes for equipment
  • Verify water source availability
  • Check ground stability for rig weight
  • Plan rod and core storage areas
  • Identify environmental sensitivities

Rig Setup Sequence:

STEP 1: POSITION RIG ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Level within 1¬∞ in all directions ‚Ä¢ Use timber mats if ground is soft ‚Ä¢ Ensure adequate clearance for rod handling ‚Ä¢ Position for efficient workflow ‚Üì STEP 2: SECURE RIG ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Anchor or stake down if required ‚Ä¢ Chock wheels/tracks if applicable ‚Ä¢ Verify stability before operations ‚Üì STEP 3: CONNECT POWER ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Diesel: Check fuel, oil, coolant levels ‚Ä¢ Electric: Verify voltage, connect safely ‚Ä¢ Hydraulic: Check reservoir level ‚Ä¢ Test all controls before drilling ‚Üì STEP 4: CONNECT WATER SYSTEM ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Run water line from source ‚Ä¢ Install pump and filters ‚Ä¢ Prime system, check for leaks ‚Ä¢ Verify adequate flow rate ‚Üì STEP 5: ALIGN MAST ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Use plumb bob or digital level ‚Ä¢ Adjust leveling jacks ‚Ä¢ Verify collar angle (usually vertical) ‚Ä¢ Lock mast position ‚Üì STEP 6: SAFETY CHECK ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Emergency stop accessible? ‚Ä¢ Fire extinguisher present? ‚Ä¢ First aid kit available? ‚Ä¢ Communication system working? ‚Ä¢ Crew briefed on hazards? ‚Üì READY TO DRILL

‚ö†Ô∏è CRITICAL SETUP SAFETY

  • NEVER operate rig on unstable or sloped ground
  • ALWAYS use proper lifting equipment - never jury-rig
  • VERIFY all hydraulic connections are tight
  • TEST emergency stop before starting work
  • CHECK for overhead power lines - maintain 10m clearance

‚öôÔ∏è Safe Operation Procedures

Pre-Start Checks (Every Shift):

  • Walk-around inspection for damage/leaks
  • Check fluid levels (hydraulic, engine, coolant)
  • Inspect hoses for wear, cracks, abrasion
  • Test all control levers and buttons
  • Verify safety equipment is present
  • Check weather forecast
  • Brief crew on daily plan and hazards
  • Document pre-start inspection

Operating Best Practices:

  • Start Slow: Begin with low RPM/WOB, gradually increase
  • Monitor Constantly: Watch gauges, listen for unusual sounds
  • Maintain Records: Log parameters, depths, problems
  • Stay Alert: Fatigue causes accidents - take breaks
  • Communicate: Use hand signals or radio clearly
  • Keep Clean: Remove trip hazards, wipe up spills immediately
  • Never Rush: Haste causes mistakes and damage

Common Operating Parameters by Rig Type:

Rig Type RPM Range (NQ) WOB Range Water Pressure
Light Skid (LF-70) 300-600 RPM 1,000-3,000 lbs 200-400 PSI
Standard Skid (2500) 250-500 RPM 2,000-5,000 lbs 300-600 PSI
Heavy Skid (3500) 200-450 RPM 3,000-8,000 lbs 400-800 PSI
Track-Mounted (MB) 200-500 RPM 3,000-10,000 lbs 400-1,000 PSI

Note: Always refer to manufacturer's specifications. Adjust for formation type and core size.

üî® Maintenance Schedules

Daily Maintenance (Every Shift):

  • Grease all fittings per manufacturer schedule
  • Check hydraulic oil level (engine off, cold)
  • Inspect hoses for damage or leaks
  • Check engine oil level
  • Inspect coolant level
  • Drain water from fuel filter (diesel rigs)
  • Check tire pressure (if applicable)
  • Clean air filter if dusty conditions
  • Tighten any loose bolts found during operation
  • Document any abnormalities or repairs needed

Weekly Maintenance:

  • Comprehensive inspection of all hoses and fittings
  • Check hydraulic filter indicator
  • Inspect drill string connections
  • Check belt tension and condition
  • Clean/service air filter
  • Inspect wire ropes (if present) for wear
  • Check structural bolts for tightness
  • Clean radiator and oil cooler fins
  • Test emergency stop and safety systems
  • Update maintenance log

Monthly/250 Hour Maintenance:

  • Change engine oil and filter
  • Change hydraulic filter
  • Lubricate all pivot points and pins
  • Inspect electrical connections
  • Check battery electrolyte level and terminals
  • Inspect hydraulic cylinder seals for leaks
  • Check control valve operation
  • Service water pump and filters
  • Comprehensive structural inspection

Seasonal/1000 Hour Maintenance:

  • Change hydraulic oil
  • Flush and replace coolant
  • Service transmission/gearbox
  • Replace hydraulic hoses (as needed or 5 year max)
  • Comprehensive inspection by certified technician
  • Update equipment manuals and parts inventory
  • Paint touch-up and corrosion protection

üìù Maintenance Documentation:

Always document:

  • Date and hour meter reading
  • Maintenance performed
  • Parts replaced (with part numbers)
  • Abnormalities found
  • Technician name
  • Future work required

Good records prevent breakdowns and maintain resale value.

üöÅ Transportation & Mobilization

Helicopter Slinging Guidelines:

Only qualified riggers should prepare loads for helicopter transport!

  • Weight Limits: Know your helicopter's capacity
    • Light helicopters (Bell 206): 500-800 lbs external
    • Medium (A-Star 350): 1,200-1,500 lbs external
    • Heavy (Bell 212): 3,000-4,000 lbs external
  • Rig Breakdown: Most skid rigs need to be broken down
    • Separate mast from base
    • Remove pumps and power unit if needed
    • Typical loads: 3-5 sling loads per rig
  • Sling Load Preparation:
    • Use proper lifting eyes/points (4-6 points minimum)
    • Bridle should be 60¬∞ angle or less
    • Load must be balanced and stable
    • Secure all loose items (nothing can fall off)
    • Bright colored marker on top for visibility
    • Tag line for load control
  • Landing Zone:
    • Minimum 30m x 30m (100 ft x 100 ft) clear area
    • No overhead wires or obstacles
    • Solid ground for landing
    • Clear approach and departure paths

Truck Transportation:

  • Use proper trailer rated for rig weight
  • Secure with chains (minimum 4 points)
  • Flag if rig extends beyond trailer
  • Check tie-downs every 50 km
  • Verify permit requirements for oversized loads
  • Remove or secure loose components
  • Protect exposed hydraulics and electronics

‚ö†Ô∏è TRANSPORTATION SAFETY

  • NEVER overload helicopter or truck
  • ALWAYS use proper rigging hardware - no shortcuts
  • CHECK all connections before lifting
  • COMMUNICATE clearly with pilot/driver
  • HAVE backup plan if weather deteriorates

üîç Common Mobile Rig Problems

Problem Possible Cause Solution
Low hydraulic power Low oil level; Clogged filter; Cold oil; Pump wear Check level; Replace filter; Warm up; Inspect pump
Rig won't rotate Hydraulic issue; Mechanical jam; Control valve problem Check hydraulics; Clear jam; Test valve
Excessive vibration Unbalanced drill string; Worn bearings; Bent rod Check string; Replace bearings; Straighten/replace rod
Water pump no pressure Air in line; Clogged filter; Pump cavitation; Worn pump Prime system; Clean filter; Check suction; Rebuild pump
Engine overheating Low coolant; Clogged radiator; Failed thermostat; Bad fan Add coolant; Clean radiator; Replace thermostat; Fix fan
Hydraulic oil overheating Low oil level; Dirty cooler; High ambient temp; System overwork Add oil; Clean cooler; Add cooler; Reduce load
Control sluggish Cold hydraulic oil; Low pressure; Worn valve Warm up; Check pressure; Replace valve
Hose failure Age; Abrasion; Heat; Exceeded pressure Replace hose; Protect from damage; Use proper rating

‚ö†Ô∏è Mobile Equipment Safety Reminders

HAZARDS SPECIFIC TO MOBILE RIGS:

  • Tipping hazard: Mobile rigs can tip if not level or on soft ground
  • Pinch points: Moving parts during transport and setup
  • Hydraulic injection: High-pressure hydraulic leaks are deadly
  • Crush hazard: During lifting operations and transport
  • Remote locations: Medical help may be hours away

PPE Requirements:

  • Hard hat (ANSI Z89.1 Type I, Class E minimum)
  • Safety glasses with side shields
  • Steel-toed boots (CSA Grade 1)
  • High-visibility vest
  • Hearing protection (when noise > 85 dBA)
  • Gloves appropriate for task
  • Protective clothing (long sleeves, long pants)

Emergency Equipment (Required):

  • Fire extinguisher (ABC type, minimum 10 lbs)
  • First aid kit (CSA Standard Z1220)
  • Communication device (satellite phone for remote)
  • Spill kit for hydraulic oil
  • Emergency shelter if remote/cold climate
  • Emergency contact list posted visibly

üöú Mobile Equipment for Diamond Drill Sites

üìã Overview of Drill Site Mobile Equipment

Remote drill sites require specialized mobile equipment to transport personnel, supplies, and drill equipment across challenging terrain in all weather conditions.

Equipment Categories:

Category Equipment Types Primary Use Terrain Rating
Tracked Carriers Maruka, Prinoth, Bombardier Heavy equipment transport ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÖ Excellent
Amphibious Argo 8x8, Sherp ATV Water crossings, soft terrain ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÖ Water+Land
ATVs ATVs, Side-by-Sides, Spider Personnel transport, light cargo ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÜ Very good
4x4 Vehicles Fat trucks, Pickup trucks Road transport, crew shuttle ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÜ‚òÜ Good trails
Heavy Equipment Bulldozers, Skidders Site prep, road building ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÖ Makes trails
Winter Vehicles Snowmobiles (Ski-Doo) Winter access, ice roads ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÖ Winter only

üöú Maruka Tracked Carrier

Japanese-made tracked carrier, workhorse of remote drill sites. Exceptional for moving heavy drill equipment through muskeg, swamps, and rough terrain. Known for reliability and rugged construction.

üìä Technical Specifications by Model:

Model Capacity Engine Weight Best Use
Maruka MW-1500 1,500 kg (3,300 lbs) 40-45 HP diesel 2,800 kg Light drill moves, supply transport
Maruka MW-2200 2,200 kg (4,850 lbs) 65-75 HP diesel 4,000 kg Standard drill setup, heavy supplies
Maruka MW-3000 3,000 kg (6,600 lbs) 110-130 HP diesel 5,800 kg Large rigs, multiple trips consolidated

‚ö†Ô∏è Safety Information:

  • Pre-operation inspection mandatory - Check tracks, hydraulics, fuel, oil before every shift
  • Maximum load capacity - NEVER exceed rated capacity, factor in uneven terrain (reduce 20%)
  • Steep grade limit - Maximum 30¬∞ angle, use extreme caution above 20¬∞, avoid side slopes over 15¬∞
  • Rollover risk - High center of gravity when loaded, avoid side slopes, turn slowly on grades
  • Pinch points - Tracks, rollers, dump mechanism - keep hands and body clear during operation
  • Vision obstructions - Large loads block forward view, always use a spotter for blind spots
  • Fire extinguisher - 10 lb ABC rated, mounted and accessible, inspect monthly
  • ROPS/FOPS - Rollover and falling object protection - stay in cab during operation
  • Communication - Radio contact mandatory when moving drill equipment, establish hand signals with spotter
  • Weather conditions - Reduce speed 50% in rain/snow, avoid lightning storms (tall loads), ice reduces traction

‚ö†Ô∏è CRITICAL SAFETY: When loading drill rods or mast sections, secure ALL loads with chains and binders. A shifting load can cause catastrophic rollover. Always load lowest/heaviest items first, distribute weight evenly side-to-side.

üîß Complete Start-Up Procedure:

  1. Walk-around inspection (5-10 minutes)
    • Check track tension - proper tension has 2-3" deflection mid-span when pressed
    • Inspect track pads - replace if rubber worn to steel, cracks >2" long, chunks missing
    • Look for hydraulic leaks - check cylinders, hoses, fittings (wipe clean, run, re-inspect)
    • Verify fuel level - refuel at 1/4 tank, don't run low (sucks in debris/water)
    • Check engine oil - dipstick level, color (black = OK, milky = water contamination)
    • Inspect hydraulic oil level and condition - should be amber, not foamy or burnt smelling
    • Check coolant level in expansion tank - top up with 50/50 mix, never remove rad cap when hot
    • Verify all guards and covers secure - hydraulic pump, engine, drive train
    • Clear debris from tracks and undercarriage - sticks, mud, wire can jam
    • Test work lights and signals - replace burned bulbs immediately
    • Check fire extinguisher - gauge in green, no visible damage, mount secure
  2. Operator position setup
    • Use three-point contact climbing aboard - two hands, one foot or two feet, one hand
    • Clean boots before entering - mud on pedals causes slip accidents
    • Adjust seat for comfortable reach to all controls - back should touch backrest
    • Adjust mirrors if equipped - eliminate blind spots as much as possible
    • Fasten seatbelt if equipped - ROPS only works if you're belted in
    • Identify all controls - throttle, steering levers, hydraulic dump, emergency stop
    • Test emergency stop - pull to verify engine kills, push back in before starting
  3. Cold weather pre-start (below -10¬∞C)
    • Block heater - should be plugged in 4+ hours (check cord not damaged)
    • Battery warmer - verify battery voltage >12.4V before starting
    • Use winter fuel blend - summer diesel gels below -20¬∞C
    • Check hydraulic oil - may need arctic hydraulic fluid below -30¬∞C
    • Have ether start assist ready - but use sparingly (damages engine)
  4. Starting sequence
    • Ensure transmission in NEUTRAL - verify shift lever position
    • Engage parking brake - lever up or button pushed
    • Turn key to ON position - wait for dash lights to illuminate
    • Wait for glow plug light on diesel models - typically 5-30 seconds depending on temperature
    • Turn key to START position - release immediately when engine catches
    • If fails to start - wait 30 seconds before retry (prevents starter burnout)
    • Let engine warm up - minimum 3 minutes, 5-10 minutes below -10¬∞C
    • Watch temperature gauge - should rise toward operating range before working
  5. System operational checks
    • Check oil pressure gauge - should read 40-60 PSI at idle, higher when revved
    • Monitor temperature gauge - normal is 180-220¬∞F, shut down if exceeds 240¬∞F
    • Check fuel gauge - verify accurate reading
    • Hydraulic pressure gauge - should read 2000-3000 PSI when system activated
    • Test hydraulic dump bed - raise 6", hold 10 seconds, lower smoothly (checks for leaks)
    • Test steering - left lever (left track), right lever (right track), both forward/reverse
    • Check brakes - parking brake should hold on slope, release fully
    • Verify all warning lights extinguished - red lights = DO NOT OPERATE
    • Test horn - must be audible 100+ feet for site safety

üí° Operating Best Practices:

  • Load distribution - Keep heavy items low and centered, use dunnage to prevent shifting
  • Slope operation - Straight up or down only, never across, keep bucket/bed low
  • Speed control - Max 5 km/h with heavy loads, 8 km/h light loads, slower on rough terrain
  • Spotter use - Mandatory for all blind spots, back-up operations, tight maneuvering
  • Radio communication - Maintain contact with drill site, report position, ETA
  • Track care - Avoid sharp rocks, stumps, wire; can tear tracks or puncture
  • Muskeg travel - Spread weight by not turning sharply, keep moving, momentum is key
  • River crossings - Walk it first, max depth varies by model (typically 2 feet), cross at angle to current
  • Winter operation - Use lower gear ratios, momentum for drifts, avoid sudden movements
  • Fuel management - Refuel at day end to prevent condensation, use fuel additives in winter

üèîÔ∏è Prinoth Tracked Vehicles (Panther, Husky)

Italian-Swiss manufacturer known for extreme terrain capability. Prinoth tracked carriers excel in deep snow, steep grades, and arctic conditions. Originally designed for ski resort grooming, adapted for mining/drilling with exceptional reliability.

üìä Common Models for Drill Sites:

Model Load Capacity Engine Power Special Features Best Application
Husky H12 1,200 kg dump
