1,800 kg on platform		 80 HP Tier 4 Diesel Hydrostatic drive, heated cab Crew transport, light drill equipment
Panther T4 2,000 kg dump
3,500 kg platform		 130 HP turbocharged Low ground pressure, 6 cyl diesel Medium drill rigs, muskeg work
Panther T6 3,000 kg dump
5,500 kg platform		 160 HP turbodiesel Wide tracks, ROPS cab, hydraulic tilt Heavy drill equipment, extreme terrain
Panther T8 4,000 kg dump
7,000 kg platform		 200 HP turbo intercooled Articulated steering, massive GVW Largest rigs, winter road building

Key Prinoth Advantage: Hydrostatic transmission allows infinitely variable speed control from 0-25 km/h without shifting. Exceptional control on steep grades and in deep snow compared to mechanical transmissions.

‚ö†Ô∏è Prinoth-Specific Safety Considerations:

  • Hydrostatic system - Never tow at speed (damages hydraulic motors), max tow speed 2 km/h in neutral
  • Steep grade capability - Can handle 45¬∞ slopes, but load must be secure and operator experienced
  • Track width - Extra-wide tracks (24-30") mean watch for tight spots, tree strikes
  • Visibility - Excellent forward visibility, poor rear - always use spotter backing up
  • Cold weather - Rated to -50¬∞C, but hydraulic oil must be arctic spec (Mobil 424 or equivalent)
  • Tilt bed operation - Hydraulic tilt allows 45¬∞ dump angle - never stand beside during dump
  • Articulation joint - On T8, pinch point during turns - keep personnel clear
  • Ground pressure - Very low (3-5 PSI) allows floating on muskeg but can bog in soft mud

üí° Prinoth Operating Tips:

  • Hydrostatic finesse - Feather the pedal for smooth control; mashing causes jerky movement
  • Hill climbing technique - Build momentum before steep sections, maintain steady speed
  • Deep snow strategy - Keep moving, wide turns, if stuck DON'T spin tracks
  • Load management - Always secure with chains - the tilt feature can shift loads
  • Fuel efficiency - Hydrostatic consumes significant fuel, expect 15-25 L/hr
  • Track life extension - Avoid sharp rocks and pavement when possible
  • Cold weather - Needs arctic hydraulic fluid below -30¬∞C (Mobil 424 or Chevron Tundra HD)
  • Maintenance priority - Hydraulic system is life blood - keep oil clean, filters changed

‚ö†Ô∏è CRITICAL PRINOTH NOTE: The hydrostatic transmission is EXTREMELY SENSITIVE to contaminated hydraulic fluid. Even small amounts of water or dirt can cause $20,000+ repair bills. Use only specified hydraulic oil, change filters religiously, and investigate any leak immediately.

üõª Fat Truck (Arctic-Terrain Modified Pickup)

Heavy-duty pickup with oversized 45-54" flotation tires, portal axles, reinforced suspension. Bridges gap between standard 4x4 and tracked vehicles. Excellent for winter roads, tundra, muskeg.

üìä Specifications:

Component Standard Pickup Fat Truck Mod Purpose
Tires 33-35" all-terrain 45-54" ultra-low pressure (8-12 PSI) Flotation, <5 PSI ground pressure
Axles Standard differential Portal axles (+16" lift) Clearance, gear reduction
Suspension Coil/leaf springs Reinforced + air bags Support 400+ lb tires
Steering Hydraulic power Full hydro with ram assist Turn 54" tires
Weight 5,500-7,000 lbs 8,000-10,000 lbs Tires add 2,000 lbs

Common Base: Ford F-350/F-450, Dodge Ram 2500/3500, Chevy Silverado 2500HD/3500HD. Diesel preferred. Pre-2007 engines avoid DEF complexity.

‚ö†Ô∏è Fat Truck Safety:

  • High center of gravity - 3-4 ft higher than stock, rollover risk on slopes >20¬∞
  • Tire pressure CRITICAL - Must run 8-12 PSI for flotation, over-inflation = rollover risk
  • Wide turning radius - Portal axles limit angle, needs more space
  • Stopping distance - Allow 2-3x normal distance, brake early
  • Speed limits - Max 40 km/h trails, 60 km/h roads
  • Tire beads - Low pressure can unseat bead in hard turns
  • Load capacity reduced - Practical payload ~1,000 lbs despite rating
  • Entry/exit - 4 feet to ground, use three-point contact

‚ö†Ô∏è TIRE PRESSURE RULE: Fat trucks MUST run low pressure on soft ground. Running 30-40 PSI on tundra/muskeg causes immediate sinking. Always verify pressure before leaving solid ground. Carry 12V compressor and gauge.

üîß Operation Guide:

  1. Pre-departure prep
    • Check tire pressure: 8-10 PSI soft ground, 10-12 PSI firm, 15 PSI road
    • Use low-pressure gauge (<20 PSI capability)
    • Inspect beads fully seated, no bulges
    • Re-torque lug nuts (portals stress lugs)
    • Check portal hub oil levels (sight glass 1/2 full)
    • Test hydraulic steering (no leaks)
    • Load recovery gear: strap, jack, tire kit, bead sealer
  2. Soft terrain technique
    • Lower to 8 PSI for maximum flotation
    • Maintain steady momentum, don't stop
    • Gentle throttle, spinning digs in
    • Wide gradual turns only
    • If sinking - STOP, back out same tracks
  3. Winter ice roads
    • Increase to 12-15 PSI for better handling
    • Max 30-40 km/h on ice
    • Use engine braking, pump brakes gently
    • Stay center of trail (edges may be thin)
    • If cracks heard - accelerate smoothly off weak section
  4. Return to hard surface
    • Re-inflate to 15 PSI before highway
    • Inspect for cuts, bead damage, debris
    • Clean mud (adds weight, vibration)
    • Check portal hubs for leaks

üîç Troubleshooting:

Problem Cause Field Solution
Tire bead unseated Low pressure + hard turn Strap around tire, inflate slowly, add bead sealer
Stuck in muskeg Lost momentum, spun tires STOP spinning, clear mud, place branches under tires, back out
Portal axle leaking Seal failure from submersion Top up 80W-90 gear oil, reduce speed, limp to base
Excessive vibration Mud buildup, tire balance Clean mud from treads, accept some vibration >60 km/h
Brakes ineffective Fade from weight, water Pump brakes, downshift, dry by applying lightly while driving

üí° Operating Wisdom:

  • Pressure is everything - Master adjustment for conditions
  • Momentum management - Plan to avoid stops, build speed before soft sections
  • Weight low - Heavy loads over front axle helps traction
  • Recovery gear mandatory - Strap, shovel, jack, sealer, compressor
  • Fuel consumption - Big tires increase use 30-50%
  • Cold prep - Diesel gels <-20¬∞C, use additives, block heater
  • Respect limits - Not amphibious, not a tractor, know when to turn back

‚ö†Ô∏è LEGAL NOTE: Fat trucks often not legal for highway due to width/tire protrusion. Check local regulations. Insurance difficult - requires off-road policy.

üåä Sherp ATV (Russian Amphibious)

Russian extreme amphibious ATV with massive 1600x600mm ultra-low pressure tires. Traverses water, 70cm vertical obstacles, 35¬∞ slopes, virtually any terrain. Tire-paddle propulsion in water.

üìä Specifications:

Feature Value Impact
Tires 1600mm x 600mm (63" x 24") Ground pressure <0.5 PSI
Tire Pressure 0.3-1.0 PSI (adjustable from cab) Water: 0.3 PSI, Land: 0.6-1.0 PSI
Engine Kubota 44 HP diesel (3 cyl) Reliable, simple maintenance
Capacity 5 people + 1,000 lbs cargo Crew shuttle, light equipment
Water Speed 6 km/h (4 mph) Tire paddles provide thrust
Land Speed 45 km/h max (28 mph) Slow but unstoppable
Fuel Capacity 60 liters (16 gallons) ~8 hours runtime
Obstacle Clearance 70cm vertical (27") Climbs logs, rocks, berms
Operating Temp -40¬∞C to +40¬∞C Arctic-rated, heated cab option

Unique Feature: Tire pressure adjustable from inside cab via on-board compressor. Change on-the-fly for terrain - 2-3 minutes to adjust.

‚ö†Ô∏è Sherp Safety:

  • Water operations - ALWAYS wear PFD (life jacket), can capsize in waves >15 km/h
  • Tire pressure critical - Must be <1.0 PSI, higher risks rollover
  • ROPS cage - Standard but go slow <10 km/h on side slopes >25¬∞
  • Blind spots - Large tires block ground view, use spotter
  • High entry - Use grab handles, three-point contact
  • Satellite communication - Remote operation = no cell service
  • CO risk - Crack windows if exhaust smell in cab
  • Fire extinguisher - 5 lb ABC inside cab, within reach
  • Weather - High winds blow over on water, lightning risk
  • Wildlife - Slow speed = bears outrun Sherp, carry spray

‚ö†Ô∏è WATER CROSSING RULES: 1) ALWAYS wear PFD. 2) Scout first. 3) Cross at narrowest/slowest current. 4) Enter/exit at shallow angle. 5) Stay upstream of hazards. 6) If floods/capsizes - EVACUATE, people first. Sherps have flooded in deep crossings.

üîß Operation Guide:

  1. Pre-operation inspection
    • Check tires for cuts (sidewalls vulnerable)
    • Verify pressure on dash (0.3-0.4 water, 0.6-1.0 land)
    • Check oil, coolant levels
    • Inspect for leaks (fuel, hydraulic, coolant)
    • Test lights (critical for visibility)
    • Emergency gear: PFDs, extinguisher, first aid, sat phone
    • Inspect ROPS cage for cracks
  2. Start-up
    • Enter using three-point contact
    • Close door firmly (latch must engage)
    • Fasten seatbelt (4-point harness if equipped)
    • Transmission in NEUTRAL
    • Key to ON, wait for glow plug light
    • Turn to START, release when engine catches
    • Warm up 3-5 minutes
  3. System checks
    • Tire pressure gauge - all four same reading
    • Test steering (skid-steer levers)
    • Test forward/reverse
    • Test brakes (stop from 10 km/h in <3m)
    • Adjust pressure if needed for terrain
  4. Land operation
    • Use 0.8-1.0 PSI for firm ground
    • Maintain momentum over obstacles
    • Approach obstacles straight-on
    • For vertical obstacles - build speed, hit square
    • Side slopes - slow (5 km/h), lean uphill
  5. Water crossing procedure
    • Scout crossing first (walk if possible)
    • Lower pressure to 0.3-0.4 PSI
    • All occupants don PFDs (mandatory)
    • Close windows and vents (seal cab)
    • Enter at shallow angle, increase depth gradually
    • Once floating - gentle throttle (tires paddle)
    • Steering slower in water (same levers)
    • Angle to current (aim upstream)
    • Exit at shallow angle
    • Stop and check for water intrusion
  6. Muskeg/swamp operation
    • Lower to 0.4-0.6 PSI (flotation mode)
    • Maintain steady speed, don't stop
    • Gentle throttle, don't spin tires
    • If sinking - stop and reverse immediately
    • Thick vegetation = firmer ground
    • Bubbling ahead = very soft, avoid
  7. Winter/ice operation
    • Increase to 0.8-1.0 PSI for traction
    • Reduce speed (Sherp slides sideways)
    • If ice cracks - accelerate smoothly off weak area
    • Avoid ice <8" thick (Sherp weighs 2,900 lbs)
    • If breakthrough - Sherp floats, exit via roof hatch
    • Carry ice picks and rope
  8. Shut-down
    • Park on level ground away from water
    • Shift to NEUTRAL, parking brake
    • Idle 2-3 minutes to cool
    • Key to OFF, remove and secure
    • Post-op inspection for leaks/damage
    • Log hours and issues
    • Refuel if <1/2 tank
    • Plug block heater if <-10¬∞C forecast

üîç Troubleshooting:

Problem Cause Solution
Won't start Dead battery, fuel, cold glow plugs Check 12V+, fuel valve on, wait longer for glow plugs
Tire losing pressure Puncture, valve leak, bead leak Use on-board compressor, find leak with soap, plug if tread
Compressor not working Blown fuse, pump failure Check 20A fuse, inspect hose connections, manual inflation needed
Loss of power Clogged filter, low tire pressure Replace fuel/air filter, increase pressure to 1.0 PSI
Steering pulls Uneven pressure, debris in tracks Equalize tire pressure both sides, clear debris
Water in cab Door seal worn, vents open Check seal, close vents, test bilge pump, bail manually
Overheating Low coolant, clogged radiator Cool and check coolant, clean radiator of mud
Can't maintain speed in water Pressure too high, current strong Lower to 0.3 PSI for paddle effect, angle to current

üí° Operating Wisdom:

  • Pressure mastery - Learn terrain, adjust proactively
  • Water confidence - Start shallow, work up gradually
  • Momentum is life - Keep moving over obstacles
  • Respect limitations - Can capsize in rough water
  • Communication survival - Sat comm mandatory remote areas
  • Spare parts - Hard to get, carry belts, fuses, hoses, filters
  • Fuel planning - 15-20 km range per tank rough terrain
  • Weather awareness - Slow speed = caught in storms
  • Environmental responsibility - Still impacts sensitive terrain
  • Training invaluable - Get experienced operator training first

‚ö†Ô∏è SHERP LIMITATIONS: 1) Top speed 45 km/h - very slow. 2) Fuel limited - 60L, uses 10-15L/hr. 3) Parts from Russia - weeks wait. 4) Not amphibious in waves >1m. 5) Unheated cab miserable <-20¬∞C. Not magic, just highly capable in right conditions.

üöô 4x4 Pickup Trucks (General Purpose)

Standard heavy-duty 4x4 pickups are workhorses for drill sites with road/trail access. Common models: Ford F-250/F-350, Chevy Silverado 2500/3500, Ram 2500/3500, Toyota Tundra, Nissan Titan XD. Diesel preferred for torque and cold weather.

üìä Typical Heavy-Duty Pickup Specs:

Feature 3/4 Ton (2500) 1 Ton (3500) Drill Site Use
Engine 6.6L diesel (400+ HP) 6.7L diesel (400-500 HP) Torque for towing, cold starts
Payload 3,000-4,000 lbs 4,000-7,000 lbs (dually) Drill rods, tools, supplies
Towing 15,000-18,000 lbs 20,000-37,000 lbs Trailers, equipment haul
4WD System Part-time or full-time Part-time or full-time Traction on trails, snow, mud
Bed Length 6.5' or 8' 8' standard 8' bed for 10' drill rods
Fuel Capacity 100-130 liters 130-170 liters Extended range remote sites

Diesel vs Gas: Diesel preferred for drill sites: better torque, fuel economy when towing, cold weather reliability with block heater, longer engine life. Pre-2007 engines avoid DEF (diesel exhaust fluid) complexity in remote areas.

‚ö†Ô∏è Pickup Truck Safety on Drill Sites:

  • Load security - Always tie down drill rods, use bed extender for overhang, red flag required
  • Weight distribution - Heavy items over axle, tongue weight 10-15% of trailer weight
  • 4WD engagement - Don't use on dry pavement (damages transfer case), engage before stuck
  • Ground clearance - Stock ~9-10", watch for rocks, stumps on rough trails
  • Approach/departure angles - Long wheelbase can high-center on steep transitions
  • Braking distance - Loaded/towing = 2-3x normal stopping distance
  • Tire pressure - Adjust for load: empty 35 PSI, loaded 50-80 PSI rear (check door sticker)
  • Trailer sway - Improper loading causes sway, deadly at highway speed
  • Backup cameras - Bed extenders and trailers create huge blind spots
  • Winter operation - Block heater mandatory <-15¬∞C, diesel gels without additives

‚ö†Ô∏è CRITICAL TOWING SAFETY: Never exceed towing capacity. Trailers need weight distribution hitch if >5,000 lbs. Tongue weight too light = trailer sway (deadly). Too heavy = poor steering/braking. Always use safety chains crossed under tongue, trailer brakes required >3,000 lbs in most jurisdictions.

üîß Pickup Operation for Drill Sites:

  1. Daily inspection
    • Walk-around - tire pressure, leaks, lights, bed security
    • Engine oil - check daily on older diesels (can consume 1 qt per 1000 km)
    • Coolant - check overflow, top up (mix 50/50 antifreeze)
    • DEF level - if 2011+ diesel, needs diesel exhaust fluid (refill at 1/4 tank)
    • Brake fluid - should be at MAX, dark = needs flush
    • Power steering fluid - check level, leak = hard steering
    • Trailer lights - test all signals before towing
    • Trailer hitch - ball correct size (2" or 2-5/16"), safety chains attached
  2. Cold weather starting (diesel)
    • Block heater - plug in 4+ hours below -10¬∞C (critical below -20¬∞C)
    • Battery - needs 12.6V+ for glow plugs, cold cranking amps important
    • Fuel additives - anti-gel additive in tank below -15¬∞C (diesel gels at -20¬∞C)
    • Glow plug cycle - wait full duration (15-60 seconds depending on temp)
    • If won't start - cycle glow plugs 2-3 times, don't crank continuously (damages starter)
    • Warm-up period - idle 5-10 minutes, don't drive hard until temp gauge up
  3. Loading drill rods safely
    • Use bed extender for rods >8 feet (most drill rods are 10')
    • Place rods over wheel wells (strongest part of bed)
    • Red flag on overhang (legal requirement, also safety)
    • Tie down - ratchet straps at 3 points minimum, prevents bouncing
    • Check tightness after 5 km - straps loosen as load settles
    • Drive slowly - 60 km/h max with overhang, 80 km/h with secured load
  4. Towing trailers safely
    • Hitch ball correct size - 2" for <10,000 lbs, 2-5/16" for heavier
    • Weight distribution hitch - mandatory for trailers >5,000 lbs
    • Tongue weight - 10-15% of trailer weight, measure with bathroom scale
    • Safety chains - cross under tongue, hooks up, clips secured
    • Trailer brakes - controller in cab, adjust gain for trailer weight
    • Mirrors - extend towing mirrors for visibility past trailer
    • Test brakes - 30 km/h stop test in safe area before highway
  5. Off-road/trail driving technique
    • Engage 4WD before you need it - once stuck, hard to engage
    • Low range (4L) for steep hills, deep mud, rock crawling
    • Tire pressure - lower 5-10 PSI for soft ground (increases footprint), air back up for highway
    • Momentum management - maintain steady speed over obstacles, don't floor it
    • Wheel placement - straddle ruts, watch approach angle (don't drag front)
    • Hill descent - low gear, engine braking, avoid riding brakes (overheat)
    • Water crossings - max depth 18-24" (varies by model), check air intake location
    • Recovery points - use frame-mounted points, never bumper (will rip off)
  6. Shut-down procedure
    • Park on level ground, transmission in PARK
    • Diesel cool-down - idle 3-5 minutes after towing/hard work (turbo protection)
    • Parking brake - engage, critical if any slope
    • Chock wheels if trailer attached (trailers can roll, push truck)
    • Disconnect trailer - lower jack, remove safety chains, pull forward slowly
    • In winter - plug block heater if below -10¬∞C forecast
    • Refuel - end of day refueling prevents condensation in tank overnight

üîç Troubleshooting:

Problem Cause Solution
Won't start (diesel, cold) Gelled fuel, weak battery, glow plugs Use fuel additive, warm battery indoors, replace glow plugs, cycle glow plugs 3x
DEF warning light DEF level low, DEF frozen, quality issue Refill DEF (blue cap), warm DEF tank (drive), use fresh DEF (expires), clear code after refill
4WD won't engage Actuator failure, low fluid, electrical Check transfer case fluid, inspect vacuum lines (older models), scan for codes, may need dealer
Loss of power towing Clogged fuel filter, turbo issue, transmission overheating Replace fuel filter every 20,000 km, check turbo boost pressure, let trans cool (downshift on hills)
Trailer sway Tongue weight wrong, weight distribution, speed CRITICAL - stop and rebalance load (60% weight ahead of axle), reduce speed to 80 km/h, use WD hitch
Brakes overheating Riding brakes downhill, trailer brakes weak Use low gear for engine braking, increase trailer brake gain, let cool before continuing
Stuck in mud/snow Wrong gear, not in 4WD, tires spinning STOP spinning (digs deeper), engage 4L, rock back/forth, place boards/rocks under tires, winch/tow out
Check engine light Emissions system (common on diesels) Scan codes with OBD2 reader, often DPF regen needed (drive highway 30 min), may need forced regen

üí° Pickup Operating Wisdom:

  • Maintenance critical - Oil changes every 5,000 km towing, fuel filters 15,000 km, transmission service 60,000 km
  • Load rating knowledge - Know your payload (door sticker), factor in passengers, fuel, gear
  • Tire management - Rotate every 10,000 km, replace at 50% tread (drill sites are hard on tires)
  • Winter prep - Block heater, battery tender, fuel additives, winter tires, recovery gear
  • Fuel planning - Towing cuts economy 30-50%, plan fuel stops on remote routes
  • Recovery gear - Tow strap (30'), shackles, shovel, traction boards, jack, spare tire
  • Communication - Satellite phone or SPOT for remote areas, CB radio for convoy travel
  • Insurance - Verify coverage for off-road use, towing, tools in bed

‚ùÑÔ∏è Snowmobiles (Ski-Doo) for Winter Drilling Access

Snowmobiles essential for winter drill site access in deep snow. Ski-Doo (Bombardier) most common brand but also Polaris, Arctic Cat, Yamaha. Utility models preferred over performance sleds. Can pull sleds with supplies, faster than tracked vehicles on snow.

üìä Snowmobile Types for Drill Sites:

Type Models Features Drill Site Use
Utility/Work Sleds Ski-Doo Expedition, Tundra Wide track, 2-up seat, cargo rack, hitch BEST - crew transport, pull supply sleds, reliable
Touring Ski-Doo Grand Touring Heated seats, windshield, comfortable GOOD - long distance crew shuttle, less cargo
Trail Sleds Ski-Doo MXZ, Renegade Narrow track, high performance OK - fast but limited cargo, hard ride
Mountain Sleds Ski-Doo Summit Long track, lightweight, deep snow SPECIALIST - steep terrain, deep powder only

Drill Site Recommendation: Utility sleds (Expedition, Tundra) are workhorses. Wide track (20") for flotation, strong chassis for towing, 2-person capacity, cargo racks. 600cc or larger engines for reliable power. 4-stroke preferred for fuel economy and quiet operation.

‚ö†Ô∏è Snowmobile Safety (CRITICAL - High Injury Risk):

  • Helmet MANDATORY - Full-face with visor, DOT approved, prevents 85% of serious injuries
  • Avalanche risk areas - Carry beacon, probe, shovel if mountain terrain, take avalanche safety course
  • Ice crossings - Minimum 4" clear ice for snowmobile, 8-10" to be safe, listen for cracks
  • Hidden obstacles - Stumps, rocks, fences under snow - deadly at speed, follow marked trails
  • Hypothermia risk - Windchill at 60 km/h is extreme, dress in layers, carry survival gear
  • Carbon monoxide - Never run in enclosed space (sheds, tents), exhaust is odorless, causes death
  • Towing limits - Max 150-200 lbs on sled, heavy loads cause handling issues, track spinning
  • Night riding - Limited visibility, reduce speed 50%, watch for wildlife, moose strikes common
  • Alcohol ZERO tolerance - Same DUI laws as cars, cold impairs judgment already
  • Buddy system - NEVER ride alone in remote areas, mechanical failure = life threatening in cold

‚ö†Ô∏è LIFE-THREATENING HAZARDS: 1) Thin ice - snowmobile breaks through, heavy machine sinks, traps rider. 2) Tree wells (deep powder near trees) - suffocation risk. 3) Avalanches - bury rider in seconds. 4) Hypothermia - windchill can be -50¬∞C+, frostbite in minutes. 5) High speed crashes - trees, rocks, other sleds. Snowmobiling has high fatality rate - take seriously.

üîß Snowmobile Operation Guide:

  1. Pre-ride inspection (EVERY TIME)
    • Fuel level - fill tank, carry extra in sled (2-stroke consumes 15-20 L/hr)
    • Oil level - 2-stroke needs premix or oil tank full, 4-stroke check dipstick
    • Coolant - check overflow, should be at full mark
    • Track tension - 1" deflection under track center, too loose = comes off
    • Track condition - look for tears, missing lugs, damage (track failure strands you)
    • Skis - carbides sharp for steering, check ski bolts tight
    • Lights - headlight, taillight critical for visibility/safety
    • Kill switch tether - MUST be attached to you (stops sled if you fall off)
    • Emergency gear - survival kit, first aid, tools, spare belt, spark plugs
  2. Cold weather starting
    • Choke on for cold start (carbureted models)
    • Key to ON, pull kill switch to RUN position
    • Primer button - 3-5 pumps (if equipped)
    • Pull recoil starter (if manual) or press electric start
    • Let warm up 3-5 minutes before riding
    • If flooded - choke off, throttle wide open, try starting
    • Choke off once engine warms (engine will bog if left on)
  3. Safe riding technique
    • Seated riding - most control, weight centered, feet on running boards
    • Standing - deep snow, rough terrain, bumps (absorb with knees)
    • Cornering - shift weight to inside, stand on inside running board, counterlean
    • Braking - squeeze brake lever progressively, don't grab (will skid), use engine braking too
    • Uphill - build momentum before hill, stand and lean forward, throttle steady
    • Downhill - sit back, low gear for engine braking, light brake pressure
    • Side hills - stand, lean uphill, ready to jump off uphill side if sliding down
    • Deep powder - keep speed up (momentum), stand, weight forward, smooth throttle
  4. Ice crossing procedure
    • Scout first - look for cracks, dark spots (thin ice), measure thickness with auger
    • Minimum 4" clear ice, prefer 8-10" for safety margin
    • Cross at steady speed (30-40 km/h), don't stop mid-lake
    • If ice cracks - ACCELERATE straight ahead, don't turn (increases weight concentration)
    • If breakthrough - UNSTRAP and jump off immediately, don't fight sled (it will sink)
    • Self-rescue - ice picks to pull out, roll away from hole, crawl don't walk
  5. Towing supply sleds
    • Use proper hitch - ball hitch or clevises, not makeshift
    • Rigid tongue (not rope) - prevents overrunning on downhills
    • Load limit - 150 lbs for short distances, 100 lbs for long hauls
    • Weight distribution - heavy items low and forward in sled
    • Speed reduction - 30-40 km/h with sled vs 60 km/h solo
    • Extra stopping distance - sled pushes on stops, brake early
  6. Shut-down and securing
    • Let idle 30 seconds to cool
    • Turn key OFF
    • Attach kill switch tether to handlebar (prevents blowing away)
    • Tip on side if wind forecast (prevents snow burial/drift)
    • Cover with tarp if parking overnight (easier starting)
    • Remove battery if extended storage <-30¬∞C (bring inside)

üîç Snowmobile Troubleshooting:

Problem Cause Field Solution
Won't start Flooded, old fuel, plugs fouled, kill switch Check kill switch, wait 5 min, try with choke off + throttle open, clean/replace spark plugs
Drive belt broken Wear, overheating, shock load Carry spare belt (critical), replace (15 min job), inspect clutches for damage
Track came off Too loose, hit obstacle, worn Tip sled on side, align track on drivers, tighten adjusters, if damaged - tow out
Overheating No snow ingestion, clogged coolers STOP immediately, let cool, ride in snow (not on ice/trail), clean heat exchanger fins
Engine bogging/won't rev Choke stuck on, carb icing, clutch issue Turn choke off, use carb heat if equipped, check clutch not frozen (warm it)
No power/won't move Clutch frozen, belt issue, drive failure Warm clutch with hand warmth, check belt not shredded, if chaincase failed - tow out
Steering locked/hard to turn Ice buildup, frozen ski pivots Tip sled, break ice off skis and steering components, warm pivots
Track spinning, no traction Icy surface, worn track, too light Add weight over track (passenger, cargo), use studs (if legal), find snow

üí° Snowmobile Operating Wisdom:

  • Dress in layers - Base layer, insulation, windproof outer, windchill is extreme at speed
  • Carry survival gear - Fire starter, emergency blanket, high-energy food, headlamp
  • Fuel planning - 2-stroke uses 15-20 L/hr, 4-stroke 10-12 L/hr, range ~150-200 km
  • Spare parts critical - Belt, spark plugs, tools, duct tape, zip ties
  • GPS essential - Whiteout conditions = zero visibility, easy to get lost
  • Wildlife awareness - Moose, caribou on trails at dawn/dusk, deadly collisions
  • Group travel mandatory - Never ride solo remote areas, breakdown in cold = survival situation
  • Communication - Satellite phone, radios, tell someone your route and return time
  • Ice thickness changes - Springs, current, dark ice (thinner) vs white (safer)
  • Maintenance schedule - Service every 50 hours or season start, reliable machine = survival

‚ö†Ô∏è REMEMBER: Snowmobiling is one of the most dangerous forms of transportation. Combine high speed (60+ km/h) with extreme cold (-30¬∞C to -50¬∞C windchill), remote locations (no help available), and hidden hazards (thin ice, obstacles, avalanches). ALWAYS wear helmet, ride with buddy, carry survival gear, tell someone your plans. A breakdown 50 km from camp in -40¬∞C is a life-threatening emergency.

üöú Bulldozers for Site Preparation

Essential for drill site access - build roads, clear pads, move equipment. Common sizes: D3-D5 (small), D6-D7 (medium), D8-D9 (large). Caterpillar most common but also Komatsu, John Deere. Small dozers (D4/D5) most practical for drill sites.

üìä Dozer Sizes for Drill Sites:

Size Class Models Operating Weight Blade Width Best Use
Mini Dozer Cat D3, Komatsu D39 8,000-12,000 lbs 7-8 feet Tight trails, finishing work
Small Dozer Cat D4, D5, Komatsu D51 18,000-35,000 lbs 10-12 feet BEST for drill sites - versatile
Medium Dozer Cat D6, D7, Komatsu D65 40,000-60,000 lbs 12-14 feet Main road building, big pads
Large Dozer Cat D8, D9, Komatsu D85 80,000-110,000 lbs 14-16 feet Major projects only (overkill usually)

Drill Site Recommendation: Cat D5 or equivalent (Komatsu D51, Deere 700K) is sweet spot. Big enough for road building, small enough to maneuver, good fuel economy (~8-12 L/hr), can transport on single trailer. Ideal for 2-4 km of trail building.

‚ö†Ô∏è Bulldozer Safety (Heavy Equipment - High Risk):

  • Rollover protection - ROPS/FOPS cage mandatory, stay in cab during operation, seatbelt required
  • Slope limits - Max 45¬∞ straight up/down, 30¬∞ across slope, can tip sideways easily
  • Blind spots HUGE - Cannot see behind, limited sides, ALWAYS use spotter/signaller
  • Crush hazard - Massive weight (20+ tons), crushes vehicles/structures, keep personnel clear
  • Blade dropping - Hydraulic failure drops blade instantly, never work under raised blade
  • Track tension - Tracks under extreme pressure, can snap and whip (fatal), inspect daily
  • Noise and vibration - Hearing protection mandatory, whole-body vibration causes fatigue
  • Limited speed - Max 6-10 km/h, takes forever to stop, plan ahead
  • Underground hazards - Can't see buried rocks, stumps, old equipment - will damage undercarriage
  • Fire risk - Hydraulic oil under pressure = fire if rupture near hot engine, 20 lb extinguisher required

‚ö†Ô∏è FATAL HAZARD - ROLLOVERS: Dozers roll sideways on slopes, crushing operator. Rules: 1) NEVER exceed 30¬∞ side slope. 2) Work straight up/down slopes when possible. 3) Lower blade when traversing slopes (acts as outrigger). 4) If tipping felt - turn uphill immediately, drop blade. 5) NEVER jump off - ROPS protects IF you stay in cab with belt on.

üîß Dozer Operation Basics:

  1. Pre-operation inspection
    • Walk-around - look for leaks, damage, track condition
    • Track tension - should have 1-2" sag at midpoint
    • Track pads/grouser bars - worn smooth = poor traction, replace at 50%
    • Hydraulic cylinders - check for leaks, scoring on rods
    • Engine oil, coolant, hydraulic fluid - check all levels
    • Fuel level - dozers burn 8-15 L/hr depending on size
    • Blade cutting edge - should be sharp, replace if rounded
    • ROPS/FOPS - inspect for cracks (critical safety)
    • Fire extinguisher - 20 lb ABC, mounted and charged
  2. Starting procedure
    • Climb aboard using three-point contact and handholds
    • Adjust seat and mirrors
    • FASTEN SEATBELT (non-negotiable with ROPS)
    • Ensure all controls in neutral (blade, ripper)
    • Turn key to ON, wait for glow plug cycle (diesel)
    • Turn to START, release when engine catches
    • Warm up 5-10 minutes (critical for hydraulics)
    • Check gauges - oil pressure, temp, hydraulic pressure
  3. Basic dozing technique
    • Blade angle - tilt for sidecast, straight for max push
    • Cutting depth - 6-12" passes, more efficient than deep cuts
    • Blade down during travel - lowers center of gravity, safer on slopes
    • Use low gear for pushing, higher gear for travel (if multi-speed)
    • Straight passes - overlap by 1/3 blade width
    • Slot dozing - cut paths before full-width pushes (saves fuel)
  4. Road building technique
    • Clear trees/stumps first - blade or ripper
    • Remove organic soil (muskeg) to mineral soil
    • Build roadbed 1-2' above grade (drainage)
    • Crown center of road - 2-4% slope to sides
    • Cut ditches on sides for water runoff
    • Compact by running over multiple times
    • Width minimum 12' for drill equipment passage
  5. Slope work safety
    • Inspect slope before committing - hidden drop-offs, soft spots
    • Straight up or down ONLY, never diagonal
    • Blade down going uphill (dig in if sliding)
    • Blade slightly raised going downhill (drag reduces speed)
    • Low gear, steady speed, don't stop mid-slope if avoidable
    • If sliding sideways - turn uphill, drop blade, stop and back out
  6. Shut-down procedure
    • Park on level ground
    • Lower blade fully to ground
    • Lower ripper if equipped
    • Idle 5 minutes to cool (hydraulics and turbo)
    • Shut off engine, remove key
    • Engage parking brake/lock controls
    • Log hours and report any issues

üîç Dozer Troubleshooting:

Problem Cause Solution
Blade won't lift Low hydraulic fluid, pump failure, cylinder leak Check hydraulic level, listen for pump running, inspect cylinders for leaks, may need mechanic
Tracks throwing/loose Tension too loose, damaged track link Adjust track tension (grease fittings), inspect for broken links, replace damaged sections
No power/won't push Engine issue, transmission problem Check air filter, fuel filters, may be clutch or transmission - needs mechanic
Overheating Clogged radiator, low coolant, fan belt Clean radiator (reverse flush), check coolant level when cold, inspect fan belt tension
Steering sluggish Clutch/brake adjustment, hydraulic issue Older dozers - adjust clutches/brakes, newer dozers - hydraulic system issue, check fluid/filters

üí° Dozer Operating Wisdom:

  • Fuel economy - Push downhill when possible, coast with blade up, avoid spinning tracks
  • Undercarriage life - Avoid rocks/stumps, turn gradually, clean mud/debris daily
  • Blade maintenance - Flip/replace cutting edge at 50% wear, saves fuel
  • Weather planning - Work in frozen ground when possible (easier), rain makes mud
  • Environmental care - Minimize disturbance, avoid wetlands, stockpile topsoil for reclamation

üèéÔ∏è Side-by-Sides (UTVs - Utility Terrain Vehicles)

Side-by-sides (SxS/UTV) are enclosed cab ATVs with automotive-style seating, steering wheel, roll cage. Superior to ATVs for crew transport - safer, more comfortable, better cargo. Common brands: Polaris Ranger, Can-Am Defender, Honda Pioneer, Kubota RTV, Yamaha Viking.

üìä Side-by-Side Classes:

Class Models Seats Bed Capacity Best For
Utility Work Polaris Ranger, Can-Am Defender 2-3 1,000-1,500 lbs BEST for drill sites - tough, reliable
Crew/Passenger Ranger Crew, Defender MAX 4-6 600-1,000 lbs Crew shuttle, less cargo
Sport Performance RZR, Maverick X3 2-4 300-500 lbs Recreation only - avoid for work
Diesel Utility Kubota RTV, Polaris Diesel 2-3 1,000-2,000 lbs Heavy work, reliable, great torque

Drill Site Recommendation: Polaris Ranger 1000 or Can-Am Defender HD10 for gas, Kubota RTV-X1140 for diesel. Features needed: 4WD, locking differential, dump bed, full cab with heat/AC, 1,000+ lb capacity, towing 2,000+ lbs. Diesel better for heavy use and cold weather.

‚ö†Ô∏è Side-by-Side Safety:

  • SEATBELTS MANDATORY - Roll cage only protects if belted, ejection = fatal
  • Rollover risk - High center of gravity, can tip on slopes >25¬∞, side hills dangerous
  • Doors and nets - Keep closed/secured, prevents ejection in rollover
  • Speed control - Top speed 50-80 km/h but trail speed should be 20-40 km/h max
  • Load security - Tie down all loads, shifting load causes handling issues
  • Helmet debate - Some sites require helmets in SxS, check site rules
  • Pinch points - Dump bed hydraulics, steering linkage, keep hands clear
  • Towing limits - Don't exceed rated capacity, use proper hitch, safety chains
  • Steep hills - Can flip backwards on steep climbs, can flip forward on steep descents
  • Water crossings - Most handle 18-24" water, check air intake location, cross slowly

‚ö†Ô∏è ROLLOVER SURVIVAL: If rollover occurs: 1) Keep hands/arms INSIDE cab (don't reach out). 2) Stay belted in (let ROPS protect you). 3) Brace for impact (hands on wheel, feet braced). 4) After stop - turn off key, check for injuries, exit uphill side. Common mistake: unbuckling during roll = ejection and crush injury.

üîß Side-by-Side Operation:

  1. Pre-ride checks
    • Tire pressure - typically 12-15 PSI, check all four
    • Fluid levels - oil, coolant, brake fluid, windshield washer
    • Lights - headlights, brake lights, turn signals (if equipped)
    • Brakes - test foot brake and parking brake
    • Cargo bed - inspect for cracks, test dump hydraulics
    • Seatbelts - all belts functional, buckles not damaged
    • Doors/nets - properly secured
  2. Starting and system checks
    • Enter vehicle, close door securely
    • FASTEN SEATBELT - everyone in vehicle
    • Adjust seat and mirrors
    • Brake pedal pressed, transmission in PARK
    • Turn key or press start button
    • Check dash for warning lights (should go out)
    • Test brakes before moving - foot brake should be firm
    • Select 2WD or 4WD based on terrain (4WD for off-road)
    • Engage differential lock if conditions require (mud, snow)
  3. Driving technique
    • Smooth inputs - gradual acceleration, braking, steering
    • Speed control - match terrain (20 km/h rough, 40 km/h trails, 60 km/h max roads)
    • Hill climbing - approach straight, momentum helps, don't stop mid-hill
    • Hill descending - low gear for engine braking, steady brake pressure
    • Side slopes - AVOID if possible, if unavoidable go slow, downhill wheels near edge
    • Obstacles - straddle small obstacles, drive over at angle (not straight on)
    • Ruts - let one side ride out of rut for steering control
    • Mud - momentum helps, don't stop, 4WD and differential lock
  4. Cargo and towing
    • Load limit - 1,000-1,500 lbs depending on model, don't exceed
    • Weight low - heavy items on bed floor, reduces rollover risk
    • Secure loads - ratchet straps, bungees not sufficient for heavy items
    • Dump bed operation - clear area behind, stand aside when dumping
    • Towing capacity - typically 1,500-2,500 lbs, use proper ball hitch
    • Trailer brakes - required for trailers >1,500 lbs in most areas

üîç Troubleshooting:

Problem Cause Solution
Won't start Safety interlock, dead battery Press brake fully, ensure in PARK, check battery voltage, jump start if needed
4WD not engaging Actuator issue, switch failure Stop and shift to 4WD again, try rocking back/forth, may need dealer service
Overheating Mud clogging radiator, low coolant Stop and let cool, clean radiator with water, check coolant level, inspect fan operation
Loss of power Clogged air filter, fuel filter, belt wear Clean/replace air filter, change fuel filter, inspect CVT belt for wear/glazing
Brakes spongy/weak Air in lines, low fluid, worn pads Check fluid level, bleed brakes if air present, inspect pads (replace <25% material)
Dump bed won't lift Hydraulic leak, pump failure, overload Check for leaks at cylinder, reduce load, may need hydraulic service
Stuck in gear Clutch not fully engaging, linkage issue Rock vehicle back and forth while shifting, check transmission fluid level

üí° Side-by-Side Wisdom:

  • Seatbelts save lives - 80% of UTV deaths involve ejection, wear belt always
  • Maintenance schedule - Oil every 100 hours, air filter every 50 hours (dusty = more often)
  • CVT belt life - 1,000-3,000 km depending on use, carry spare
  • Tire wear - Aggressive driving wears tires fast, rotate every 500 km
  • Winch usefulness - 3,000-4,500 lb winch is excellent recovery tool, worth adding
  • Cab comfort - Heater essential for cold, AC nice for summer dust, full windshield reduces fatigue
  • Fuel economy - 15-25 L/100km depending on terrain and driving style

üï∑Ô∏è Spider Vehicles (Steep Slope Specialists)

Specialized machines for extreme steep slopes and rough terrain. Hydraulic articulating legs instead of wheels/tracks. Can navigate 45-55¬∞ slopes, step over obstacles, operate on irregular surfaces. Brands: Prinoth Spider, Menzi Muck. Rare but valuable for mountainous drill sites.

üìä Spider Capabilities:

Feature Capability Advantage
Slope Capability 45-55¬∞ sustained Works where wheeled/tracked can't
Leg Articulation Independent hydraulic control Maintains level platform on uneven ground
Ground Pressure Variable by leg position Can concentrate or distribute weight
Step Height Up to 1 meter vertical Climbs over large obstacles, logs, rocks
Payload 500-2,000 kg Adequate for light drill equipment, personnel

Spider Use Case: Spiders excel where terrain is too steep or irregular for conventional equipment. Alpine drill sites, mountainsides, steep ravines. Slow (3-5 km/h) but unstoppable. Expensive to buy/rent ($200K-$500K+) so only justified for challenging sites. Operator training essential - complex controls.

‚ö†Ô∏è Spider Safety:

  • Specialized training required - Not intuitive, hydraulic leg control complex, get trained
  • Tip over risk - Despite stability, can tip if legs positioned wrong, especially with load
  • Leg pinch points - Hydraulic legs have huge crushing force, keep personnel clear
  • Limited speed - Max 5 km/h, painfully slow for long distances
  • Hydraulic failure - Loss of hydraulics = machine stuck, no movement possible
  • Visibility issues - Legs obstruct view of ground, use spotter
  • Weather sensitivity - Heavy rain/ice on slopes very dangerous, wait for better conditions

üí° Spider Operating Notes:

  • Plan movements carefully - Slow machine, think ahead, minimize repositioning
  • Leg sequencing - Front legs stabilize, rear legs step, alternate pattern
  • Load management - Keep loads low, centered, secure - affects stability
  • Fuel use - Hydraulics consume significant power, 10-20 L/hr
  • Maintenance intensive - Hydraulic system complex, regular service critical
  • Specialized parts - Long lead times for parts, expensive

‚ö†Ô∏è REALITY CHECK: Spiders are highly specialized and expensive. Only economical for specific challenging sites where no other access possible. Not general-purpose machines. Require skilled operator. Consider alternative access methods (helicopter, cable systems) before committing to Spider.

üõ∂ Argo 8x8 Amphibious ATV

Canadian-made 8-wheeled amphibious vehicle. Unique capability for water, muskeg, deep snow, soft terrain. Low pressure tires (1-2 PSI) provide flotation. Models: Argo Aurora, Frontier, Avenger. Popular for exploration drilling in wetland-heavy areas (Canadian Shield, muskeg regions).

üìä Argo Specifications:

Feature Specification Impact
Configuration 8 wheels, all-wheel drive, chain driven Excellent traction, even with wheels spinning
Engine 24-30 HP V-twin gas (Kohler/Briggs) Simple, reliable, easy maintenance
Tire Pressure 0.5-2.0 PSI Ultra-low pressure = floats on water/muskeg
Capacity 6-8 people OR 600 kg cargo Crew shuttle or light cargo, not both
Water Speed 3-4 km/h (paddling action) Slow in water but functional
Land Speed 25-35 km/h max Slow but adequate for short distances
Ground Pressure <1 PSI Won't sink in muskeg, snow, mud

Unique Argo Feature: Steered by braking one side (skid-steer like dozer). No steering wheel - steering levers or pedals. Water propulsion comes from tire paddles (small lugs on tires). Can tow small sled/trailer. Extremely simple mechanically - easy field repairs.

‚ö†Ô∏è Argo Safety:

  • Water operations - ALWAYS wear PFD (life jacket), Argo floats but can swamp in waves
  • Rollover risk - Can tip on steep side slopes (narrow width), low speed reduces risk
  • No doors/roof - Open cab, exposure to elements, wear appropriate gear
  • Steering learning curve - Skid-steer takes practice, easy to oversteer
  • Speed limitations - Very slow (25 km/h), not for long-distance travel
  • Tire damage - Low pressure means sidewalls vulnerable to puncture
  • Water currents - Slow water speed, strong currents sweep Argo downstream
  • Cold weather - No cab = extreme exposure, dress in full winter gear

‚ö†Ô∏è WATER CROSSING SAFETY: 1) ALWAYS wear PFD in/near water. 2) Scout crossing - check current, depth, hazards. 3) Enter/exit at gentle slopes (Argo struggles with steep banks). 4) Cross at narrowest point with slowest current. 5) Stay upstream of waterfalls, log jams, rapids. 6) If swamping - evacuate immediately, people before machine.

üîß Argo Operation:

  1. Pre-operation checks
    • Tire pressure - all 8 tires should be equal (typically 1-1.5 PSI)
    • Check for punctures, cuts in tire sidewalls
    • Engine oil and fuel levels
    • Chain tension - should have 1/2" play
    • Inspect chains for damage, wear
    • Check bilge plug installed (prevents water entry when on land)
    • Emergency gear - PFDs, paddles, rope, first aid
  2. Starting and basic operation
    • Start engine (recoil or electric start)
    • Steering by levers - pull left to turn left, right to turn right
    • Forward/reverse transmission - simple shift pattern
    • Smooth inputs - jerky movements stress chains
    • Slow speed - 15-20 km/h on trails, 5-10 km/h rough terrain
  3. Land operation
    • Higher tire pressure (1.5-2.0 PSI) for firmer ground
    • Maintain momentum over obstacles
    • Can climb over logs, rocks up to 18" with speed
    • Steer gently - aggressive turns strain chains
  4. Water entry and crossing
    • Lower tire pressure to 0.5-1.0 PSI for maximum buoyancy
    • Remove bilge plug before entering water
    • All occupants don PFDs (mandatory)
    • Enter water at gradual angle (not steep drop)
    • Once floating - tires provide slow forward propulsion
    • Steering same as land but slower response
    • Cross perpendicular to current (angle upstream)
    • Exit at gradual bank
    • After exiting - install bilge plug, pump out water
  5. Muskeg/swamp operation
    • Use low tire pressure (0.5-1.0 PSI)
    • Keep moving steadily, don't stop
    • Gentle throttle, avoid wheel spin
    • If sinking - reverse out immediately on same path

üîç Troubleshooting:

Problem Cause Solution
Won't move Chain broken, transmission issue Inspect chains (carry spare master links), check transmission fluid, may need tow
Tire losing pressure Puncture, bead leak, valve Find leak with soapy water, plug if in tread, reseat bead, carry pump and patches
Poor steering response Brake adjustment, chain tension Adjust steering brakes, tighten chains if loose, lubricate brake linkage
Engine overheating Low oil, debris on cooling fins Check oil level, clean cooling fins, ensure fan working
Sinking in water Bilge plug out, hull crack, overloaded Install bilge plug, inspect hull for damage, reduce load, pump out water
Chain noise/vibration Loose chain, worn sprocket Adjust chain tension, lubricate chains, inspect sprockets for wear

üí° Argo Operating Wisdom:

  • Tire pressure is key - Lower for soft terrain/water, higher for trails, carry pump and gauge
  • Chain maintenance critical - Lubricate chains weekly, adjust tension, carry spare links
  • Slow and steady - Argo is slow but capable, patience is virtue
  • Water confidence builds - Start with shallow crossings, learn gradually
  • Load management - 6 people OR cargo, not both (overloading sinks in water)
  • Emergency paddle - Carry paddle in case engine fails in water
  • Spare parts - Chain master links, tire patches, bilge pump, spark plug
  • Not for long distance - Max 25 km/h, uncomfortable, use for site access only

üöë First Aid & CPR - Emergency Response

‚ö†Ô∏è EMERGENCY - WHEN TO CALL 911

Call 911 IMMEDIATELY for:

  • Unconsciousness or unresponsiveness
  • Difficulty breathing or no breathing
  • Chest pain or pressure
  • Severe bleeding
  • Head, neck, or back injury
  • Severe burns
  • Suspected broken bones
  • Poisoning or overdose
  • Seizures
  • Severe allergic reactions

For remote/drilling sites: Use satellite phone, radio, or emergency beacon. Know your GPS coordinates!

About This Section

This section covers basic first aid and CPR techniques based on St. John Ambulance and similar accredited programs. This is for reference only and does not replace proper certification training.

Get certified: Take a hands-on course from St. John Ambulance, Red Cross, or other accredited provider. Certification typically requires:

  • Standard First Aid: 2-day course (14-16 hours)
  • CPR Level C: Included in Standard First Aid
  • Recertification: Every 3 years

üîç Primary Survey - DR ABC

The foundation of all emergency response. Check in this order:

D - DANGER ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Check scene safety first ‚Ä¢ Is it safe for you to approach? ‚Ä¢ Hazards: Traffic, fire, chemicals, unstable ground ‚Ä¢ NEVER put yourself at risk ‚Ä¢ Make scene safe if possible ‚Üì R - RESPONSE ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Is the person responsive? ‚Ä¢ Tap shoulders firmly ‚Ä¢ Shout: "Are you okay?" ‚Ä¢ Check for movement or sound ‚Üì IF RESPONSIVE ‚Üí Secondary Survey IF UNRESPONSIVE ‚Üí Call 911 and continue ‚Üì A - AIRWAY ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Is airway open and clear? ‚Ä¢ Head-tilt, chin-lift to open airway ‚Ä¢ Look in mouth for obstructions ‚Ä¢ Remove visible objects if safe ‚Ä¢ DO NOT use finger sweep if nothing visible ‚Üì B - BREATHING ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Is person breathing normally? ‚Ä¢ LOOK for chest rise ‚Ä¢ LISTEN for breath sounds ‚Ä¢ FEEL for breath on your cheek ‚Ä¢ Check for 10 seconds maximum ‚Üì IF BREATHING ‚Üí Recovery position IF NOT BREATHING ‚Üí Begin CPR ‚Üì C - CIRCULATION ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Check for severe bleeding ‚Ä¢ Look for signs of shock ‚Ä¢ Treat life-threatening bleeding immediately

‚ö†Ô∏è CRITICAL: If person is UNRESPONSIVE and NOT BREATHING normally:

  1. CALL 911 IMMEDIATELY (or send someone to call)
  2. BEGIN CPR - Don't wait, don't delay
  3. GET AED if available (send someone to get it)
  4. CONTINUE until help arrives or person starts breathing

üíì CPR - Adult (Over 8 years old)

ADULT CPR SEQUENCE STEP 1: SCENE SAFETY & RESPONSIVENESS ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Ensure scene is safe ‚Ä¢ Tap shoulders, shout "Are you okay?" ‚Ä¢ No response ‚Üí Send someone to call 911 and get AED ‚Üì STEP 2: CHECK BREATHING ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Open airway (head-tilt, chin-lift) ‚Ä¢ Look, listen, feel for breathing (max 10 seconds) ‚Ä¢ Not breathing or only gasping ‚Üí Start CPR ‚Üì STEP 3: HAND POSITION ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Kneel beside person ‚Ä¢ Place heel of one hand on center of chest ‚Ä¢ Place other hand on top, interlock fingers ‚Ä¢ Position: Lower half of breastbone (sternum) ‚Ä¢ Keep arms straight, shoulders over hands ‚Üì STEP 4: CHEST COMPRESSIONS ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Compress straight down 5-6 cm (2-2.4 inches) ‚Ä¢ Rate: 100-120 compressions per minute ‚Ä¢ Let chest fully recoil between compressions ‚Ä¢ COUNT: "1, 2, 3... 30" ‚Ä¢ Give 30 compressions ‚Üì STEP 5: RESCUE BREATHS (if trained) ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Open airway (head-tilt, chin-lift) ‚Ä¢ Pinch nose closed ‚Ä¢ Make complete seal over person's mouth ‚Ä¢ Give 1 breath over 1 second ‚Ä¢ Watch for chest rise ‚Ä¢ Give second breath ‚Ä¢ Each breath should make chest rise visibly ‚Üì STEP 6: CONTINUE CYCLE ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ 30 compressions : 2 breaths ‚Ä¢ Continue cycles without stopping ‚Ä¢ Switch rescuers every 2 minutes if possible ‚Ä¢ Continue until: - Person starts breathing normally - AED arrives and is ready - EMS takes over - You are too exhausted to continue

üìè CPR Quality Checklist:

  • Depth: At least 5 cm, not more than 6 cm (2-2.4 inches)
  • Rate: 100-120 compressions per minute (beat of "Stayin' Alive")
  • Recoil: Let chest return to normal position between compressions
  • Minimize interruptions: No more than 10 seconds between cycles
  • Hand position: Center of chest, lower half of breastbone

Hands-Only CPR (If not trained in rescue breaths):

For untrained rescuers or if you're unwilling to give breaths:

  1. Call 911
  2. Push hard and fast in center of chest
  3. 100-120 compressions per minute
  4. Don't stop until help arrives

Hands-only CPR is better than no CPR!

üíì CPR - Child (1-8 years old)

Similar to adult CPR with these modifications:

Component Child CPR Difference from Adult
Compression depth 5 cm (2 inches) Slightly less depth
Hand technique 1 or 2 hands May use 1 hand for small child
Compression rate 100-120/minute Same as adult
Ratio 30:2 (one rescuer)
15:2 (two rescuers)		 15:2 if two trained rescuers
Rescue breaths Smaller breaths, gentle Just enough to make chest rise

Important for Children:

  • If you're alone: Do 5 cycles of CPR (2 minutes) BEFORE calling 911
  • If possible, take child with you to call
  • Use less force - ribs are more flexible
  • Most common cause: Airway obstruction - check for foreign objects

‚ö° Using an AED (Automated External Defibrillator)

An AED can save a life in cardiac arrest. Use it as soon as available.

AED USAGE STEPS STEP 1: TURN ON AED ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Open AED case ‚Ä¢ Turn on power (follow voice prompts) ‚Ä¢ Continue CPR while AED is being set up ‚Üì STEP 2: EXPOSE AND PREPARE CHEST ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Remove clothing from chest ‚Ä¢ Wipe chest dry if wet ‚Ä¢ Remove medication patches if present ‚Ä¢ Shave chest hair only if necessary ‚Üì STEP 3: ATTACH PADS ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Peel backing off pads ‚Ä¢ Place pads as shown on diagram: - One pad: Upper right chest (below collarbone) - Second pad: Lower left side (below armpit) ‚Ä¢ Press firmly to ensure good contact ‚Ä¢ Plug in connector if not pre-connected ‚Üì STEP 4: ANALYZE HEART RHYTHM ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ AED will say "Analyzing, stand clear" ‚Ä¢ STOP CPR ‚Ä¢ Make sure nobody touches patient ‚Ä¢ AED analyzes heart rhythm automatically ‚Üì STEP 5: FOLLOW AED PROMPTS ‚Üì IF SHOCK ADVISED: - AED will say "Shock advised, stand clear" - Make sure nobody touches patient - AED will say "Deliver shock now" or shock automatically - Press shock button if required - IMMEDIATELY resume CPR after shock IF NO SHOCK ADVISED: - AED will say "No shock advised" - IMMEDIATELY resume CPR - Continue for 2 minutes ‚Üì STEP 6: CONTINUE CARE ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Follow AED voice prompts ‚Ä¢ Continue CPR when instructed ‚Ä¢ Allow AED to reanalyze every 2 minutes ‚Ä¢ Do not remove pads ‚Ä¢ Continue until EMS arrives

‚úì AED Safety:

  • Safe to use: AED will only shock if needed
  • Clear area: Make sure nobody touches patient during shock
  • Water: Move person away from puddles if possible
  • Metal surfaces: Safe to use on metal surfaces
  • Children: Use pediatric pads/mode if under 8 years (if available)

ü´Å Choking - Airway Obstruction

Recognize Choking:

Universal choking sign: Hands clutching throat

Mild Obstruction Severe Obstruction
  • Can cough forcefully
  • Can speak or cry
  • Can breathe
  • Cannot cough effectively
  • Cannot speak or make sounds
  • Cannot breathe
  • May turn blue
Action: Encourage coughing Action: Act immediately!

Choking - Conscious Adult/Child (Over 1 year):

HEIMLICH MANEUVER (Abdominal Thrusts) STEP 1: ASSESS SEVERITY ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Ask: "Are you choking?" ‚Ä¢ If cannot speak/breathe ‚Üí Severe obstruction ‚Ä¢ Call 911 or have someone call ‚Üì STEP 2: POSITION YOURSELF ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Stand behind person ‚Ä¢ Wrap arms around waist ‚Ä¢ Person should be standing or sitting ‚Üì STEP 3: HAND POSITION ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Make fist with one hand ‚Ä¢ Place thumb side of fist on abdomen ‚Ä¢ Position: Above navel, below ribcage ‚Ä¢ Grasp fist with other hand ‚Üì STEP 4: DELIVER ABDOMINAL THRUSTS ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Pull inward and upward with quick thrust ‚Ä¢ Use enough force to dislodge object ‚Ä¢ Give up to 5 thrusts ‚Ä¢ Check if object expelled after each thrust ‚Üì STEP 5: ALTERNATE IF NOT EFFECTIVE ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Give 5 back blows (between shoulder blades) ‚Ä¢ Then 5 more abdominal thrusts ‚Ä¢ Continue alternating until: - Object is expelled - Person can breathe/cough/speak - Person becomes unconscious ‚Üì IF PERSON BECOMES UNCONSCIOUS ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Lower person to ground carefully ‚Ä¢ Call 911 if not already called ‚Ä¢ Begin CPR starting with chest compressions ‚Ä¢ Check mouth before giving breaths ‚Ä¢ Remove object if visible and easy to reach

‚ö†Ô∏è Special Considerations:

  • Pregnant/obese: Use chest thrusts instead of abdominal
  • Small child: Kneel behind child, use less force
  • Self-choking: Use fist on your own abdomen or lean over chair/railing

ü©∏ Severe Bleeding Control

‚ö†Ô∏è Life-threatening bleeding can kill in minutes!

Act quickly to control severe bleeding.

BLEEDING CONTROL SEQUENCE STEP 1: ENSURE SAFETY ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Protect yourself - wear gloves if available ‚Ä¢ If no gloves: Use plastic bag, cloth, or have person apply pressure ‚Üì STEP 2: APPLY DIRECT PRESSURE ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Place clean cloth/dressing directly on wound ‚Ä¢ Apply firm, steady pressure ‚Ä¢ Don't lift dressing to check - maintain pressure ‚Ä¢ If blood soaks through: Add more dressings on top ‚Ä¢ Continue pressure for 10-15 minutes ‚Üì STEP 3: CALL FOR HELP ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Call 911 for severe bleeding ‚Ä¢ While waiting, maintain pressure ‚Üì STEP 4: BANDAGE WOUND ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Once bleeding slows, bandage firmly ‚Ä¢ Wrap bandage around limb (if applicable) ‚Ä¢ Check for circulation beyond bandage (pulses, color) ‚Üì STEP 5: TREAT FOR SHOCK ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Lay person down (if no spinal injury) ‚Ä¢ Elevate legs 30 cm (12") if possible ‚Ä¢ Keep person warm ‚Ä¢ Reassure and monitor

Tourniquet Use (Severe limb bleeding only):

Use tourniquet ONLY when:

  • Severe life-threatening limb bleeding
  • Direct pressure does not control bleeding
  • Multiple casualties and you must move to others

How to apply:

  1. Place tourniquet 5-7 cm (2-3") above wound
  2. NEVER place over joint
  3. Tighten until bleeding stops
  4. Note time of application
  5. Do NOT loosen or remove - let hospital do this
  6. Write time on tourniquet or person's forehead

DO NOT:

  • ‚ùå Remove embedded objects - stabilize them in place
  • ‚ùå Use direct pressure on eye injuries
  • ‚ùå Apply tourniquet unless absolutely necessary
  • ‚ùå Remove blood-soaked dressings - add more on top

üò∞ Shock - Circulatory Emergency

Shock occurs when body's organs don't get enough blood/oxygen. Can be life-threatening.

Signs and Symptoms of Shock:

  • Pale, cold, clammy skin
  • Rapid, weak pulse
  • Rapid, shallow breathing
  • Weakness, dizziness, faintness
  • Confusion, anxiety, restlessness
  • Nausea, vomiting
  • Thirst
  • Dilated pupils

Treatment for Shock:

  1. Call 911 - Shock is life-threatening
  2. Treat cause - Control bleeding, etc.
  3. Lay person down - On back if no spinal injury
  4. Elevate legs - 30 cm (12") if no leg injury
  5. Keep warm - Cover with blanket (don't overheat)
  6. Loosen clothing - Around neck, chest, waist
  7. Nothing by mouth - No food or drink
  8. Reassure - Stay calm, speak soothingly
  9. Monitor - Watch breathing and consciousness

DO NOT elevate legs if:

  • Suspected spinal injury
  • Head injury
  • Chest injury or difficulty breathing
  • Suspected broken leg or hip

In these cases: Keep person flat or in position found.

üî• Burns - Thermal, Chemical, Electrical

Severity Classification:

Burn Type Appearance Characteristics
Superficial (1st degree) Red, no blisters Painful, dry, minor (like sunburn)
Partial thickness (2nd degree) Red, blistered Very painful, weeping fluid
Full thickness (3rd degree) White, charred, leathery May be painless (nerve damage)

Treatment for Thermal Burns:

BURN TREATMENT SEQUENCE STEP 1: STOP THE BURNING ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Remove from heat source ‚Ä¢ Stop, Drop, Roll if clothes on fire ‚Ä¢ Remove hot clothing (if not stuck) ‚Ä¢ Remove jewelry before swelling starts ‚Üì STEP 2: COOL THE BURN ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Cool with running water for 10-20 minutes ‚Ä¢ Use cool (not ice cold) water ‚Ä¢ If no water: Cool, wet cloth ‚Ä¢ Don't use ice - causes more damage ‚Üì STEP 3: ASSESS SEVERITY ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Call 911 if: - Large area (bigger than person's hand) - Face, hands, feet, genitals, joints - Full thickness (3rd degree) - Caused by chemicals or electricity - Person is infant, elderly, or has medical conditions ‚Üì STEP 4: COVER THE BURN ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Use clean, dry, non-stick dressing ‚Ä¢ Loosely wrap with gauze ‚Ä¢ DO NOT apply: Butter, oil, ointments, ice ‚Ä¢ DO NOT break blisters ‚Üì STEP 5: TREAT FOR SHOCK ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Lay person down ‚Ä¢ Elevate burned area if possible ‚Ä¢ Keep warm (except burned area) ‚Ä¢ Monitor for shock

Chemical Burns:

Special treatment for chemical exposure:

  1. Remove from source - Get away from chemical
  2. Remove contaminated clothing - Carefully, avoid spreading
  3. Flush with water - 20+ minutes continuous flushing
  4. Brush off dry chemicals first - Before flushing with water
  5. Call 911 - All chemical burns need medical attention
  6. Bring chemical container/MSDS - For hospital

Electrical Burns:

‚ö†Ô∏è DANGER: Do not touch person still in contact with electricity!

  1. Turn off power - At breaker/source if safe
  2. Use non-conductive object - To separate person from source (dry wood, etc.)
  3. Call 911 immediately - ALL electrical burns need hospital
  4. Check for cardiac arrest - May need CPR
  5. Look for entry AND exit wounds - Internal damage may be severe

Never assume electrical burn is minor - internal damage can be life-threatening!

ü¶¥ Fractures & Sprains

Signs of Possible Fracture:

  • Deformity or abnormal position
  • Swelling and bruising
  • Pain, especially with movement
  • Loss of function
  • Grinding or popping sound at time of injury
  • Open wound with bone visible (open fracture)

Treatment (RICE):

R - REST ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Stop activity immediately ‚Ä¢ Don't use injured area ‚Ä¢ Support injured part ‚Üì I - IMMOBILIZE ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Splint in position found ‚Ä¢ Include joints above and below injury ‚Ä¢ Pad splint for comfort ‚Ä¢ Don't try to straighten deformity ‚Üì C - COLD ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Apply ice pack wrapped in cloth ‚Ä¢ 20 minutes on, 20 minutes off ‚Ä¢ Reduces swelling and pain ‚Ä¢ Never apply ice directly to skin ‚Üì E - ELEVATE ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Raise injured area above heart level ‚Ä¢ Reduces swelling ‚Ä¢ Only if no spinal injury suspected

When to Call 911:

  • Suspected spinal injury
  • Suspected skull, neck, back, hip, or pelvis fracture
  • Open fracture (bone visible through skin)
  • Multiple injuries
  • Circulation problems (pale, cold, no pulse beyond injury)
  • Severe pain or person is in shock

‚ö†Ô∏è SPINAL INJURY WARNING:

Suspect spinal injury if:

  • Fall from height
  • Motor vehicle collision
  • Diving accident
  • Head injury
  • Unconscious from trauma

If spinal injury suspected:

  • DO NOT MOVE person unless immediate danger
  • Stabilize head and neck manually
  • Call 911 immediately
  • Keep person still

üíä Common Medical Emergencies

Heart Attack:

Symptoms:

  • Chest discomfort (pressure, squeezing, fullness)
  • Pain spreading to arms, neck, jaw, back
  • Shortness of breath
  • Cold sweat, nausea, lightheadedness

Action:

  1. Call 911 immediately - Don't wait!
  2. Have person rest - Stop all activity
  3. Loosen tight clothing
  4. ASA (Aspirin): Give 1 regular (325 mg) or 2 low-dose (81 mg) if:
    • Person is conscious
    • Can swallow
    • Not allergic to ASA
    • No bleeding disorder
  5. Monitor - Be ready to do CPR

Stroke (Brain Attack):

Use FAST to recognize stroke:

Face Is face drooping on one side? Ask person to smile.
Arms Can person raise both arms? Does one drift down?
Speech Is speech slurred? Can they repeat simple sentence?
Time TIME TO CALL 911! Note time symptoms started.

Action: Call 911 immediately. Note time symptoms began. Keep person calm and comfortable. DO NOT give food, drink, or medication.

Severe Allergic Reaction (Anaphylaxis):

Symptoms (can develop rapidly):

  • Difficulty breathing, wheezing
  • Swelling of face, lips, tongue, throat
  • Hives, skin rash, itching
  • Rapid pulse, drop in blood pressure
  • Dizziness, loss of consciousness

Action:

  1. Call 911 immediately
  2. EpiPen: If person has one, help them use it
    • Remove from carrier tube
    • Hold firmly against outer thigh
    • Push down until you hear click
    • Hold for 3 seconds
    • Massage injection site for 10 seconds
  3. Position: Lay person flat (unless having breathing trouble)
  4. Monitor: Be ready to do CPR
  5. Second dose: May give second EpiPen after 5 minutes if no improvement

Seizures:

During seizure:

  • Protect: Move objects away, cushion head
  • Time: Note duration of seizure
  • Turn: Gently roll to side when convulsions stop
  • DO NOT: Put anything in mouth, restrain person

Call 911 if:

  • First time seizure
  • Lasts more than 5 minutes
  • Repeated seizures
  • Person injured during seizure
  • Person doesn't regain consciousness
  • Pregnant, diabetic, or infant

üò¥ Recovery Position

Use for unconscious person who is BREATHING normally:

RECOVERY POSITION STEPS STEP 1: KNEEL BESIDE PERSON ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Position yourself at their side ‚Ä¢ Ensure arms are free from body ‚Üì STEP 2: POSITION ARM ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Place arm nearest you at right angle to body ‚Ä¢ Elbow bent, palm up ‚Üì STEP 3: POSITION FAR ARM ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Bring far arm across chest ‚Ä¢ Hold back of hand against person's near cheek ‚Üì STEP 4: BEND FAR LEG ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Reach across and bend far leg at knee ‚Ä¢ Keep foot flat on ground ‚Üì STEP 5: ROLL PERSON ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Pull on far knee ‚Ä¢ Roll person toward you onto their side ‚Ä¢ Support head while rolling ‚Üì STEP 6: ADJUST POSITION ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Tilt head back to keep airway open ‚Ä¢ Adjust hand under cheek if needed ‚Ä¢ Bend top leg at right angle for stability ‚Üì STEP 7: MONITOR ‚Üì ‚Ä¢ Continue to monitor breathing ‚Ä¢ Call 911 if not already called ‚Ä¢ Keep warm with blanket

‚ö†Ô∏è DO NOT use recovery position if:

  • Suspected spinal injury
  • Not breathing (do CPR instead)
  • Injuries that would be aggravated by moving

üß∞ First Aid Kit Contents

Workplace/Drilling Site First Aid Kit (CSA Type 3):

Item Quantity Purpose
First Aid Manual 1 Reference guide
Adhesive bandages (various sizes) 40 Minor cuts/scrapes
Sterile gauze pads (10x10 cm) 20 Cover wounds
Sterile gauze pads (5x5 cm) 20 Smaller wounds
Roller bandages (5 cm) 6 Secure dressings
Roller bandages (7.5 cm) 6 Larger areas
Triangular bandages 4 Slings, bandaging
Adhesive tape (2.5 cm) 2 rolls Secure dressings
Safety pins 12 Secure bandages
Scissors 1 Cut bandages/clothing
Tweezers 1 Remove splinters
Disposable gloves (pairs) 4 Infection control
Face shield/pocket mask 1 CPR barrier
Instant cold pack 2 Reduce swelling
Emergency blanket 1 Prevent shock/hypothermia
Antiseptic wipes 10 Clean wounds
Eyewash 1 bottle Eye injuries
Burn gel/dressing 2 Burn treatment

Additional Items for Remote Sites:

  • Splinting materials (SAM splint)
  • More trauma dressings
  • Tourniquet
  • Hemostatic gauze
  • Additional emergency blankets
  • Satellite phone/communication device
  • GPS coordinates of site written down
  • Evacuation plan posted

üìù First Aid Kit Maintenance:

  • Check monthly for expired items
  • Replace used items immediately
  • Keep kit in accessible, marked location
  • Ensure all crew know where kit is located
  • Record all first aid incidents

üìú First Aid Certification

Standard First Aid & CPR Level C:

Course includes:

  • CPR for adults, children, and infants
  • AED usage
  • Choking management (all ages)
  • Severe bleeding control
  • Shock management
  • Wound care
  • Burns
  • Fractures and sprains
  • Medical emergencies (heart attack, stroke, etc.)
  • Environmental emergencies

Course Details:

Duration: 2 days (14-16 hours)
Format: In-person, hands-on training
Certification: Valid for 3 years
Cost: $120-$180 (varies by provider/location)
Providers: St. John Ambulance, Red Cross, others

Workplace Requirements:

Many workplaces require:

  • At least one certified first aider per shift
  • More first aiders for remote locations
  • Higher level certification (Emergency First Aid) for remote sites
  • Annual recertification or refresher training

Check your provincial/territorial regulations for specific requirements.

‚úì Remember: The Best First Aid is Prevention

  • Follow safety procedures
  • Use proper PPE
  • Stay alert and aware
  • Report hazards
  • Get proper training
  • Know emergency procedures

But when accidents happen - act quickly, stay calm, and provide care to the best of your ability.

ü§° Prevention & Maintenance

Daily Pre-Start Checklist (15 minutes)

Before Starting Equipment:

  • Walk-around inspection complete
  • Check all fluid levels (engine oil, hydraulic, coolant, fuel)
  • Check for leaks (hydraulic, fuel, water)
  • Check hoses for damage or wear
  • Check bolts and connections for tightness
  • Inspect drill string and BHA
  • Test safety equipment and alarms
  • Check fire extinguishers accessible
  • Verify first aid kit stocked
  • Test emergency shutdown
  • Check weather forecast
  • Brief crew on day's work plan

Starting Equipment:

  1. Remove all guards and covers properly reinstalled
  2. Clear all personnel from equipment
  3. Sound horn/alarm before starting
  4. Start engine and let warm up (5-10 minutes)
  5. Check all gauges (pressure, temperature, RPM)
  6. Test all controls at low speed
  7. Listen for unusual sounds
  8. Check for vibration or movement

During Operations Monitoring

Continuous Monitoring:

  • Watch all gauges constantly
  • Monitor penetration rate
  • Check return flow quality
  • Listen for unusual sounds
  • Feel for unusual vibrations
  • Track torque levels
  • Monitor pump pressure
  • Watch for leaks developing
  • Check weather conditions
  • Monitor crew fatigue

Hourly Checks:

  • Quick walk-around inspection
  • Check for new leaks
  • Check fluid levels if accessible
  • Listen to engine sound
  • Check temperature gauges

Every 3 Meters Drilled:

  • Record depth
  • Record penetration rate
  • Note formation changes
  • Record any drilling problems
  • Log bit performance
  • Update drilling parameters

End of Shift Procedures

Shutdown Sequence:

  1. Pull bit off bottom if leaving overnight
  2. Circulate hole clean (15-30 minutes)
  3. Reduce engine to idle for 5 minutes
  4. Shut down in proper sequence
  5. Secure all controls
  6. Close all valves
  7. Lock out if required

Clean Up & Inspection:

  • Clean up all spills immediately
  • Store tools properly
  • Secure loose materials
  • Check for damage from day's work
  • Note any maintenance needed
  • Refill consumables (fuel, fluids)
  • Cover sensitive equipment

Documentation:

  • Update drilling log
  • Record meters drilled
  • Document problems encountered
  • Note solutions that worked
  • Log maintenance performed
  • Record bit hours/meters
  • Update equipment hours
  • Brief next shift crew

Scheduled Maintenance

Daily Maintenance:

  • Grease all fittings
  • Check and tighten bolts
  • Clean air filters (dusty conditions)
  • Drain water from fuel filters (diesel)
  • Check tire pressure
  • Clean equipment

Weekly Maintenance:

  • Change engine oil if dusty/heavy use
  • Check hydraulic filter condition
  • Inspect all hoses thoroughly
  • Check belt tension and condition
  • Clean radiator and coolers
  • Inspect drill string thoroughly
  • Check all safety equipment
  • Test emergency systems

Monthly Maintenance:

  • Change engine oil and filter (normal use)
  • Change hydraulic filter
  • Lubricate all pivot points
  • Inspect electrical connections
  • Check battery condition
  • Inspect structural components
  • Service air compressor if equipped
  • Update maintenance records

Seasonal Maintenance:

  • Change hydraulic oil
  • Flush cooling system
  • Service transmission/gearbox
  • Comprehensive inspection by mechanic
  • Replace worn components
  • Update equipment manuals

Record Keeping

Drilling Logs Should Include:

  • Date, time, shift
  • Crew names
  • Start/end depth
  • Meters drilled
  • Penetration rate by interval
  • Formation descriptions
  • Drilling parameters (WOB, RPM, pressure)
  • Bit type and performance
  • Problems encountered
  • Solutions implemented
  • Maintenance performed
  • Weather conditions

Why Good Records Matter:

  • Track equipment performance
  • Predict maintenance needs
  • Learn from past problems
  • Train new operators
  • Justify equipment upgrades
  • Legal/safety documentation
  • Optimize drilling parameters
  • Budget planning

Troubleshooting Mindset

The 5-Step Systematic Approach:

  1. Observe First
    • What EXACTLY is happening?
    • When did it start?
    • What changed before it started?
    • Is it getting worse?
  2. Think Systematically
    • What systems are involved?
    • What could cause this symptom?
    • What's most likely?
    • What's most dangerous?
  3. Start Simple
    • Check obvious things first
    • Low fluid? Loose connection?
    • Switch off? Filter plugged?
    • Don't assume complex problem
  4. Change One Thing
    • Only change ONE variable at a time
    • So you know what fixed it
    • If it doesn't work, change it back
    • Try something else
  5. Document Everything
    • Write down what you tried
    • Record what worked
    • Note for next time
    • Share knowledge with crew

When to Ask for Help

There's NO shame in asking for help!

Experienced drillers ask questions all the time. It's how you learn and stay safe.

Ask for Help When:

  • Safety is at risk
  • Beyond your training/experience
  • Expensive equipment at stake
  • Problem is worsening despite your efforts
  • You've tried obvious solutions
  • Time pressure is making you rush
  • You're not confident in next step
  • You're not sure what to do

Who to Contact:

  • Immediate supervisor
  • Senior driller
  • Equipment manufacturer support
  • Company technical support
  • Experienced crew members

Remember:

It's better to take 2 hours to solve a problem correctly than to make it worse in 5 minutes of hasty action.

Good troubleshooting is patient, methodical, and documented.

üçå Steel Wedges & Hole Orientation

Comprehensive Guide to Directional Drilling

Steel wedges are precision tools used to deliberately deviate a borehole from its current trajectory to bypass obstructions, intersect ore zones, or correct unwanted hole deviation. This section covers wedge types, setting methods (Mechanical & Cemented), orientation control, reaming procedures, and detailed cost analysis for directional drilling operations.

üìä Quick Reference: Methods & Costs

Setting Method Key Procedure Steps Estimated Cost Range
Mechanical (Shear Pin) Run wedge assembly with orientation key ‚Üí Apply back-off torque ‚Üí Shear pin with drive shoe ‚Üí Ream with bullnose ‚Üí Standard bit $4,500 - $10,500 USD
Cemented (Resin/Grout) Pump fast-setting resin/cement plug ‚Üí Seat wedge into soft cement ‚Üí Allow cure time (4-12 hrs) ‚Üí Ream carefully with bullnose $6,000 - $15,000 USD
Retrievable Wedge Expandable wedge with mechanical anchor ‚Üí Test deflection ‚Üí Retrieve if unsuccessful ‚Üí Reset if needed $8,000 - $18,000 USD

üî© Wedge Components & Classifications

A complete wedge assembly consists of multiple precision-machined components that work together to create a controlled deviation in the borehole path. Each component must function perfectly or the entire operation fails.

Component Function & Critical Details
Wedge Body Hardened steel inclined plane (typically 30-45¬∞ angle) that deflects the bit off the original hole trajectory. The surface must be machined to mirror-finish smoothness to prevent bit jamming. Made from heat-treated alloy steel rated to 60+ Rockwell hardness.
Orientation Key The critical component that prevents rotation during setting. Fits into a precisely milled keyseat in the casing or a natural rock formation notch. Must be aligned to within ¬±2¬∞ of target azimuth before setting. Typically made from hardened tool steel with anti-rotation teeth.
Shear Pin (Mechanical) Precision-engineered pin designed to break at exact torque threshold (typically 500-800 ft-lbs). When sheared, it releases the wedge allowing it to be pushed/driven into final position. Pin strength must be carefully matched to drill string torque capacity.
Drive Shoe Heavy-duty downhole assembly that applies impact force to seat the wedge after shear pin breaks. Works like a pneumatic hammer to drive wedge firmly against hole wall. Must withstand 10,000+ pounds of impact force without failure.
Guide Tube/Stabilizer Ensures wedge assembly runs true down the hole without spinning or deviating. Prevents premature orientation key engagement. Critical for holes with significant deviation or rough walls.

‚öôÔ∏è Wedge Types & Applications

üî∏ Fixed Angle Wedges (30¬∞, 35¬∞, 40¬∞, 45¬∞)

Applications: Standard directional drilling where deflection angle is known in advance. Most common in mineral exploration and production drilling.

Advantages: Simple, reliable, proven technology with 50+ years of field use. Lower cost ($1,500-$3,500 per wedge). Easy to set with standard equipment.

Limitations: Cannot adjust angle once manufactured. Must order correct angle for ground conditions. Typically need inventory of 3-4 different angles.

üî∏ Adjustable Wedges (20-50¬∞ range)

Applications: Exploration drilling where ground conditions are unknown, or when multiple deflection angles needed on same project.

Advantages: Single wedge serves multiple purposes, reducing inventory costs. Can adjust angle based on real-time survey results.

Limitations: More complex mechanism = higher failure risk. Typically 30-50% more expensive ($4,000-$7,000). Requires specialized setting tools.

üî∏ Retrievable/Retractable Wedges

Applications: High-cost holes where wedge failure would be catastrophic, testing uncertain ground conditions, or when multiple attempts may be needed.

Advantages: Can be pulled back if deflection is insufficient or oriented incorrectly. Reusable (5-10 uses typical). Reduces risk of losing hole.

Limitations: Significantly more expensive ($8,000-$15,000). Requires specialized retrieval tools. More complex setting procedure. Not suitable for all ground conditions.

‚úÖ Best Practices for Wedging Success

  • Survey First, Always: Shoot accurate down-hole azimuth and inclination before wedging. Calculate exact orientation required using proper surveying software or verified trigonometric calculations. Verify all calculations twice with different methods. A 2¬∞ orientation error at 500 feet depth = 17 feet lateral miss.
  • Never Compromise on Quality: Inferior wedges cause expensive catastrophic failures. Always purchase from reputable manufacturers (Boart Longyear, Atlas Copco, Sandvik) with documented quality control. Inspect wedge face with magnification for any surface defects. Reject any wedge with scratches, pits, or uneven machining.
  • Bullnose Step is Absolutely Mandatory: This cannot be skipped, substituted, or "done quick." Attempting to ream directly with face diamonds will fail 90% of the time and likely destroy the wedge. Budget 2-4 hours for proper bullnose reaming. Use worn/used bullnose bits for cost savings - they don't need to be new.
  • Exercise Extreme Patience During Reaming: Complete reaming sequence takes 4-8 hours minimum in normal conditions, up to 12-16 hours in difficult ground. Rushing causes bit breakage, wedge failure, crooked holes, or complete loss of deviation. Budget appropriate time and don't let schedule pressure force shortcuts.
  • Document Everything Meticulously: Record all orientation measurements, torque readings, weights, depths, times, and drilling parameters in real-time. Take photos of wedge assembly before installation. Essential for troubleshooting failures, insurance claims, and planning future wedging operations. Use standardized data sheets.
  • Always Have Contingency Equipment: Stock backup wedges (at least 1 spare of each type needed), extra cement/resin, complete fishing tool kit, and overshot tools. Wedge failures in remote locations with no backup can cost $50,000+ in mobilization, delays, and lost hole. Insurance is cheap compared to failure costs.

‚ö° Critical Safety Reminder

Wedging operations involve heavy steel components under high stress, rotating machinery, and pressurized systems. Always follow lockout/tagout procedures during assembly. Wear appropriate PPE including hard hat, safety glasses, and steel-toed boots. Never stand directly under suspended loads. Ensure all personnel are clear of the "red zone" during drive shoe operations. One moment of carelessness can result in serious injury or death.
